**Implication:** When AI pushback intensifies despite stronger evidence, stop adding evidence. Switch to a values conversation. Ask what the person is trying to protect or preserve, and start there.

**AI adoption blockers are trending in the wrong direction despite widespread training efforts.** The share of people who don't know how to apply AI grew from 75% in Q4 2024 to 83% in January 2026. More exposure to AI content is not closing the gap — it may be widening it. ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Despite 18 months of tutorials, courses, podcasts, and corporate AI initiatives—we're going backward. (And yes, that trend line surprised me too.)"*

**Implication:** Conventional AI education strategies — courses, podcasts, slide decks — are not solving the core problem. Organizations need to fundamentally rethink how they build AI capability, moving away from content consumption toward experiential learning.

**Surfacing and celebrating what AI power users are already doing is a high-leverage leadership action.** John Devine at Meta posts a one-minute weekly video to team Slack showcasing a creative AI use — a consistent signal that experimentation is noticed and valued. People pay attention to what leaders celebrate. ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"John Devine, a global head at Meta—posts a 1-minute video to his team Slack every single week celebrating something cool someone is doing with AI. People pay attention to what leaders celebrate. That weekly drumbeat signals: this matters, I notice, keep going."*

**Implication:** Organizations should create systematic structures — weekly showcases, internal newsletters, Slack channels — to surface and spread AI practices from hidden power users. Celebration is a discovery mechanism as much as a motivation tool.

**Organizations are cutting headcount while simultaneously admitting AI has not yet made them more efficient.** Shrinking without transformation is not a strategy — it is managed decline. The dangerous combination is believing AI will matter while failing to capture any of its gains before making workforce cuts. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"If you're cutting headcount but can't point to AI-driven efficiency gains, you're not leveraging AI—you're just shrinking. And shrinking without transformation isn't a strategy. It's managed decline."*

**Implication:** Headcount reductions should follow demonstrated efficiency gains from AI, not precede them. Leaders who cut first and transform never are not managing AI risk — they are compounding it.

**AI saves employees an average of 7.5 hours per week, according to LSE research.** The critical and largely unasked question is what people are doing with that reclaimed time — pursuing a backlog of impossible ideas or simply doing the same work with fewer people. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"That's remarkable. But who's asking: What are people doing with those 7.5 hours? Are they pursuing ideas they never had capacity for? Or are they doing the same work with fewer people?"*

**Implication:** Time savings from AI are only strategically valuable if redirected toward idea creation and transformation. Organizations that reclaim hours but don't reinvest them into new possibilities are trading a gift for a marginal cost reduction.

**Measuring AI adoption by login counts or usage volume is a false metric.** The right question is which workflows have been fundamentally transformed — because more use is not the same as better use. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Stop tracking 'number of AI logins' and start asking: 'Which workflows have been fundamentally transformed?' More use isn't better use. Better use is better use."*

**Implication:** Organizations should replace shallow AI activity metrics with transformation metrics — tracking meaningful workflow changes, quality improvements, and new capabilities unlocked rather than raw engagement numbers.

**When AI goals are achievable without AI, there is no forcing function for adoption — people default to familiar methods because the old way 'works well enough.' A board member in Utley's session articulated this trap: if everyone has enough time and resources to do things manually, there is no urgency to automate. Organizations only truly adopt AI when their ambitions outpace what humans alone can accomplish.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"If you don't give limited resources, and everybody can have anything without automating anything all the time, the force against implementation is too big. You have enough time and assistance to do everything manually anyway, so what's the rush?"*

**Implication:** Leaders should deliberately set goals that are impossible to achieve without AI augmentation. If targets are reachable by 'mere humans,' the goals are too small and will produce no real transformation.

**Utley identifies 'AI theater' as a specific governance risk.** boards without personal AI experience will accept performative AI activity — impressive-sounding committee work, consultants, roadmaps — without being able to distinguish it from real capability and progress. The ability to tell the difference requires having done the work yourself. ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"If you haven't done the work yourself, you can't tell the difference. You'll accept AI theater. You'll approve conservative timelines. You'll rubber-stamp strategies built for yesterday's constraints."*

**Implication:** Leaders must develop enough personal AI fluency to recognize when they are being given a performance rather than a real answer. AI theater is a governance failure enabled by inexperience — and the antidote is personal practice, not more reporting.

**The central irony Utley identifies is that the very thing people fear — looking stupid while learning — is the only path to the thing they want, which is confidence. Avoiding the awkward learning stage guarantees permanent insecurity, while going through it guarantees fluency.** ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The irony is that the thing everyone's afraid of—looking stupid while learning—is the only path to the thing everyone wants: confidence."*

**Implication:** Organizations that create cultures where visible AI experimentation and failure are acceptable will develop fluent teams faster than those that only reward polished AI outputs.

**Challamel's analogy — 'using AI is like showering, if you don't do it every day it's a problem' — reframes AI engagement as basic professional hygiene rather than advanced capability. It shifts the conversation from 'how much' to 'how consistently.'** ([source](It's a Skill, Not a Pill — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Using AI is like showering. If you don't do it every day, it's a problem — and at some point, there are diminishing returns."*

**Implication:** Leaders building AI culture should communicate a frequency expectation, not just a capability expectation. The standard isn't 'become an expert' — it's 'engage every day,' which is both more achievable and more impactful.

**Innovation has become over-hyped as a topic while remaining an under-nourished capability in practice.** This gap exists not just at the individual level but also at the team and organizational levels. Having taught innovation for 14+ years at Stanford, Utley sees this pattern consistently across industries and geographies. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders))

> *"Having taught the subject of innovation for the last 14+ years at Stanford, he has seen the topic become over-hyped, yet remain an under-nourished capability. This is true not only on the personal level, but also on the team and organizational levels."*

**Implication:** Organizations that talk about innovation loudly but don't build the underlying daily practices and metrics to support it are engaged in performance, not transformation. The focus should shift from innovation theater to measurable creative capability-building.

**The most powerful AI use cases are often discovered by users themselves rather than prescribed by experts.** Students and practitioners regularly arrive at applications that even experienced AI educators have never imagined. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"My students are regularly coming to me with use cases I've never imagined that landed them in a destination I could have never predicted, and they could never have predicted."*

**Implication:** Organizations should create space for employee-driven AI experimentation rather than only top-down prescribed use cases — the frontier of AI application is being mapped from the ground up.

**The contrast between Bryan McCann's stressful Fridays and most people's relaxing weekends is Utley's sharpest illustration of the gap between those who have internalized AI's potential and those who haven't. Both groups have equal access to the same technology — the difference is entirely cognitive and habitual, not structural.** ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Bryan's Fridays are stressful. Most people's weekends are relaxing. That's the difference between someone who has grasped what's available and someone who hasn't."*

**Implication:** Organizations trying to build AI culture should study the attitudinal and habitual differences between high-deployers and non-deployers — the gap is not in tools or training, but in whether people have internalized the identity of someone who has a team working for them around the clock.

**Narrating impact — sharing solutions broadly and letting others copy them — multiplies the value of individual initiative. Jeremy cites Adam at the National Park Service, whose 45-minute AI tool now saves 20 years of labor annually because he shared it with others in need. Visibility and generosity of sharing are force multipliers for initiative.** ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Narrate — Broadcast impact. Show before/after. Let others copy. Remember Adam at the National Park Service? His 45-minute tool now saves 20 years of labor annually because he shared it with others in need!"*

**Implication:** Building a solution in private captures only a fraction of its potential value. Deliberately broadcasting what you built — and making it easy for others to adopt — transforms individual initiative into organizational-scale impact.

**Organizations face creativity obstacles at three distinct levels.** organizational (incentives, compensation, culture), team (interpersonal dynamics), and individual (cognitive biases). The cognitive level is simultaneously the one we are most susceptible to and the one we are most capable of addressing ourselves. ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"There's organizational stuff at play, there's team stuff at play — organizationally there's incentives, compensation, culture. In the team there's interpersonal dynamics. And then cognitively we have barriers as well. The one obviously that we're most susceptible to but then also most capable of overcoming is our own cognitive barriers."*

**Implication:** Leaders addressing innovation obstacles should target all three levels — but individuals can begin immediately by recognizing and working around their own cognitive biases, without waiting for organizational change.

**Many employees don't use AI not because they lack access, but because they haven't received explicit permission from their leaders. When a leader actively asks 'Have you tried AI?', it signals that using AI is not just acceptable but expected. This cultural signal is often the missing ingredient for broader AI adoption within teams.** ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Even if they have thought of it, there's often a question: 'Is it irresponsible for me to use AI for this?' 'Does my boss want me to?' When you ask the question—when you explicitly suggest they try AI—you're giving them permission. You're signaling: Yes, this is not only okay, it's expected."*

**Implication:** Organizational AI adoption often stalls not from lack of tools but lack of cultural permission. Leaders can unlock adoption simply by explicitly endorsing AI use in everyday conversations.

**Leaders who redirect team members to AI create a flywheel of success stories they can then amplify publicly.** By following up with 'How did AI help?' and celebrating those stories, leaders normalize AI use and multiply its impact across the organization. This approach turns individual experiments into collective learning. ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Here's the move: When your teammate comes back with their work product, ask a follow-up question: 'How did AI help?' Then—and this is important—get them to share with others how AI helped them. Even better, brag on them publicly, yourself. This is how you create a flywheel."*

**Implication:** AI success stories are underutilized leadership tools. Systematically collecting and sharing them accelerates adoption, builds psychological safety, and creates a culture where AI use is the norm.

**The ultimate organizational goal of consistently asking 'Have you tried AI?' is a culture where the default instinct is 'ask AI first' rather than 'ask the boss.' This shift reduces managerial load, speeds problem-solving, and builds a more resilient, self-sufficient organization. It requires consistent practice over weeks, not a single conversation.** ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"You're giving your organization the most important gift of all: a culture where the default instinct isn't 'ask the boss' but 'ask AI first.'"*

**Implication:** Culture change around AI is a long game that requires leaders to model and reinforce the behavior repeatedly. A 2-3 week sustained practice is suggested as the minimum to establish the new pattern.

**Onboarding AI the same way you would onboard a new human teammate — with examples, style guides, feedback on drafts, and context about stakeholder preferences — dramatically improves its output quality. Most people skip this step and then blame AI for underperforming.** ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"If you had a new teammate who could write your all-staff memos, how would you onboard them? You wouldn't just say 'write me a memo' and expect perfection, right? You'd show them examples. Give them your company's style guide. Provide feedback on drafts. Have you onboarded AI like that?"*

**Implication:** Organizations should develop structured AI onboarding protocols — examples, brand guidelines, past work, stakeholder context — treating AI setup as a talent onboarding investment rather than a one-click tool deployment.

**AI fluency is a learnable skill, not a cognitive shortcut or personality trait.** Utley cites a 40% quality boost available to anyone who develops the skill, yet fewer than 3% of the workforce has taken it up — and that number is declining. He made this observation while teaching at Wharton, suggesting the gap is motivational and cultural, not technical. ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker))

> *"A 40% quality boost, available to anyone. Taken up by fewer than 3% of the workforce (and that number is going down, not up)."*

**Implication:** The AI skills gap is not primarily a training-content problem — if it were, adoption would be rising. Something else is suppressing uptake. Leaders need to diagnose and address the cultural and psychological barriers, not just provide more resources.

**Standard AI dashboards measure visible activity — prompt experiments, tool logins — but miss the most meaningful transformations. Utley highlights the example of a finance manager who quietly eliminated three recurring reports, creating real organizational change that never registers on usage metrics. What gets measured shapes what gets valued.** ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker))

> *"The engineer who experiments with 40 prompts shows up on the dashboard. The finance manager who quietly kills three recurring reports doesn't — even though she's the one who actually changed."*

**Implication:** Organizations need to rethink how they measure AI impact. Activity metrics reward visible experimentation but ignore the quieter, higher-leverage work of redesigning workflows and eliminating unnecessary processes. Better measurement frameworks are needed.

**Organizations and teams that measure AI progress by frequency — whether people have opened it, are logging in, staying current — are tracking the wrong indicator and will remain blind to whether AI is generating real value.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Stop asking, 'Am I using AI enough? Is my team using AI enough?' It's the wrong question. It keeps you focused on frequency and on checking a box, logging in, staying current."*

**Implication:** Leaders assessing AI transformation in their organizations should replace frequency metrics with outcome metrics tied to the quality and strategic importance of the work AI is being applied to.

**The future of ideation will feature AI as an equal partner sitting at the creative table alongside human teams.** Utley envisions AI becoming a trusted creative colleague — one that always has new ideas, pushes thinking, and helps teams see blind spots. This represents a fundamental shift in how creative teams are composed and how ideas are generated. ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

> *"I cannot imagine a world where AI is not an equal partner in the ideation process. It's going to sit at the table as a member of creative teams. Teams will come to see it as a creative partner, a trusted colleague who always has new ideas, who pushes your thinking, who helps you see blind spots."*

**Implication:** Leaders should begin designing team structures and creative processes now with AI as a standing participant — not an occasional tool. This includes defining how AI inputs are weighted, reviewed, and integrated into decision-making.

**Every problem, at its core, is an ideas problem.** Whether in Fortune 50 companies, mid-market firms, or startups, teams succeed to the degree that ideas flow freely. Constraining or blocking ideaflow is therefore a direct constraint on organizational performance. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck))

> *"Every problem is an ideas problem. Having worked in Fortune 50 companies, mid-market companies, and startups, I have seen that teams succeed to the degree that there is a free flow of ideas."*

**Implication:** Leaders should diagnose organizational problems not just as resource or execution failures, but as idea-flow failures. Removing barriers to free ideation is a high-leverage intervention for any struggling team.

**The d.school was explicitly structured to tear down university silos.** Rather than granting degrees or admitting its own students, it hosted cross-departmental classes where disciplines collided. This structural choice — no territorial degree program — was what made genuine interdisciplinary collaboration possible. ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

> *"especially when I joined, we didn't grant degrees, we didn't admit students. And so what we did is, we would host kind of cross departmental collaborative opportunities."*

**Implication:** Institutions and organizations that want to foster innovation should consider whether their structural incentives (degrees, departments, job titles) are creating silos that prevent the cross-pollination they claim to want. Sometimes the most innovative move is to not own the output.

**Non-technical professionals can create high-impact AI tools with minimal training.** A backcountry park ranger with 45 minutes of instruction built a tool that the National Park Service estimates will save 7,000 days of human labor in a single year. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"In 45 minutes, he built a tool with natural language that saves him two days of work. The National Park Service is estimating that the tool that Adam built in 45 minutes is going to save the service 7000 days of human labor this year."*

**Implication:** The ROI of basic AI training for non-technical employees can be extraordinary — organizations that withhold foundational AI education from frontline workers are leaving massive value on the table.

**Many organizations understand they need AI transformation but lack the foundational language and concepts to even begin.** Before organizations can transform with AI, individuals within them need to learn how to collaborate with AI at a basic level. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"While lots of organizations are asking me how can we work with AI to transform our business? Where I have to start with them is how do you work with AI?"*

**Implication:** Organizational AI strategy frequently outpaces individual AI literacy — closing this gap at the individual level is the prerequisite, not an afterthought, for any meaningful organizational AI transformation.

**Knowledge workers who define their work solely as 'sitting in front of a screen' are actively hindering their creative performance. For creative and intellectual work, physical movement, environmental change, and non-screen time are legitimate and necessary components of productive work — not distractions from it.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"There's an element of we have to give ourselves permission to work more broadly than sitting in front of the screen. We mostly define our work in terms of sitting in front of the screen, and yet for those of us who are knowledge workers, that's not — sometimes the screen actually hinders our ability to work well."*

**Implication:** Organizations should redesign what 'at work' looks like for knowledge workers to legitimize non-screen time, movement, and environmental exploration as recognized contributors to creative output.

---

## Innovation Process & Problem Framing

**The imagination ceiling that limits most teams is not a talent ceiling — it is a framing ceiling.** When the problem statement is too narrow, too solution-shaped, or too laden with embedded assumptions, it doesn't matter how creative your team is: the framing has already predetermined the answer space. Expanding the imagination means expanding the question first. ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Audit your standing problem statements and innovation briefs. Ask: does this framing allow for breakthrough solutions, or does it only permit incremental ones? Rewrite briefs that contain embedded solutions as genuine open questions.

**Sitting with a problem — giving it time before rushing to solutions — is not procrastination; it is incubation, a documented stage in the creative process. The pressure to generate solutions immediately is antithetical to breakthrough innovation. The most important ideas often emerge only after the problem has been held long enough for the subconscious to engage with it.** ([source](The Habit That Built Every Genius (It Looks Like Doing Nothing) — Jeremy Utley YouTube channel))

**Implication:** Build incubation periods into project timelines as formally as you build in brainstorming sessions. Schedule problem-framing discussions days before solution-generation sessions, not minutes before. Give the question time to work on people.

**Utley argues that the discomfort of being stuck is a diagnostic signal, not a problem to suppress.** When a team feels blocked on a problem, that sensation is information: it signals that the current framing has exhausted its productive space and a reframe is required. Leaning into the discomfort — asking 'what would we have to believe for this problem to look completely different?' — is the correct response, not pushing harder on the existing frame. ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters Blog))

**Implication:** Train your teams to treat stuckness as a reframe trigger rather than a productivity problem to be overcome by more effort. When a group reports being stuck, the first intervention should always be a problem reframe exercise, not a pep talk or a deadline extension.

**Utley uses the 'big rocks before sand' metaphor to argue that the sequence of problem-solving activities matters as much as the activities themselves. If you fill a jar with sand first, the rocks don't fit. The 'big rocks' in innovation are the high-leverage, uncomfortable upstream activities — deep empathy, genuine problem reframing, divergent ideation — that get crowded out when teams default to the comfortable sand of execution and refinement.** ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters Blog))

**Implication:** Sequence your innovation sprints so that the highest-leverage, most uncomfortable activities — problem reframing, empathy research, divergent ideation — happen first, while attention and energy are at their peak. Never let execution logistics eat the space that should belong to framing.

**Utley uses AI as both a subject and a method for improving problem framing.** He teaches practitioners to give AI the problem statement and ask it to generate five alternative framings of the same problem — not to outsource the thinking, but to stress-test their own framing assumptions. The AI's alternative framings function as a mirror that makes invisible assumptions visible. ([source](Don't Use AI. Work With It. — Methods of the Masters Blog))

**Implication:** Before finalizing any problem statement for an innovation sprint, run it through an AI model with the prompt: 'Generate five alternative ways to frame this problem that would lead to fundamentally different solution spaces.' Use the output as a reframing stimulus, not a substitute for human judgment.

**Reframing a problem from multiple angles before generating solutions is not a soft creative exercise — it is the highest-leverage discipline in the innovation toolkit. A 'How Might We?' prompt, carefully designed, changes what ideas are even possible to generate. The container shapes the contents.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Treat prompt and question design as a distinct, time-boxed phase in every innovation process. Run at least three different framings of any problem before generating solutions — each framing will surface a different solution space.

**Expertise is a double-edged sword in innovation.** it grants depth but narrows the frame. The more expert you become in a domain, the more your problem-framing is constrained to that domain's existing vocabulary and assumptions. Cross-disciplinary exposure is not a nice-to-have — it is corrective medicine for the narrowing effect of expertise. ([source](Methods of the Masters — Jeremy Utley blog essays))

**Implication:** Deliberately include non-experts in early problem-framing sessions. Their naive questions expose assumptions that experts have stopped questioning. The outsider's framing is often the breakthrough framing.

**The framing of a problem determines not just which solutions are possible but which solutions are visible to the people in the room. A poorly framed problem does not merely limit options — it renders entire categories of breakthrough solutions literally unimaginable to the team. This is why framing investment is not optional for organizations that want non-incremental outcomes.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer podcast))

**Implication:** Treat problem framing as a capability to develop and measure, not as an informal preamble to the 'real work' of ideation. Track how many framings your team generates per problem, and treat framing diversity as a leading indicator of solution quality.

**The 'How Might We' prompt is not a linguistic nicety — it is a precision instrument for problem framing.** The phrase 'how' assumes a solution exists; 'might' removes judgment from early possibilities; 'we' makes it a collective challenge. Crafting the right HMW statement before generating any ideas is the disciplined act that separates design thinkers from wishful thinkers. ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Treat your HMW statement as a hypothesis to be tested, not a sentence to be perfected. Write five to ten versions of it before picking one — the act of rewriting the question is itself the innovation work.

**Utley draws a sharp distinction between 'bonehead' and 'brilliant' innovation.** the bonehead move is to jump immediately to solutions; the brilliant move is to realize that the presented problem is almost never the real problem. The real problem is usually one or two 'why' questions upstream from the symptom that triggered the meeting. ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer Podcast))

> *"The problem as stated is rarely the problem worth solving."*

**Implication:** When a team brings you a problem, make it a discipline to ask 'why is this a problem?' and 'what would solving this make possible?' at least twice before allowing solution generation to begin.

**Generation and evaluation are cognitively incompatible modes that cannot operate simultaneously without evaluation winning every time. When teams are asked to brainstorm while implicitly judging, the strangest ideas — which are statistically the most likely to be breakthrough ideas — die before they are spoken. Protecting the generative phase from evaluative interference is the core discipline of innovation facilitation.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

> *"Brainstorming as commonly practiced is an evaluation meeting with a permission slip."*

**Implication:** Run divergence and convergence in separate, explicitly named sessions. When you open a generative phase, announce it, set a quota, and enforce a no-evaluation rule. When you switch to evaluation, announce that too.

**The 'what does this make me think of?' reframe is Utley's signature micro-intervention for shifting a team from evaluative to generative mode. The question 'what do I think of this idea?' triggers critical judgment; the question 'what does this idea make me think of?' triggers associative thinking. This single linguistic pivot can unlock a stuck room in under a minute.** ([source](How to Master AI Powered Creativity in Just 13 Minutes — Jeremy Utley YouTube Channel))

**Implication:** Teach this one phrase to every facilitator and team lead in your organization. Post it on the wall of every meeting room where innovation work happens. It costs nothing and consistently shifts cognitive posture.

**Utley draws on Stanford d.school's design thinking tradition to argue that problem framing is an iterative, not linear, process. The team's first articulation of the problem is almost always a first draft — a placeholder that will be revised, narrowed, broadened, or completely discarded as they learn more. Treating the initial problem statement as fixed is the most common design thinking mistake.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Build explicit 'problem reframe' checkpoints into your innovation sprints — moments where the team is required to ask whether they are still solving the right problem before continuing. Make reframing an expected event, not a sign of failure.

**Utley identifies the instruction 'think of something new' as operationally useless advice.** The brain cannot execute that command because newness is not a retrievable category — it is an emergent property of unexpected collisions between existing frames. The operational replacement is 'think of connections' — specifically, connections between this problem and something apparently unrelated. ([source](Stop Trying to Be Original — Jeremy Utley YouTube Channel))

> *"The instruction 'think of something new' is bad advice. 'Think of connections' is operational."*

**Implication:** Replace 'think of new ideas' with 'find connections between this problem and...' in every ideation prompt you write. The more specific and surprising the second domain, the more novel the ideas that emerge.

**Originality, properly understood, is not the starting point of innovation — it is the outcome of combinatorial depth.** Utley argues that the obsession with being original is itself an innovation block because it frames the goal as producing something from nothing, which is neurologically impossible. The more productive frame is: what two things, when combined, have not been combined before? ([source](Stop Trying to Be Original — Jeremy Utley YouTube Channel))

**Implication:** Stop asking your teams to 'be more creative' or 'think more originally.' Instead, ask them to map the edges of their domain and identify what adjacent domains or technologies have never been connected to the problem at hand. Originality will follow.

**The belief that you are 'not a creative person' is itself the primary creative block — and it operates at the organizational level as much as the individual level. When teams accept the narrative that creativity belongs to certain people or certain departments, they structurally exclude most of the organization's cognitive capacity from the innovation process. Utley treats creativity as a trainable, measurable skill, which means the narrative is falsifiable.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

**Implication:** Audit the implicit creativity narrative in your organization. If innovation is siloed in a 'creative team' or 'innovation lab,' you have a framing problem before you have a process problem. Redistribute ideation as a universal expectation, not a specialist function.

**Utley draws on Stanford d.school's principle that empathy precedes ideation — you cannot frame a problem well without first understanding the human experience at its center. Teams that skip the empathy phase and proceed directly to 'How Might We' statements are framing problems through their own assumptions rather than through observed human behavior, which reliably produces solutions that solve no one's actual problem.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Make direct human observation or interview a required input before any HMW statement is finalized. Even a single thirty-minute conversation with someone who lives the problem will produce more accurate framing than hours of internal discussion.

**Utley notes that the Capgemini research context surfaced a consistent pattern.** leaders who believe they understand their customers' problems without direct observation are almost always solving a proxy problem — a smoothed, averaged, assumption-laden version of the real one. The practice of going back to direct observation whenever a solution feels stuck is the structural antidote to proxy-problem solving. ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

**Implication:** When a solution has been refined multiple times and still doesn't land, resist the urge to refine it further. Instead, go back to the problem definition and ask whether you are solving the problem people actually have or the problem you assumed they have.

**For Utley, the design thinking process is not a linear pipeline but a permeable membrane — teams should be able to move back from prototyping to ideation, from ideation to reframing, and from reframing to empathy whenever new information demands it. The innovation process that cannot move backward is not a process — it is a conveyor belt, and conveyor belts produce commodities.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Build explicit 'permission to regress' into your innovation process documentation. Make it clear that returning to an earlier phase when new information warrants it is intelligent process discipline, not project failure.

**Utley's research at Stanford d.school surfaces a consistent finding.** the teams that produce the most breakthrough outputs are not the teams with the most talented individuals — they are the teams with the most disciplined process for protecting the generative phase from premature evaluation. Process design predicts innovation output more reliably than talent composition. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

**Implication:** Stop optimizing your innovation teams primarily through talent selection. Optimize the process first — specifically, the structural protection of divergent thinking time. A disciplined process will outperform talented individuals operating inside a broken process.

**Most innovation failures are framing failures, not execution failures.** Teams spend enormous energy brilliantly solving the wrong problem because they skip the uncomfortable work of sitting with the question before generating solutions. The highest-leverage skill in innovation is not idea generation — it is problem definition and reframing before any solution-seeking begins. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** Before your next ideation session, invest at least as much time defining and reframing the problem as you plan to spend generating solutions. If the group can't articulate the problem three different ways, they aren't ready to brainstorm.

**Premature convergence is the silent killer of innovation processes.** Most teams instinctively rush from problem statement to solution, skipping the expansive, uncomfortable middle ground where the real insight lives. Sitting with a question — deliberately resisting the urge to answer it — is a trainable discipline, not a personality trait. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** Install a structural pause between problem statement and ideation in every innovation process. Make it explicit and timed: 'We are not going to generate solutions for the next twenty minutes. We are going to reframe the question.'

**Utley frames the diverge/converge distinction as meta-language that every innovation team must internalize.** Most meeting dysfunction comes not from bad ideas or bad people but from mismatched cognitive modes — one person is generating while another is filtering, and neither knows it. Naming the mode explicitly — 'are we flaring or focusing right now?' — is the facilitator's most powerful single intervention. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** Before every meeting that involves creative work, explicitly declare which mode you are in and why. If you catch the group switching modes prematurely, name it out loud rather than letting the collision continue invisibly.

**Idea quotas are not motivational gimmicks — they are the structural mechanism that changes the social contract inside a brainstorm. When the goal is 'share a good idea,' every participant filters ruthlessly before speaking, and the strange ideas die in silence. When the goal is 'help us hit 50,' participants stop self-editing and the collective intelligence of the room actually becomes available.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

> *"Quotas change the social contract from 'say something impressive' to 'help us hit the number,' and that is the only condition under which strange ideas survive."*

**Implication:** Set an explicit, uncomfortably high idea quota for every generative session — higher than seems reasonable. The discomfort of the number is a feature, not a bug; it forces the group past the obvious and into genuinely novel territory.

**Most organizations have a generation problem, not an evaluation problem.** Companies build sophisticated portfolio frameworks, stage gates, and scoring rubrics — elegant filters applied to an impoverished idea pipeline — and then wonder why nothing breakthrough emerges. A world-class filter on a mediocre pipeline still produces mediocrity. The leverage is almost always upstream of the funnel, in the generation stage. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** Audit your innovation process for where the real bottleneck sits. If you have more evaluation infrastructure than generation infrastructure, you have your answer. Redirect investment upstream — toward volume, diversity, and quality of ideas generated before any selection begins.

**The Wonder Wander is Utley's physical practice for escaping the closed loop of the brain reasoning with itself.** When stuck on a problem, you take the question into the world — a walk, a store, a neighborhood — and deliberately collide it with whatever you encounter. A traffic light becomes a metaphor for prioritization; a nail salon becomes a model for upselling. The world is a library of analogies for any problem you carry into it. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** When a team is stuck on a problem framing, send them on a twenty-minute Wonder Wander with the question written on a card. Reconvene and share what each person encountered and what it made them think about the problem. The collisions almost always produce reframes that desk-bound thinking cannot.

**Forced collision with random stimuli is a deliberate technique, not a happy accident.** Utley argues that the brain alone with itself only recirculates what it already knows, which is why breakthroughs rarely come from sitting quietly at a desk and thinking harder. Creativity requires importing novel frames — random images, objects, domains — and asking 'how does this relate to my problem?' to trigger genuinely new connections. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** When facilitating ideation sessions, bring random provocations into the room: magazine cutouts, objects from a dollar store, random words from a dictionary. Force explicit connections between the provocation and the problem. The strangeness of the connection is directly proportional to the novelty of the idea it produces.

**Utley consistently names 'ideaflow' as the leading indicator of innovation health — the volume and velocity of ideas per unit of time — in contrast to lagging indicators like revenue, launches, or patents. This leading/lagging distinction is not merely semantic: it determines where leaders direct attention and investment. If you want to change next year's innovation output, you must change this quarter's idea generation rate.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** Measure your team's ideaflow explicitly and regularly. Track how many ideas are generated per person per week in designated ideation time. Treat a declining ideaflow number as an early warning system for innovation health, not a cultural curiosity.

**Utley identifies a specific failure mode he calls the 'thin pipeline problem'.** organizations with sophisticated evaluation frameworks but weak generation practices. The solution is not a better filter — it is a dramatically higher volume of inputs entering the pipeline at the ideation stage. This requires treating idea generation as a discrete, protected, measurable activity rather than an incidental byproduct of meetings. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley))

**Implication:** Audit your last innovation cycle: how many distinct ideas were generated before selection began? If the number is under thirty, your evaluation infrastructure is irrelevant — the constraint is upstream. Invest in generation capacity before refining your selection criteria.

**Most innovation failures are not execution failures — they are framing failures.** Teams invest enormous energy solving the wrong problem brilliantly, because the discipline of sitting with a question before jumping to solutions is systematically undervalued. The highest-leverage moment in any innovation process is before the first idea is generated, not after. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Before your next brainstorm, invest at least as much time defining and reframing the problem as you plan to spend generating solutions. The question design phase is not overhead — it is the work.

**Most organizations treat the innovation process as a funnel that begins with ideas.** This is structurally wrong. The funnel begins with problems — specifically, problems that have been carefully selected, framed, and interrogated. Ideas generated against a poorly framed problem are not raw material for innovation; they are expensive distraction. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Invest in a formal 'problem portfolio' practice: a regularly curated list of the organization's most important unsolved problems, each expressed as an open question rather than a solution mandate. This is the actual top of the innovation funnel.

**Reframing is not a single act but an iterative practice.** Each new framing of a problem reveals assumptions embedded in the previous framing. The process of moving from the first framing to the fifth is not merely additive — it is transformative, as each iteration exposes the invisible constraints that were limiting the solution space all along. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Make reframing iterative and time-boxed. Set a norm that no problem framing is accepted until at least three to five alternatives have been generated and compared. The comparative act is what makes the embedded assumptions visible.

**Jeremy introduces the concept of an 'augmentation backlog' as a practical diagnostic tool.** He recommends listing things that went undone last week specifically because you were the bottleneck — not to solve them immediately, but to make the cost of unaugmented operation visible. ([source](This Is What Real Augmentation Looks Like — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Open a document. List three things that went undone last week because you were the bottleneck — proposals you didn't send, follow-ups you didn't write, ideas you didn't explore. Don't solve them yet. Just name them. That list is the map."*

**Implication:** Making the augmentation opportunity concrete and personal — rather than abstract — is a prerequisite to acting on it. Leaders who name their specific bottlenecks have a clearer target for AI integration than those who think about it generically.

**Utley's own curriculum evolution eliminated presentations, shifted to demo-first delivery, and built in live practice during sessions. These changes were driven by observing thousands of professionals fail to translate information into behavior. The core metric he now tracks is 'meaningful uplift in actual work,' not satisfaction scores or completion rates.** ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The first thing I eliminated? Presentations. The second thing? Lead by demo, not by explanation. The third? Carve out space in every session for people to actually try the tactics themselves—not later, not as homework, but right there in the room."*

**Implication:** The right success metric for AI learning programs is behavioral change and work impact, not engagement proxies. Program designers should audit whether their format — presentations, completion rates, satisfaction surveys — is actually measuring or producing real-world uplift.

**When leaders claim AI has made their workforce 'more innovative,' Jeremy suspects they mean 'producing more stuff faster' — which is efficiency, not innovation. Producing mediocre outputs faster is not a competitive advantage; it is losing more efficiently.** ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"When 78% of leaders say AI made them more innovative, I suspect what they mean is: 'We're producing more stuff faster.' That's efficiency, not innovation. And if you're producing mediocre stuff faster, you're not winning—you're just losing more efficiently."*

**Implication:** Speed and volume of output are not proxies for innovation. Leaders must distinguish between efficiency gains and genuine creative breakthroughs — and resist the temptation to conflate the two in their AI narratives.

**Jeremy frames initiative as a five-step 'Initiative Stack' he calls the 5 N's.** Notice, Name, Nudge, Nail, and Narrate. The sequence moves from personal friction-spotting through to broadcasting impact so others can replicate the solution. Each step is designed to be actionable before any formal approval or assignment. ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Notice — Keep a bug list. What frustrates you? What wastes time? AI will never notice; only you can. Name — Identify the unasked question. Nudge — Start before the meeting. Draft the doc. Build the prototype at lunch. Nail — Deliver the 8-hour proof. Narrate — Broadcast impact. Show before/after. Let others copy."*

**Implication:** Teams and individuals can use the 5 N's as an operational framework for converting personal frustration into organizational value — without waiting for top-down mandates or resource allocation.

**Utley's AI work produces measurable, concrete outcomes, not just mindset shifts.** His interventions have saved 7,000+ labor days for National Park Service rangers and helped reinvent corporate workflows at Qualtrics. These examples demonstrate that AI adoption, done right, creates quantifiable operational value. ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker))

> *"From saving 7,000+ labor days for National Park Service rangers to reinventing corporate workflows at Qualtrics, he turns complex AI ideas into actionable results."*

**Implication:** AI transformation should be evaluated against tangible outcomes — labor hours recovered, processes eliminated, decisions accelerated. Utley's case studies provide a model for how to frame and measure AI's organizational ROI.

**Creative problem-solving requires space — psychological and temporal — to function effectively.** Without space, the incubation stage of the creative process is bypassed, and people jump from preparation directly to forced output. Space is not a luxury for creative work; it is a structural requirement. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck))

> *"Creativity and problem solving, more broadly, needs space."*

**Implication:** Teams and individuals should audit how much unstructured, low-pressure time exists in their work rhythms. If every hour is scheduled and accountable, creative breakthroughs become statistically unlikely regardless of talent or effort.

**Most people and organizations default heavily toward convergent thinking — making decisions, eliminating options, reducing variability. But divergent thinking, which generates options and embraces variation, is equally critical to innovation and far less practiced. The imbalance stems from education, organizational incentives, and ingrained norms.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders))

> *"The challenge is recognizing that diverging, generating options, embracing variation is also critical to the innovation process and the creative process. And yet we're much less practiced at that. And because of practice, because of our education, because of our organizational incentives and structures and norms, we're much more comfortable converging."*

**Implication:** Organizations need to deliberately create protected space and explicit permission for divergent thinking — it won't happen naturally because every structural force pushes people toward premature convergence.

**The Einstellung effect — identified since the 1940s — is the cognitive bias where people fixate on a suboptimal solution even when a better one is available. Related phenomena like 'need for cognitive closure' (Kruglanski) and 'satisficing' (Herbert Simon) all reflect the same underlying drive to resolve problems quickly rather than finding the best answer.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"The Einstellung effect has been demonstrated since the 1940s — Abraham and Edith Luchins identified this tendency of a human to fixate on a solution despite the fact that there's a better solution available. That's closely related to what Arie Kruglanski calls longing for cognitive closure — there's a tendency to want to resolve things as quickly as possible."*

**Implication:** Awareness of the Einstellung effect allows innovators to deliberately pause when they believe they've found a solution and ask whether better alternatives remain unexplored.

---

## Problem Finding & Question Design

**The reason you don't have better ideas is almost never a lack of creative capacity — it is a lack of better inputs and better questions. Idea quality is downstream of question quality. Upgrading the prompts, briefs, and questions you work from is a more reliable path to better ideas than any technique applied to the generation phase itself.** ([source](The Reason You Don't Have Better Ideas — Jeremy Utley YouTube channel))

**Implication:** Before investing in ideation techniques or creativity training, audit the quality of the questions and briefs your team is working from. Often a one-hour reframing session on the problem statement will produce more impact than a full-day brainstorming workshop.

**AI dramatically amplifies the leverage of good question design while exposing the cost of bad question design.** A precisely framed prompt to an AI system yields dramatically superior outputs compared to a vague one. The human skill that AI makes most visible — and most valuable — is the ability to ask the right question. Problem finding becomes the primary differentiator in an AI-augmented world. ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The scarce resource shifts entirely to the front end: insight, framing, taste, and the ability to ask questions that machines can't generate on their own."*

**Implication:** As AI handles execution, the return on investment for question design skills increases dramatically. Leaders and builders who develop the discipline of precise, creative problem framing will extract exponentially more value from AI tools than those who use AI as a vague brainstorming partner.

**Asking better questions is not just a facilitation technique — it is a competitive advantage.** In a world where execution is increasingly automated, the ability to identify the right problem to work on becomes the primary source of strategic differentiation. Utley argues that AI will make this even more pronounced: the humans who win will be those who can ask questions that machines cannot generate. ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Invest in question-asking as a formal organizational capability — not just a soft skill. Train facilitators, build question-generation rituals into innovation processes, and evaluate leaders partly on the quality of the questions they ask, not just the decisions they make.

**The board — or any leadership body that has been insulated from frontline experience — often cannot imagine futures it hasn't lived. This is a problem-finding deficit: if the people responsible for identifying strategic challenges are disconnected from emerging realities, the organization will systematically misframe its most important questions. Problem-finding requires proximity to the problem.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Leaders who want to find better problems must stay close to the ground: customers, emerging technologies, adjacent industries, and their own team's daily friction. Strategic distance from the problem produces strategic misframing. Build deliberate feedback loops that keep leadership in contact with ground-level reality.

**Utley frames AI prompting as a direct extension of question design discipline.** The people who get extraordinary outputs from AI are not the ones with the best technical knowledge of the tool — they are the ones who have developed the human skill of precise, creative question formulation. Prompt engineering is problem framing applied to a new medium. ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Be the prompt."*

**Implication:** Frame your AI practice as question design practice. Every interaction with an AI tool is a repetition in the discipline of problem formulation. Teams that use AI heavily will, over time, either get better or worse at asking questions — the outcome depends on whether they treat prompting as a thoughtless habit or a deliberate craft.

**Utley argues that the imagination ceiling — the limit on what people can envision as possible — is often located in the problem statement itself. When a problem is framed in terms of existing categories ('How do we improve our current product?'), it activates only existing knowledge. When it is framed in terms of desired outcomes ('How might we make our customers feel X?'), it opens access to a much wider range of creative possibilities.** ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Shift your problem statements from process-oriented to outcome-oriented. Instead of 'How do we fix our onboarding flow?' ask 'How might we make new users feel capable and excited within their first five minutes?' Outcome framing breaks the gravitational pull of existing solutions.

**The reason most companies have a generation problem rather than an evaluation problem applies equally to the question space. Organizations have robust processes for evaluating which problems to prioritize — roadmaps, OKRs, strategy reviews — but almost no process for generating a rich set of possible problem framings in the first place. The funnel is wide at evaluation and narrow at problem generation, when it should be the reverse.** ([source](Methods of the Masters blog — Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Flip your innovation funnel. Generate many possible framings of your strategic challenge before applying any prioritization or evaluation criteria. The OKR you set is only as good as the problem-finding process that preceded it. Most strategic misfires are upstream of the roadmap.

**The best problem-solvers are actually great problem-finders.** The premium skill in innovation is not solution generation — it is knowing which problem is worth solving in the first place. Organizations that skip the problem-framing phase are optimizing the wrong function entirely. ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker profile / Methods of the Masters))

**Implication:** Reorient how you evaluate innovative talent. Stop rewarding the person who generates the cleverest answers and start rewarding the person who surfaces the most important questions. Problem-finding is the senior skill.

**The 'How Might We?' framing — a core tool from Stanford's d.school — does something precise and powerful: it presupposes that a solution exists, invites collaboration through 'we,' and scopes the problem at the right altitude of generativity. Too broad and the prompt paralyzes; too narrow and it predetermines the answer. Finding the generative middle is itself a creative act.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Practice writing five different 'How Might We?' framings for every important challenge before generating solutions. Notice how each framing opens a different solution space — and choose the one that opens the richest territory.

**Innovation processes that skip directly to solution generation are actually solving an implicit problem that no one has interrogated. Every team has a 'presenting problem' — what they say they're trying to solve — and a 'real problem' — the underlying need driving the presenting one. The discipline of asking 'why is this the problem?' repeatedly before generating solutions is what separates design thinking from conventional brainstorming.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer podcast))

**Implication:** Before any ideation session, run a structured 'five whys' or root-cause analysis to distinguish the presenting problem from the real problem. You may discover the brief you were given is solving the wrong thing.

**One of the most powerful reframing moves in innovation is shifting from 'how do we solve this?' to 'who has already solved this, somewhere?' The adjacent possible is full of solved problems that are structurally identical to the one you face — they just wear different clothes. Systematic borrowing from analogous domains is not laziness; it is sophisticated problem framing.** ([source](Methods of the Masters — Jeremy Utley blog essays))

**Implication:** Make analogical thinking a standard phase in your problem-framing process. Before generating original solutions, ask: where has this exact underlying problem been solved in another industry, era, or context? Let existing solutions reframe the question.

**One of Utley's core teaching moves at the Stanford d.school is separating the generative and evaluative phases of thinking — and this applies equally to the problem space. Teams tend to evaluate problem definitions before they have generated enough of them. The result is premature lock-in on the first plausible problem framing rather than the best one.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Apply the same 'no judgment' rule to problem generation that you apply to idea generation. Generate a large number of possible problem definitions before evaluating any of them. Premature commitment to a problem frame is as costly as premature commitment to a solution.

**The 'How Might We?' prompt is not just a linguistic convention — it is a structural move that holds three critical design choices simultaneously. 'How' assumes there is a solution. 'Might' removes the pressure of correctness and invites possibility. 'We' signals that this is a collaborative challenge. Together, the phrase opens a generative space that neither a closed question nor a statement of the problem can create.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Swap declarative problem statements for 'How Might We?' prompts before any creative session. The linguistic structure of the question is not cosmetic — it is psychologically load-bearing. The right prompt design can double the creative output of the same group of people.

**Problem finding is a trainable skill, not an innate gift.** Just as Utley argues that creativity itself is a learnable discipline, the ability to identify and frame the right problem can be developed through deliberate practice — specifically through the habit of generating multiple reframings of the same challenge before committing to any single definition. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

**Implication:** Before accepting the first formulation of a problem, generate at least five alternative framings. Each reframing reveals different constraints, different stakeholders, and different solution spaces. The team that practices reframing as a discipline will consistently out-innovate the team that takes problem definitions at face value.

**Most organizations treat the problem definition as a given — handed down from leadership, inherited from last quarter's strategy, or assumed from customer complaints. This is the structural source of most innovation misfires. The question itself needs to be questioned. Utley's design thinking framework begins not with divergent solution generation but with divergent problem generation — surfacing multiple competing definitions of what the real challenge is.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Make problem generation — not just solution generation — an explicit team activity. Bring your team together to generate ten different framings of your current strategic challenge before you brainstorm any solutions. The reframe you uncover is often worth more than any answer you could have found.

**Reframing is not just a creative technique — it is a diagnostic tool.** When a team is stuck, the most productive intervention is rarely more brainstorming; it is stepping back to ask whether the problem has been correctly identified. Utley consistently applies this move in his Stanford d.school teaching: the breakthrough often comes not from a better answer but from a better question. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

**Implication:** When your team hits a creative wall, resist the instinct to push harder on solutions. Instead, run a reframing exercise: 'What if the problem isn't X but actually Y?' The most valuable 30 minutes in a stuck project is often spent questioning the question itself.

**The 'How Might We?' question format is specifically designed to sit at the correct level of abstraction — not so broad that it gives no direction, not so narrow that it forecloses the solution space. Calibrating question breadth is itself a skill: too broad ('How might we improve the world?') and you get paralysis; too narrow ('How might we add a blue button?') and you get iteration, not innovation.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

**Implication:** Practice tuning your 'How Might We?' prompts to the right level of scope. A well-calibrated question is broad enough to allow surprising solutions but specific enough to point creative energy in a productive direction. Test your question by asking: does it invite multiple meaningfully different answers? If not, widen it.

**Utley draws a sharp distinction between the problem a customer articulates and the problem they actually have.** The articulated complaint is a symptom; the real problem is often one level deeper. Design thinking's empathy and observation phases exist precisely to surface the underlying challenge that customers cannot self-diagnose — the gap between the reported problem and the real one is where genuine innovation lives. ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Never accept the first problem statement a customer, user, or stakeholder offers. Use empathy interviews, observation, and 'five whys' techniques to move from the symptom to the root cause. The most valuable insight is almost never the one that comes first.

**Utley consistently points out that the question 'How might we?' is not just a brainstorming opener — it is a commitment to a specific cognitive mode. Entering a meeting with a 'How might we?' question signals to every participant that the room is in generative, not evaluative, mode. This environmental signal changes the quality of participation before a single idea is offered.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer Podcast))

**Implication:** Use the explicit language of 'How Might We?' as an environmental design tool, not just a semantic one. When you open a meeting with a HMW question instead of a problem statement, you change the psychological contract of the room. People arrive to generate, not to defend.

**Utley's design thinking pedagogy at the Stanford d.school treats the problem brief not as a starting constraint but as a provisional hypothesis. The initial problem statement is a place to begin exploration, not a specification to execute against. This distinction — between a fixed brief and a generative hypothesis — is foundational to creative problem-finding.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Treat your initial problem brief as a hypothesis, not a contract. Build in explicit checkpoints to revisit and revise the problem definition as you learn more. The most successful innovation projects are often the ones that ended up solving a different problem than they started with.

**One of Utley's recurring observations is that the questions leaders ask in meetings determine the quality of thinking in their organizations. A leader who asks 'What's the solution?' gets answers; a leader who asks 'What's the real problem here?' gets insight. Question design is a leadership behavior, not just a facilitation technique — and it cascades through the entire organization.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

**Implication:** Audit the questions you ask in your own meetings for the next two weeks. Are you asking for solutions or interrogating the problem? The questions you ask most frequently become the cognitive defaults of your team. Leaders who are great question-askers build organizations that are great at finding the right problems.

**Utley connects the skill of problem-finding directly to the capacity for empathy — the ability to see a situation from a perspective other than your own. The reason experts often miss the real problem is not lack of intelligence but lack of beginner's eyes. The most valuable problem-finders are those who can temporarily suspend their expertise and see a challenge the way a newcomer — or a user — would see it.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Build structured empathy practices into your problem-finding process: user interviews, job shadows, customer immersions. The goal is not just to gather data but to reset your own perception of what the problem actually is. Expertise is an asset in solution space and a liability in problem space.

**The best problem-solvers are actually great problem-finders.** The premium skill in innovation is not solution generation — it is knowing which problem is worth solving. Organizations that skip the problem-framing phase are optimizing the wrong function, investing enormous energy solving brilliantly-defined wrong problems. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Before your next sprint or brainstorm, invest deliberate time in the question space. Ask whether you have correctly identified the problem before mobilizing resources to solve it. Reframing the question is often worth more than any solution you could generate.

**Most innovation failures are fundamentally framing failures.** Teams invest enormous energy solving the wrong problem brilliantly. The discipline of problem framing — sitting with the question, reframing from multiple angles, designing 'How might we?' prompts before generating solutions — is the highest-leverage skill in the entire innovation toolkit. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** The moment a team jumps from 'here's the problem' to 'let's brainstorm solutions' is often the moment innovation fails. Build a deliberate pause — a problem-framing phase — before any solution-generation activity. The quality of the question determines the ceiling of every answer.

**Spending time in the question space is not indecision — it is the highest-leverage phase of the creative process.** Great innovators are disproportionately patient with questions and impatient with premature answers. The rush to solutions is a form of creative impatience that systematically produces inferior outcomes. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Reframe 'dwelling in questions' as a productive activity, not a delay. Schedule and protect time for question exploration explicitly — make it a visible, calendared phase of the innovation process rather than something that gets compressed under deadline pressure.

**The way a problem is framed determines the solution space before a single idea is generated.** A narrowly framed question produces incremental answers; a broadly framed question opens exponentially more solution territory. Question design is therefore a form of upstream architecture — it determines the shape of everything downstream. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Treat the framing of a challenge as a design artifact in its own right — something to be iterated, tested, and refined. Share multiple framings of the same problem with your team and observe how the solution ideas shift. The best frame is the one that unleashes the most creative energy.

**The distinction between problem-finding and problem-solving maps directly onto the distinction between leading and lagging indicators in Utley's broader framework. Problem-finding is the upstream, generative activity that determines the quality of everything downstream. Investing in problem-finding capacity is the innovation equivalent of investing in a leading indicator.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Measure how much time your team invests in problem-finding versus problem-solving. If your ratio heavily favors solving over finding, you are likely working on well-optimized answers to the wrong questions. Rebalance deliberately.

**There is a systematic organizational bias toward solution space over problem space.** Meetings are structured to evaluate options, not to interrogate the question. Budgets are allocated to execution, not to exploration. Timelines reward delivery, not discovery. This structural bias means organizations are systematically under-investing in the phase of innovation where the highest leverage lives. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Audit your meeting structures, budget allocations, and timeline incentives for bias toward solution space. If you have no formally protected time for problem-finding and question exploration, you have structurally guaranteed that your team will work on the wrong problems efficiently.

**Jeremy identifies imagination — not model capability — as the binding constraint in advanced AI systems.** Once the technical stack is working, the limit is the user's ability to conceive of worthwhile questions to ask. This reframes AI literacy as primarily a creative and conceptual challenge, not a technical one. ([source](This Is What Real Augmentation Looks Like — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The limit is increasingly not model capability. It is my ability to imagine worthwhile questions."*

**Implication:** Investment in AI tools without investment in the imaginative capacity to use them will hit a ceiling quickly. Developing the skill to ask better questions — and building visceral experience of what's possible — is the real leverage point for individuals and organizations.

**Jeremy connects his longstanding thesis about question quality directly to advanced AI use.** He had previously written that 'the answer is yes, so what's your question?' — and here he identifies the lived next-order version: when the system can execute almost anything, the only remaining bottleneck is the quality of what you ask it to do. ([source](This Is What Real Augmentation Looks Like — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"That echoes something I wrote recently — the answer is yes, so what's your question? Josh is living the next-order version. When the stack works, your bottleneck isn't the tool. It's you — specifically, your imagination."*

**Implication:** The most important skill in an AI-augmented world is not prompt engineering in a narrow sense but the broader capacity to imagine valuable problems and frame them well. This is a creative and strategic competency, not a technical one.

**Leaders who have normalized inefficiency stop seeing it as opportunity.** Jeremy cites a CEO who claimed he had 'no pain points to automate' while also spending six hours editing every report his team sent to clients — a vivid example of how deep habituation to friction blinds people to obvious leverage points. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The CEO who told me he had 'no pain points to automate' also mentioned he spends six hours editing every report his team sends to clients. When you normalize inefficiency so completely, you stop seeing it as an opportunity."*

**Implication:** The first step in AI adoption is often perceptual — learning to see normalized friction as automatable waste. Leaders should regularly audit their own workflows with fresh eyes, or enlist outside observers to identify invisible inefficiencies.

**Utley proposes a set of questions boards should be asking that go beyond 'is our AI strategy sound' — including whether leaders are using AI on their most important decisions, when they were last surprised by what's possible, and whether the company is setting goals that are impossible without AI augmentation. These questions require personal AI experience to ask and evaluate credibly.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Show me how you're personally using AI for the most important decisions—not the least... Are we setting goals that are impossible to achieve without AI augmentation? Or are we setting goals that 'mere humans' could hit?"*

**Implication:** Boards and senior leaders should add these diagnostic questions to their governance practice immediately. The questions reframe AI oversight from a technology question to a strategic ambition and personal practice question.

**Utley uses survey non-response as a discovery mechanism.** a Stanford professor bypassed the multiple-choice options entirely to write in the real reason he hadn't enrolled. This unsolicited candor revealed an emotional truth that structured options had no category for — fear disguised as indifference. ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters blog))
