> *"A Stanford professor friend of mine didn't click any of them. He replied directly to the email instead."*

**Implication:** Product builders and educators should design feedback channels that allow freeform response — closed-choice surveys can systematically miss the emotional drivers behind non-adoption.

**Even 'agentic AI' operates toward goals that must first be given by a human.** Ethan Mollick's definition — AI that plans and acts autonomously 'toward given goals' — reveals that goal-setting and caring about outcomes remain irreducibly human responsibilities. Someone still has to 'be the prompt.' ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"AI that can plan and act autonomously toward given goals. Toward given goals. Someone still has to give them. Someone has to care first. Someone has to be the prompt."*

**Implication:** Even as AI autonomy increases, the human role shifts upstream to goal-setting, problem-framing, and caring about outcomes — making problem-finding skills more valuable, not less.

**Maintaining a 'bug list' — a running log of daily frictions and frustrations — is the foundational act of proactive innovation. Because AI cannot notice what annoys or wastes time in a specific human context, personal observation of friction is a uniquely human input. The bug list converts ambient irritation into actionable opportunity.** ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Notice — Keep a bug list. What frustrates you? What wastes time? AI will never notice; only you can."*

**Implication:** Professionals at all levels should treat their daily frustrations as a strategic asset, not background noise. A bug list is a low-cost, high-signal source of innovation opportunities that requires no special access or authority.

**The answer to nearly every 'could AI do this?' question is yes — the bottleneck is that most people haven't yet formed the question. Utley illustrates this with NBA legend Manu Ginobili raising his hand during a Spurs AI session: the moment someone asks 'could AI...?' is the moment real value creation begins.** ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker))

> *"When Manu Ginobili raised his hand during a Spurs AI session and said 'Could AI...?' — that was the ball game. The answer to almost every 'could it' question is yes. The problem is most people don't have a question yet."*

**Implication:** The constraint on AI value is not capability — it's question formation. Leaders should invest in developing the habit of asking 'could AI do this?' across every domain, because the capability almost certainly exists.

**Frustration and constraint are not obstacles to innovation — they are often the raw material of it.** Bette Nesmith Graham's invention emerged directly from a specific, felt frustration with a recurring problem. Paying attention to friction in your own experience is a legitimate and productive creative input. ([source](youtube:stop-trying-to-be-original-jeremy-utley))

> *"She has been frustrated with her typewriter at Texas Bank and Trust because it's always smudging her paper... why don't I paint over the mistake? She invented Liquid Paper."*

**Implication:** Innovators and creative practitioners should treat personal frustration as a signal worth mining — it points to unmet needs that, when combined with insights from other domains, can become the basis for genuine breakthroughs.

**In creative workflows, posing multiple questions — not just one — is fundamental to generating breadth of ideas.** If you ask only one question and it's the wrong one, you're stuck. Asking more than one minimizes the risk of solving the wrong problem and prevents premature evaluation by keeping the option space open longer. ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

> *"One of the most important things is to pose more than one question. Seek more than one answer. Try more than one thing... Often, people are seeking to address the wrong problem. If you ask only one question, and it's the wrong one, you're stuck."*

**Implication:** Innovators and product teams should build in a 'question multiplication' step before any ideation session — generating 5–10 problem framings before locking in on one. AI makes this cheap and fast to execute.

**Problem framing is itself a valuable creative output, not just a precursor to solutions.** Utley cites John Dewey's principle that 'a problem well put is half solved,' arguing that the articulation of the right problem is a non-trivial and meaningful stepping stone toward innovation. When the problem is properly framed, the solution almost handles itself. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders))

> *"John Dewey, who's an American educational philosopher, he said, a problem well put is half solved. And a lot of times what we find is when you frame the problem properly, the solution almost handles itself. And so much of the innovation actually in the design thinking approach writ large is legitimizing the problem as a meaningful output of a design effort."*

**Implication:** Teams should budget significant time and energy for problem definition and treat a well-articulated problem statement as a deliverable worth celebrating — not a box to rush through on the way to solutions.

**A 'bug list' — a written record of small frustrations and annoyances — is a powerful innovation tool because small frustrations are the seeds of innovation. Every great product started as somebody's annoyance.** ([source](youtube:the-habit-that-built-every-genius-jeremy-utley))

> *"One of the most powerful practices we teach at the D-School is something called a bug list. Write down the things that bug you. Frustrations, annoyances, small ways in which you see the world doesn't work the way it should. Because small frustrations are the seeds of innovation. Every great product started as somebody else's annoyance."*

**Implication:** Systematically cataloguing frustrations is a concrete, accessible method for finding genuine innovation opportunities rooted in real human experience.

**Keeping a 'bug list' — a running record of things that bother or frustrate you — is a powerful way to court creative inspiration. Problems are the seed of novel solutions, and actively cataloguing them builds an innovation input bank that can generate ideas on demand.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"One thing that I would recommend people to do is keep a bug list — write down the list of things that bug you. It turns out that's actually a fantastic way of courting the muse. One of the best ways that you can court the muse or have novel solutions is pay attention to problems, write down problems that you see."*

**Implication:** Innovators should actively document friction points in daily life rather than ignoring them, treating personal irritations as raw material for future problem-solving.

---

## Brainstorming & Facilitation

**AI is now an available participant in any brainstorm — and unlike human participants, it has no ego investment in any particular idea, no fear of judgment, and access to cross-disciplinary patterns across an enormous knowledge base. Using AI as a brainstorming partner before a human session can pre-populate the idea space and prevent the anchoring effect of a single first idea dominating the room.** ([source](Don't Use AI. Work With It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Before your next team brainstorm, spend 20 minutes running the same 'How Might We' prompt through an AI tool. Don't filter the output — bring it into the room as raw material. The goal is not to use the AI's ideas but to expand the frontier of what seems possible before human generation begins.

**Most leaders blame their people when brainstorming sessions produce uninspired output.** The real culprit is session design: who speaks first dominates the group, hierarchy silences dissent, and the loudest voices crowd out the quietest ones. The problem is structural, not personal — and structural problems require structural solutions. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

> *"The brainstorming session itself is broken. It's not your people who are the problem."*

**Implication:** Before concluding your team lacks creative talent, audit your meeting format. If people speak sequentially in a group, if the leader shares ideas first, if there is no protected quiet generation time — the format is suppressing output that already exists in the room.

**Brainwriting — having participants generate ideas silently and independently before any group discussion — is a structurally superior alternative to verbal brainstorming. It eliminates anchoring bias, prevents social loafing, and ensures every voice contributes equally regardless of personality or hierarchy. The ideas then enter the room anonymously or simultaneously, which changes the entire social dynamic of evaluation.** ([source](Brainstorming Alone? Here's the Best Method — Jeremy Utley (own channel)))

**Implication:** Replace the open verbal brainstorm with a silent individual generation round first. Give everyone five minutes to write ideas independently before a single word is spoken. This one structural change will materially increase both the quantity and diversity of ideas surfaced.

**Solo brainstorming, done properly, can outperform group brainstorming.** The research literature consistently shows that individuals brainstorming alone and then pooling ideas generate more and higher-quality ideas than the same number of people in a conventional group session. The group dynamic introduces production blocking, evaluation apprehension, and conformity pressure that silences the most novel thinking. ([source](Brainstorming Alone? Here's the Best Method — Jeremy Utley (own channel)))

**Implication:** Don't assume a group session is always better than individual generation. For high-stakes innovation challenges, consider assigning solo ideation homework before any group convenes — the pooled output will likely be richer than anything generated in a two-hour group session.

**The question design preceding a brainstorm is often more important than the brainstorm itself.** 'How might we improve customer satisfaction?' generates fundamentally different — and typically shallower — ideas than 'How might we make our customers feel like insiders rather than users?' The specificity, perspective, and embedded assumptions of the prompt shape the entire solution space before anyone generates a single idea. ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Invest serious time — potentially more time than the brainstorm itself — crafting the 'How Might We' question before sessions begin. Reframe it from multiple angles, stress-test its assumptions, and choose the version that opens the widest generative space rather than the most comfortable or obvious one.

**Effective facilitation is environmental design, not content expertise.** The facilitator's job is not to have the best ideas but to create conditions — physical arrangement, time structure, question sequencing, social safety — in which the best ideas can emerge from others. The facilitator who contributes the most ideas in a session is typically doing the job wrong. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

**Implication:** Separate the role of content expert from the role of facilitator in your sessions. The person with the most domain knowledge is often the worst facilitator because their presence reshapes the social dynamics and narrows the solution space to their own mental models.

**The leader who speaks first in a brainstorm has already killed the session's potential.** When authority figures share their ideas early, every subsequent contributor unconsciously anchors to that position — either elaborating on it or self-censoring ideas that deviate from it. Leaders who want real divergence must speak last, or not at all, during the generative phase. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

**Implication:** If you are the most senior person in the room, establish a personal rule: do not share your own ideas during the generative phase of any brainstorm. Your job in that window is to ask questions, energize contributions from others, and record — not to generate.

**The standard advice to 'build on each other's ideas' in brainstorming sessions is well-intentioned but often counterproductive. Building on an idea prematurely closes down alternative directions before they've been explored. True divergent thinking requires the courage to generate ideas that have no relationship to what came before — orthogonal ideas, not just extensions of the first idea voiced.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer Podcast))

**Implication:** Explicitly invite 'completely different directions' during your generative phase, not just 'yes, and...' elaborations. After a cluster of related ideas forms, actively redirect with prompts like 'What's a completely different approach?' to prevent premature convergence on an early anchor.

**Cross-disciplinary inputs are not a luxury for brainstorming — they are a structural requirement for non-obvious ideas.** When a room contains only domain experts, the ideas generated will cluster around the existing vocabulary of that domain. Genuine novelty almost always comes from importing a frame, metaphor, analogy, or mechanism from an entirely unrelated field. ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Deliberately include at least one 'outsider' in every significant brainstorm — someone from a different function, industry, or background whose lack of domain expertise is precisely what makes their contributions valuable. Brief them on the problem without the existing solution space.

**The 'How Might We' framing — a tool Utley teaches consistently from the Stanford d.school — is a facilitation technology that simultaneously opens possibility and focuses direction. 'How' assumes a solution exists. 'Might' removes the obligation for the solution to be perfect or even good. 'We' assigns collective ownership. Each word is doing structural work that changes the cognitive and social conditions for ideation.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Train your team to recognize the difference between a problem statement and a 'How Might We' prompt. Problem statements close down generation; HMW prompts open it. Make HMW reframing a standard pre-session ritual — not a one-time tool but a recurring facilitation habit.

**Physical and spatial conditions in a brainstorming environment send powerful signals about what kind of thinking is expected and permitted. Whiteboards, sticky notes on walls, standing rather than sitting, and visual displays of accumulated ideas all lower the psychological cost of contributing and raise the perceived value of generative quantity. Room design is facilitation before the facilitator speaks.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

**Implication:** Before evaluating your session design, evaluate your room design. If participants are seated at a conference table facing a presenter, the environment is optimized for evaluation and presentation — not generation. Change the physical configuration to change the cognitive and social output.

**Analogical thinking — deliberately importing structures, mechanisms, and solutions from unrelated domains — is one of the most reliable facilitation techniques for escaping local creative optima. Asking 'How does a hospital solve this kind of problem?' or 'How does a jazz ensemble handle this challenge?' forces the brain out of its domain-specific vocabulary and into genuinely new territory.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer Podcast))

**Implication:** Build an analogical prompt library for your facilitation practice. Before each session, prepare three to five 'How does [unrelated domain] solve this?' prompts. Deploy them specifically when the group's idea generation stalls or clusters too tightly around existing approaches.

**The 'yes, and...' principle from improvisational theater is a foundational facilitation technology because it structurally prevents the evaluative reflex. In standard conversation, 'yes, but...' is the default — it accepts the premise while redirecting toward a problem. 'Yes, and...' accepts the premise while extending it. This small linguistic shift changes the generative culture of an entire session.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

**Implication:** Introduce 'yes, and...' as an explicit ground rule at the start of ideation sessions, and have the facilitator model it visibly in their own responses. When participants hear the facilitator say 'yes, and...' to an impractical idea, it gives everyone else permission to contribute without filtering.

**Time pressure, properly calibrated, is a facilitation asset rather than a liability.** Short, focused sprints — 'generate as many ideas as you can in five minutes' — activate a generative urgency that prevents the overthinking and self-editing that suppress novel ideas under open-ended conditions. The time constraint shifts the cognitive goal from 'produce a good idea' to 'produce an idea,' which is the correct target in the divergent phase. ([source](Brainstorming Alone? Here's the Best Method — Jeremy Utley (own channel)))

**Implication:** Use timed micro-sprints within your brainstorming sessions rather than open-ended ideation windows. Two or three five-minute sprints with different question frames will typically outperform a single twenty-minute open session in both quantity and diversity of output.

**The most creative ideas in any brainstorm are often the ones that make the room laugh nervously.** Absurdity and apparent impracticality are signals of genuine novelty — an idea that departs far enough from convention to feel ridiculous is also departing far enough to potentially be breakthrough. Skilled facilitators protect these ideas from immediate dismissal precisely because they signal the outer edge of the possibility space. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

**Implication:** Train yourself and your team to treat nervous laughter as a positive signal during brainstorming, not a negative one. After a session, go back to the ideas that provoked the strongest skeptical reactions and ask: 'What would have to be true for this to work?' That question often unlocks more value than the 'realistic' ideas.

**The real test of a brainstorming culture is not what happens in the session — it is what happens afterward.** If ideas generated in sessions consistently disappear without acknowledgment, evaluation, or follow-up, participants learn quickly that generation is not valued and stop investing in it. Closing the loop — even to say 'this idea was considered and here's why it wasn't pursued' — is a facilitation discipline that sustains the creative culture between sessions. ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer Podcast))

**Implication:** Build a visible 'idea lifecycle' process that participants can see. When teams see their generated ideas tracked, discussed, and occasionally pursued — even when most are declined — they maintain the engagement and trust necessary for high ideaflow in future sessions.

**The discipline of separating divergent and convergent thinking phases is one of the core structural contributions of design thinking to innovation practice. Divergent thinking — expanding the problem and solution space without judgment — must be protected from premature convergence. Most organizations never truly enter divergent mode because convergent pressure (deadlines, seniority, consensus norms) is always present.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Create explicit structural protections for divergent phases: time boundaries, no-critique norms, and senior leaders who model exploratory rather than evaluative participation. The environment must make divergence safe before divergence happens.

**Utley consistently argues that 'yes, and' — the improv principle of building on others' contributions rather than redirecting or replacing them — is not just a creativity warm-up game but the structural rule that enables genuine collaborative ideation. It keeps the generative phase alive by ensuring every contribution is a stepping stone rather than a dead end.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck podcast))

**Implication:** Introduce 'yes, and' as an explicit rule in your ideation sessions, not as a warm-up but as the operating protocol. Train facilitators to intervene when 'but' or 'however' appears during the generative phase.

**Conventional brainstorming fails because it conflates two cognitively incompatible activities.** generating ideas and evaluating them. When both happen simultaneously in the same room, evaluation always wins — social pressure, hierarchy, and the fear of judgment suppress ideas before they can be voiced. The single most important facilitation discipline is separating the divergent phase from the convergent phase entirely. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Redesign every brainstorming session to have a strict generation phase with zero criticism allowed before any evaluative conversation begins. The structure of the meeting is more powerful than the talent in the room.

**Quantity is not the enemy of quality in ideation — it is the prerequisite for it.** Teams that generate 100 ideas are statistically far more likely to surface a breakthrough than teams searching for the single perfect idea. The path to the best idea runs through a large number of ideas, not around them. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

> *"The team that generates a hundred ideas is far more likely to find a breakthrough than the team searching for the perfect one."*

**Implication:** Set explicit idea quotas in your sessions. If your team generates fewer than 20-30 ideas before evaluating, you are likely culling from a pool too shallow to contain your best options. Volume is a process requirement, not a sign of unfocused thinking.

**Premature judgment is the single greatest idea killer in organizational life.** The moment a team member senses that their idea will be evaluated before it is fully heard, they self-censor — and the ideas that get suppressed first are typically the strangest ones, which are often the most valuable. Protecting the generative phase requires active, explicit facilitation to hold judgment at bay. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

> *"Premature judgment is the idea killer."*

**Implication:** Facilitators must do more than say 'there are no bad ideas.' They must actively interrupt evaluative language — including subtle signals like furrowed brows, 'yes, but...' responses, or silence — that signals judgment is already operating. Create explicit rules and enforce them visibly.

**Ideaflow — the volume and velocity of idea generation per unit of time — is a measurable leading indicator of creative health. Most organizations measure innovation by lagging outputs: revenue from new products, patents filed, launches completed. These metrics tell you what already happened. Ideaflow tells you what is coming. Teams with high ideaflow consistently produce better downstream outcomes.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

> *"Ideaflow is the only innovation metric that matters."*

**Implication:** Add an ideaflow metric to your team's regular operating rhythm. Count ideas generated per session, per week, per sprint. Track the trend line. A declining ideaflow number is an early warning system — not for lack of talent, but for a process or environment that is suppressing generation.

**Setting an explicit idea quota transforms the psychology of brainstorming.** When a team is told 'we need 50 ideas before we evaluate any of them,' the social contract shifts from 'say something impressive' to 'help us hit the number.' This removes the performance anxiety that kills ideation and replaces it with a collaborative production target that makes even obviously bad ideas feel like contributions. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Name a specific number at the start of every ideation session — one that feels slightly uncomfortable to reach. The stretch forces the group past obvious ideas into genuinely novel territory. The best ideas almost always appear in the second half of the quota, after the obvious options are exhausted.

**The worst outcome of a brainstorming session is not generating too few ideas — it is generating the right number of ideas too quickly, before the obvious ones are exhausted. Genuinely novel ideas live on the far side of the obvious. Effective facilitation keeps the session running past the point of comfort, specifically because the ideas that emerge after the group thinks it's 'done' are disproportionately valuable.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** When a group declares 'we've run out of ideas,' treat it as a signal to keep going, not to stop. The first ideas are usually the most obvious — everyone thought of them before the session. The breakthrough ideas come when the obvious territory is fully mined and the group is forced into new cognitive territory.

**Convergence — the act of narrowing, selecting, and prioritizing ideas — is a legitimate and necessary phase, but it must be temporally separated from divergence. Organizations that are good at convergence and poor at divergence don't just produce fewer ideas; they systematically select from a shallower and more conventional pool. The quality of convergence is entirely dependent on the quantity and diversity of what divergence produced.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

**Implication:** Explicitly schedule separate sessions for divergence and convergence — never in the same meeting. Giving both phases their own time signals to participants that generation is valued as much as decision-making, and prevents the cognitive gear-switching that undermines both activities when conflated.

**Most innovation bottlenecks are upstream of evaluation, not inside it.** Organizations build sophisticated frameworks for deciding which ideas to pursue but invest almost nothing in the systems that generate ideas in the first place. A brilliant selection process applied to a thin idea pool still produces mediocre output. The leverage is in expanding the pool, not refining the filter. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

> *"Most companies have a generation problem, not an evaluation problem."*

**Implication:** Audit where your organization's innovation investment goes. If most of your process, tooling, and meeting time is dedicated to evaluating and selecting ideas rather than generating them, you have inverted the priority. Shift at least equal resources to the front of the funnel.

**Creativity is not a personality trait that some people have and others lack — it is a skill developed through deliberate practice, and brainstorming sessions are practice environments. Every time a team generates ideas without judgment, they are training neural pathways that make subsequent ideation easier and less threatening. The long-term value of a brainstorm is not just the ideas produced but the creative muscle built.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters, Perry and Utley (2022)))

> *"Creativity is a skill, not a talent."*

**Implication:** Treat regular brainstorming sessions as training, not just output events. Even when a session produces no immediately usable ideas, it has value if it increases participants' creative confidence and ideaflow velocity. Track participation trends over time — growing engagement is a signal that creative culture is building.

**Premature judgment is the idea killer.** Evaluation and generation are cognitively incompatible activities — when they happen simultaneously, which is what most brainstorming sessions do, evaluation always wins. Protecting the generative phase from critical thinking is the most important facilitation discipline in innovation. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Separate ideation sessions from evaluation sessions with a hard structural break — different time blocks, different facilitators if possible, different explicit norms. Never let critique enter the room during generation.

**Information transfer cannot produce imagination.** You cannot teach someone to see AI as a sparring partner through a slide deck — they have to witness it or experience it first. Skills follow imagination; imagination cannot be delivered through conventional instruction. ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"You can't information-transfer your way to imagination. You can't teach someone to see AI as a sparring partner through a slide deck. They have to witness it. They have to experience it."*

**Implication:** AI training program designers should invert the typical sequence: spark imagination before building skills, lead with live demonstration over explanation, and build in hands-on practice during sessions rather than assigning it as homework.

**Utley uses a simple two-part audience question to expose the gap between claimed fatigue and actual AI practice: asking who feels AI fatigue (all hands up) and then who can demonstrate radical use cases (all hands down). This contrast makes the real problem visible in seconds without argument.** ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"That's kind of ironic, right? We're all exhausted from talking about AI... but none of us are actually doing anything with it."*

**Implication:** Facilitators and leaders can use this diagnostic question in any room to surface the gap between AI rhetoric and AI practice — turning abstract 'fatigue' into a concrete, honest conversation.

**Utley uses a 'suspension of disbelief demo' to give leaders visceral, firsthand experience of AI capability — live, meaningful work product created before their eyes using their own strategic questions, not slides or theory. This approach bypasses intellectual resistance and replaces it with direct experience. It works because imagination doesn't expand from reports; it expands from doing.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"No slides. No theory. Just live, meaningful work product created before their eyes: nuanced analysis of their own strategic questions, deep research reports, presentation decks, even an AI-generated podcast summarizing their competitive landscape. All built in minutes, not months."*

**Implication:** When trying to shift leaders' mental models about AI, skip the presentations and go straight to demonstration using their own real problems. Visceral experience changes mental models; abstract information rarely does.

**There are moments that call for expansive, divergent thinking ('flare') and moments that require convergent focus.** Knowing which mode is appropriate — and being able to shift between them — is a core creative competency. Conflating the two modes undermines both ideation and execution. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck))

> *"There's times we need to flare, and there's times we need to focus."*

**Implication:** Facilitators, leaders, and individual creators should explicitly signal which mode is active at any given moment. Mixing evaluation with generation kills idea quantity; mixing divergence with deadline execution kills follow-through.

**A useful meta-language for creative work is simply asking.** are we diverging or converging right now? This simple distinction changes how participants should show up — converging calls for critical judgment, while diverging calls for generative association. Knowing which mode you're in prevents the common mistake of applying the wrong mindset at the wrong moment. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders))

> *"One way, one very simple kind of language we'll use is just, are we focusing or are we flaring? Are we converging? Are we diverging? And that helps me know who I should be when I show up. If I'm converging, simple example, I'm going to be more critical. I'm going to be more judgmental... If I'm diverging from trying to generate possibilities, I'm not going to say, what do I think of this idea? I'm going to say, what does this idea make me think of?"*

**Implication:** Facilitators and team leads should explicitly name the mode of thinking at the start of any creative session — this single habit prevents the idea-killing dynamic of premature evaluation.

**Forced association between unrelated objects and a focal problem — asking 'how would a fire do this?' or 'what does a scooter have to do with this?' — rapidly generates novel solution angles. Random stimuli act as cognitive triggers that bypass habitual thinking patterns.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"I see a scooter — what does a scooter have to do with getting people excited? Oh, like I start on a hill — my kids always go on a hill to scoot. I see a basketball goal — what does a basketball goal have to do with it? Oh, there's a pass. All of these things are kind of sparking my thinking."*

**Implication:** Structured randomness — deliberately asking what any observed object has to do with your problem — is a practical, repeatable tool for escaping cognitive fixation and discovering unexpected solution paths.

**A simple linguistic flip transforms evaluative reactions into generative ones.** replacing 'what do I think of this idea?' with 'what does this idea make me think of?' instantly shifts the listener from critical mode to generative mode, unlocking associative thinking rather than triggering judgment. ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"When someone brings a new idea, instead of thinking 'what do I think of it?' ask 'what does it make me think of?' — 'what do I think of it' is a critical posture. 'What does it make me think of' — I'm in generative mode. I'm not criticizing."*

**Implication:** Facilitators and leaders can immediately improve idea generation in meetings by training participants to respond to new ideas with associative questions rather than evaluative judgments.

**Ideating individually before consulting AI (the 'I' in FIXIT) is a critical step because it prevents AI from short-circuiting the human creative process. Bringing your own thinking to the collaboration first means AI expands and challenges your ideas rather than replacing them.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"I stands for ideate individually before you go to AI."*

**Implication:** Teams should establish a norm of individual idea generation before opening any AI tool — this protects cognitive diversity and ensures humans are genuinely collaborating with AI rather than outsourcing their thinking to it.

**The practical instruction for idea generation should shift from 'think of something new' to 'think of connections.' The Lego metaphor — gathering conceptual pieces and assembling them in unexpected ways — is both more accurate to how creativity works and more actionable as a creative directive.** ([source](youtube:stop-trying-to-be-original-jeremy-utley))

> *"Anytime you're trying to come up with a new idea, stop it. Think of connections. Try to gather Legos and put them together in unexpected ways. It's way less pressure and way more effective for your creativity."*

**Implication:** Facilitators, leaders, and individuals should replace 'be creative' or 'think of something original' with prompts that direct attention toward combination and connection — this produces better results with less anxiety.

**The 'Wonder Wander' is a solo brainstorming technique in which you take a problem into the physical world and walk around, deliberately entertaining unexpected connections between what you observe and the challenge you're working on. It is a structured way to escape mental ruts by colliding your thinking with real-world stimuli.** ([source](youtube:brainstorming-alone-jeremy-utley))

> *"It's basically a way to spark fresh thinking by taking a problem in hand or in mind as it were and walking around and entertaining the possibility of unexpected connections and the relevance of the unexpected to the problem that you're working on."*

**Implication:** Anyone stuck on a problem can generate new ideas not by thinking harder in isolation, but by physically moving through the world and treating every observed object as a potential creative provocation.

**Physical objects in the environment can serve as 'constraints' or provocations that, when deliberately collided with a problem, generate unexpected solution directions. Jeremy illustrates this with examples: a playground suggests showcasing products, a BMW suggests customizable options packages, a stoplight suggests scarcity alerts, and a nail salon suggests checkout upsell options.** ([source](youtube:brainstorming-alone-jeremy-utley))

> *"A stop light what would a stoplight tell me — maybe we give people a yellow light before a product runs out. A nail salon — you can have different options by the checkout. All of these are simply provocations where I take a constraint in this case something that's in the physical world and collide it with the problem that I'm working on."*

**Implication:** Any random object observed during a walk can be systematically pressed into service as a creative provocation — this transforms aimless walking into a structured divergent thinking exercise.

**The core challenge of solo brainstorming is that when you are alone with your thoughts, you tend to recirculate the same familiar ideas. Recognizing that ideas are connections — not revelations — reframes the solution: instead of thinking harder, you need to introduce more and more varied inputs to collide with.** ([source](youtube:brainstorming-alone-jeremy-utley))

> *"The challenge of brainstorming by myself is I'm here, I'm alone with my thoughts and what do I do about it. When you recognize that really an idea is just a connection and creativity is the collision of apparently unrelated frames of reference, then what you can do is say I'm going to go collide my brain with lots of conceptual inputs and see what happens."*

**Implication:** Solo ideation stagnates not from lack of intelligence but from lack of novel inputs — the fix is environmental and behavioral, not cognitive.

**Reason, while powerful and essential, can act as a tyrannical filter that kills ideas before they are fully formed by demanding immediate justification. Original thinking requires a temporary suspension of the evaluative, rational mind to allow novel connections to surface.** ([source](youtube:brainstorming-alone-jeremy-utley))

> *"One of the ways you can escape the tyranny of reason — if you accept the premise that original thinking requires that you let go of reason — one of the ways that you can escape the tyranny of reason is by taking a Wonder wander."*

**Implication:** Effective creative practice must deliberately separate the generative phase from the evaluative phase — using tools like the Wonder Wander to create space where reason is temporarily held at bay.

---

## Leadership & Enabling Innovation

**The reason most boards cannot imagine the future is not that they lack intelligence — it is that they are working from an insufficiently reframed problem set. Boards ask operational questions about known futures rather than generative questions about possible ones. The framing of board-level questions determines the strategic imagination that is possible.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** If you are preparing materials for board or leadership reviews, rewrite the key questions from operational frames to generative ones. Replace 'How do we grow 15%?' with 'What would need to be true for us to grow ten times?' The question redesign is the strategy upgrade.

**Boards and senior leadership teams are structurally positioned to be the least imaginative people in the organization.** Their distance from customers and operations, combined with their heavy investment in the status quo, makes them reliable defenders of the present rather than architects of the future. Organizations that route all strategic imagination through the board are systematically filtering out the future. ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Your board can't imagine the future."*

**Implication:** Bring generative voices from outside the executive suite into strategic conversations earlier. Customer-facing employees, younger team members, and cross-functional contributors often hold more vivid images of emerging possibilities than the people formally charged with strategy.

**AI adoption in organizations is not primarily a technology problem — it is a leadership problem.** The gap between organizations that successfully integrate AI and those that stall is not access to tools but leadership willingness to model experimentation, tolerate early awkwardness, and create structural space for teams to develop new practices. The 'doing gap' is a leadership variable. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI."*

**Implication:** If AI adoption is stalling on your team, look first at what leaders are doing, not what employees are doing. Are senior leaders publicly experimenting with AI? Are they sharing their own learning curves? Visible leadership experimentation unlocks team permission faster than any training program.

**The 'imagination ceiling' in organizations is set by what leaders can personally envision, not by what the technology enables or the market demands. When leadership imagination is constrained by existing mental models, the organization's creative range is bounded by those models regardless of the talent, tools, or resources available beneath them.** ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI."*

**Implication:** Leaders who want to raise their organization's creative ceiling must first expand their own imaginative range — through cross-disciplinary input, exposure to adjacent industries, and deliberate practice with generative tools. You cannot lead people to a place you cannot imagine.

**The role of the leader in AI-augmented organizations is shifting from knowledge authority to creative catalyst.** As AI handles more execution, synthesis, and even analysis, the scarce human contribution becomes insight, framing, taste, and the ability to ask questions that machines cannot generate independently. Leaders who define their value by what they know are in a structurally deteriorating position. ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Begin redefining your leadership identity around question quality and creative direction rather than domain expertise and decision authority. The leaders who thrive in AI-augmented organizations will be those who make their teams think better — not those who have the most answers.

**Leaders who believe their job is to have the best ideas become the bottleneck.** When the team expects the leader to generate the answers, they stop generating themselves. Distributing ideation responsibility across the organization is not a loss of authority — it is a multiplication of creative capacity. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

> *"The leader's job is not to have the best ideas — it's to create the conditions where the best ideas can emerge."*

**Implication:** If you are the best idea-generator on your team, that is a structural failure, not a personal achievement. Redesign your role from idea-provider to idea-environment architect.

**Most leaders invest heavily in frameworks for evaluating ideas and almost nothing in systems that generate them.** This is a misdiagnosis of the innovation bottleneck. The problem is almost never that companies lack the judgment to pick good ideas — it is that not enough ideas are entering the funnel in the first place. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

> *"Most companies have a generation problem, not an evaluation problem."*

**Implication:** Audit your innovation budget. If the majority goes to stage-gate processes, evaluation criteria, and portfolio reviews, you are optimizing the wrong end of the funnel. The upstream investment — in ideation habits, input diversity, and question design — is where the leverage lives.

**The brainstorming session is not a neutral tool — its conventional design is actively hostile to novel ideas.** Social dominance, simultaneous evaluation, and convergence pressure ensure that the most politically safe ideas win rather than the most creative ones. When innovation sessions consistently produce mediocre output, the session format is the suspect, not the people in the room. ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer Podcast))

> *"Your brainstorming sessions are the problem, not your people."*

**Implication:** Stop diagnosing your team as uncreative and start diagnosing your session design. Separate divergent and convergent phases, use silent individual generation before group sharing, and explicitly forbid evaluation during ideation. The format is the intervention.

**Organizations under-innovate not because they lack creative people but because those people work in systems that suppress ideation. This distinction is critical: the problem is environmental, not individual. Hiring more creative talent into a creativity-suppressing system produces the same output — it just costs more.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

> *"You don't need more talented people — you need a better idea environment."*

**Implication:** Before your next creative hire, map the environment that person will enter. If the system has been filtering out creative contribution from your current team, a new hire will face the same filter. Fix the environment first.

**The belief that creativity is a fixed talent — that some people have it and others do not — is the most pervasive and damaging idea in organizational life. When leaders hold this belief, they exempt most of their team from creative contribution and concentrate ideation in a small group of designated 'creatives.' This is both empirically false and organizationally catastrophic.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders Podcast))

> *"Not being creative is a learned behavior, not a natural state."*

**Implication:** Audit which people on your team you have — consciously or unconsciously — categorized as 'not creative.' What systems or signals put them in that category? Recovering those contributors is one of the highest-return investments a leader can make.

**Leaders who wait for a crisis to invest in creative capacity will always be too late.** Ideaflow — like physical fitness — cannot be developed on demand. Organizations that build generative habits during stable periods have a reservoir to draw from when disruption arrives; organizations that neglect it have nothing in reserve. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck Podcast))

**Implication:** Don't wait for a competitive threat or a strategic pivot to build your team's creative capacity. The time to train the muscle is before you need to lift the weight. Start measuring and building ideaflow now, as an operational discipline.

**The quality obsession applied too early in the creative process is a creativity killer disguised as high standards.** When leaders demand polished, defensible ideas before generation is complete, they eliminate the rough, weird, and unconventional ideas that are the statistical source of breakthroughs. Quality standards belong at the evaluation phase — not at the generation phase. ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer Podcast))

> *"Quality standards kill innovation before it starts."*

**Implication:** Explicitly communicate to your team that early-phase ideation is a quality-free zone. The standard in generation is volume and variety, not polish. Elevate quality standards only when you move into evaluation — and make that phase transition visible and deliberate.

**Expertise, while valuable for execution, is a liability for innovation if it becomes the only lens through which leaders see problems. The deeper the expertise, the more constrained the idea space — because expert pattern-matching filters out solutions that don't fit existing domain vocabulary. Leaders must deliberately counter their own expertise with cross-disciplinary exposure.** ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

> *"Your expertise is narrowing your creative range."*

**Implication:** Build deliberate exposure to industries and disciplines outside your domain into your leadership practice — not as a curiosity, but as corrective medicine. The executive who reads widely, talks to people outside their sector, and studies analogous problems from other fields is expanding the idea space available to their entire organization.

**Leaders who model creative risk-taking — who share early-stage, unpolished ideas publicly, who say 'I don't know' and then generate hypotheses — give their teams permission to do the same. Modeling is the most powerful facilitation tool a leader has, and most leaders never use it intentionally.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** In your next team meeting, share one idea before it is ready. Name it as rough. Watch what happens to the room. Leadership modeling of creative vulnerability is contagious in a way that no creative training program can replicate.

**The leader is the single biggest variable in whether a team feels safe to generate and share ideas.** Leaders who dominate ideation — always having the loudest opinion, always evaluating first — inadvertently train their teams to stop contributing. The leader's real job is environmental design: building the conditions where the best ideas can surface from anywhere. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Before blaming your team for uninspired output, audit your own behavior in meetings. Are you the first to evaluate? Do you signal which answers are acceptable before the question is fully explored? Your habits are the ceiling on your team's creative output.

**Psychological safety is not a soft cultural nicety — it is the hard infrastructure of innovation.** Teams that do not feel safe to share half-formed, potentially wrong ideas will self-censor at precisely the moment creative contribution matters most. The cost of a psychologically unsafe environment is measured in the ideas that were never voiced. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Measure psychological safety as an innovation input, not just an HR metric. If your team only shares polished, pre-approved ideas in meetings, you have an invisible tax on your ideaflow that no talent upgrade will fix.

**Leaders set the reward signals that determine which behaviors multiply in an organization.** If speed and certainty are rewarded and creative risk-taking is penalized, rational employees will optimize for speed and certainty. Innovation culture is not built with posters and workshops — it is built by what leaders visibly celebrate and what they visibly tolerate when it fails. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Look at the last five times something failed on your team. What signal did your response send? Did it communicate that trying and learning was safe, or that safety came from not trying? Your failure response is your culture.

**Premature judgment is the idea killer in organizational settings.** When leaders react evaluatively to early-stage ideas — even with well-intentioned critical questions — they train contributors to pre-filter their ideas before sharing them. The result is that only the safest, most defensible ideas surface, which are rarely the most innovative ones. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Establish an explicit 'generative zone' in your team's creative process — a protected phase where evaluation is suspended and the only response to an idea is 'yes, and' or 'what else?' Naming the rule makes it enforceable.

**Meeting structure is innovation infrastructure.** The default meeting format — agenda, presentation, discussion, decision — is designed for convergence and execution, not for generation. Leaders who want more creative output need to design separate meeting modes with explicit generative purposes, different facilitation rules, and no pressure to reach consensus. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Create a category in your calendar called 'generative meetings' and treat them differently from decision meetings. Different room setup, different facilitation rules, different output expectation. A meeting designed to decide cannot also be designed to discover.

**Ideaflow — the volume and velocity of ideas a team generates per unit of time — is a leading indicator of organizational innovation health. Revenue, launches, and patents are lagging indicators; by the time they decline, the upstream problem has been present for years. Leaders who want to manage innovation proactively need to measure ideaflow, not just outcomes.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

> *"Ideaflow is the only innovation metric that matters."*

**Implication:** Add a generative metric to your team's health dashboard. How many ideas did your team generate this week? This quarter? If you cannot answer that question, you are flying blind on your most important creative input.

**Leaders are often the last to know that their teams have stopped generating ideas, because the suppression mechanism is invisible. Teams don't announce that they've stopped trying — they simply bring fewer and fewer rough ideas forward until only the safe ones remain. By the time the leader notices the creative drought, the culture has been set for years.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Run an ideaflow audit with your team: ask them directly what kinds of ideas they hold back, and why. The answers will reveal which of your behaviors, reactions, or structural signals are functioning as creative suppressants — often unintentionally.

**The problem framing phase is the highest-leverage moment in any innovation process, and it is almost always underinvested. Teams that rush from problem to solution are often solving the wrong problem brilliantly. A leader's ability to slow the team down at the framing stage — to sit with the question, reframe it from multiple angles, and resist premature solution generation — is one of the most valuable creative skills in leadership.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Build a mandatory reframing step into your team's problem-solving process. Before any solution generation begins, require at least three distinct reframings of the problem. 'How might we increase sales?' is not the same problem as 'How might we make customers feel understood?' The reframe changes everything downstream.

**Creative confidence is not an individual trait that some people are born with — it is a cultural variable that leadership can build or destroy. Teams whose leaders celebrate attempts, treat failure as information, and separate volume from quality develop creative confidence collectively. Teams whose leaders evaluate early and reward certainty develop creative learned helplessness.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Track the ratio of idea attempts to idea approvals on your team. If the only ideas that get celebrated are the ones that succeed, you are inadvertently training your team to attempt less. Find ways to celebrate the attempt itself, independent of outcome.

**The leader who asks better questions unlocks more creative capacity than the leader who provides better answers.** Question design — the discipline of crafting prompts that open exploration rather than close it — is among the most underrated leadership skills. A well-framed 'How might we?' question can generate more valuable output in thirty minutes than a month of solution-focused meetings. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Before your next team meeting, spend ten minutes redesigning your agenda questions. Replace 'What should we do about X?' with 'How might we reimagine X?' or 'What if the opposite of our current approach were true?' The quality of questions determines the quality of thinking that follows.

**Innovation is not primarily a function of individual genius — it is a function of organizational conditions.** The most creative teams in history were not collections of exceptional individuals operating independently; they were people working inside systems designed to maximize idea exposure, reduce friction to contribution, and reward generative behavior. The leader's leverage is in designing those systems. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Stop looking for your innovation breakthrough in a single person or a single idea. Start looking at the structure: How many ideas are being generated? How diverse are the inputs feeding them? How fast are ideas being tested? Redesign the system, not the personnel.

**Utley acknowledges his own culpability in the dynamic he critiques — by profiling Josh Wöhle as 'the first 100x human I know,' he implicitly set a destination for readers. He models intellectual honesty by sitting with the critique rather than dismissing it, and by naming that even well-intentioned advocates inadvertently impose a benchmark.** ([source](Don't Use AI. Work With It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Here's what I realized re-reading both Susanne's email reply and Alix Harrow's Instagram comment: people aren't rejecting AI. They're rejecting the assumption that someone else has set the bar. (Including, frankly, when I do it. Which I just did.)"*

**Implication:** Thought leaders on AI should regularly audit their own framing for hidden benchmarks. Naming 'this is what good looks like' — even with admiration rather than prescription — can function as coercion for audiences who don't share that vision.

**Jeremy frames the emerging productivity gap between augmented and unaugmented operators as existential, not merely incremental. He argues the gap widens every week because the augmented operator's memory and context compound while the unassisted operator's does not.** ([source](This Is What Real Augmentation Looks Like — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The opportunity cost of being an unassisted human is now much higher than ever. The gap between an unassisted operator and a Josh-style operator isn't a rounding error anymore. It's a chasm — and it widens every week, because every week the augmented operator's memory gets bigger."*

**Implication:** Delayed adoption of deep AI integration is not a neutral choice — it is a compounding disadvantage. The longer leaders wait to build context-rich systems, the larger the gap they will need to close against those who started earlier.

**The question 'Have you tried AI?' is a simple but powerful leadership tool that simultaneously plants ideas, grants permission, and opens a feedback loop. When leaders ask it regularly, they signal that AI use is acceptable and valued, and they accumulate stories of effective use that can be shared more broadly.** ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Three things happen when you do. First, you give them the idea—often they haven't even thought to try AI for that task. Second, you grant permission—your question implies it's okay. Third, you earn the follow-up: 'How did AI help?'—which stocks your reservoir of stories to celebrate."*

**Implication:** Leaders should make 'Have you tried AI?' a habitual question in one-on-ones, project check-ins, and team meetings. The compounding effect of permission-granting and story-collecting creates a culture where AI experimentation is normalized.

**Leaders unlock AI adoption most effectively by modeling in public — demonstrating their own AI use, including iterative and exploratory conversations, in front of their teams. This visibility is more powerful than any training program because it directly expands what people believe is possible.** ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Your people need to see you using AI—not just hear you talk about it. Show them what a sparring partner conversation looks like. Let them watch you iterate. Make the invisible visible. (And yes, that means being willing to look dumb in front of your team.)"*

**Implication:** Executives and managers who want to accelerate AI adoption should treat public modeling as a core leadership responsibility — not just championing AI in speeches, but visibly using it, fumbling with it, and iterating on it in shared spaces.

**The AI adoption challenge is not a training problem — it is an imagination problem.** Leaders responsible for AI adoption are not in the training business; they are in the imagination business. The goal is expanding what people can envision doing with AI before equipping them with the skills to do it. ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"You're not in the training business. You're in the imagination business."*

**Implication:** This reframe has practical consequences for how AI programs are structured, resourced, and measured. The first investment should be in exposure and inspiration — case studies, live demos, storytelling — before moving to skill-building.

**There is a 22-point gap between leaders who believe AI will impact jobs (89%) and those who say it is currently impacting work at their organizations (67%). This is not a technology problem but a leadership problem — the failure to translate belief into action is the defining crisis of the current AI moment.** ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"That's a 22-point gap between belief and action—between knowing something matters and actually doing something about it. This isn't a technology problem. It's a leadership problem."*

**Implication:** Leaders must audit their own behavior, not just their beliefs. Conviction without action is indistinguishable from indifference — and the organizations closing this gap will outpace those merely nodding along.

**At a keynote, Jeremy observed that the same audience that raised their hands for 'AI fatigue' lowered them all when asked who had radical use cases to share. This irony — exhaustion with the conversation paired with absence of actual practice — reveals that AI fatigue is often a symptom of non-use, not overuse.** ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"We're all tired of talking about it, yet none of us are doing it."*

**Implication:** What gets labeled 'AI fatigue' in organizations is frequently AI avoidance dressed up as weariness. Leaders should reframe fatigue as a diagnostic signal: if people are tired of the topic but not doing the work, the problem is adoption, not saturation.

**Leaders must model AI use visibly to catalyze team adoption. A practical technique.** when asked a question in a meeting, open ChatGPT on the shared screen and work through the answer collaboratively, making AI thinking visible to the whole room. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"When someone asks you a question in a meeting, try this: pull up ChatGPT on the shared screen and say, 'Before providing an answer, please ask me any questions you need to better understand this situation.' Then work through the answer together. Make your AI thinking visible."*

**Implication:** Behavior modeling by leaders is one of the highest-leverage levers for AI adoption. Visible, public use normalizes the practice and gives teams a concrete example of what thoughtful AI engagement looks like.

**Senior leaders at a $10 billion company unanimously admitted they had no defined standard for AI proficiency in their own roles, no company measurement of AI proficiency, and 90% didn't publicly share how they use AI. This reveals a systemic gap: the people responsible for approving AI strategy have no personal benchmark for what 'good' looks like. Governance without personal fluency is governance by assumption.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"A room full of senior leaders—people responsible for setting strategy, approving multimillion-dollar investments, holding the CEO accountable—and not one of them could articulate what 'good' looks like for AI in their own work."*

**Implication:** Organizations cannot set credible AI strategy if the people approving that strategy have never personally defined — let alone achieved — AI proficiency in their own work. Leaders must establish personal proficiency standards before they can evaluate anyone else's.

**Boards are structurally expected to push companies toward bold ambition — but that accountability requires imagination, and imagination is constrained by personal experience. A board member who has never done AI work firsthand cannot recognize when a CEO's vision is too small or a timeline too conservative. The result is rubber-stamped mediocrity dressed as governance.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Accountability requires imagination. And imagination is constrained by experience."*

**Implication:** If boards lack visceral AI experience, they will consistently approve under-ambitious strategies without realizing it. Boards must treat hands-on AI exposure as a prerequisite for fiduciary competence, not a nice-to-have.

**Utley borrows Simon Sinek's framing to define the core difference between startups and enterprises.** a startup's ambitions exceed its grasp, while an enterprise's ambitions lie within its reach. In the AI era, this distinction has become a competitive liability. Enterprises that set only reachable goals are structurally incapable of the transformative change AI enables. ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The difference between a startup and an enterprise is that a startup's ambitions exceed its grasp, while an enterprise's ambitions lie within its reach."*

**Implication:** Enterprise leaders must deliberately engineer ambition that exceeds current capability — especially in AI — or they will keep optimizing the status quo while more ambitious competitors reshape the market.

**Ethan Mollick's observation — that most CEOs delegate AI to committees that report back in three months, which then launch RFP processes to hire consultants — captures how organizations create the illusion of AI action without any real learning. If CEOs aren't personally using AI, boards are even further removed. Delegation without personal practice produces strategy theater, not transformation.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I talk to CEOs and high-level executives all the time, and none of them are using AI themselves. They've all delegated to a committee that will be reporting back in three months, which will then start a process of looking for an RFP to hire a consultant who will do an initial analysis."*

**Implication:** AI strategy cannot be fully delegated. Leaders who outsource their AI learning to committees or consultants will lack the judgment to evaluate what they receive back — and will mistake process activity for strategic progress.

**McKinsey data shows only ~6% of organizations see meaningful bottom-line AI impact, and what separates them is ambition — high performers are three times more likely to pursue transformative AI goals rather than efficiency gains. Organizations chasing cost reduction get cost reduction; organizations chasing transformation get growth, innovation, and market share. The strategic choice of what to aim for determines what you get.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The organizations chasing cost reduction are getting cost reduction. The organizations chasing transformation are getting growth, innovation, competitive differentiation, and market share."*

**Implication:** The AI ambition level leaders set is itself a strategic choice with asymmetric returns. Leaders must explicitly ask whether their AI agenda is aimed at efficiency or transformation — and recognize that only one of those paths leads to competitive differentiation.

**Melissa Cheals, CEO of Smartly in New Zealand, was able to reject a million-dollar, year-long engineering proposal after personally doing AI building work — replacing it with a two-week hackathon approach. Her personal hands-on experience gave her the judgment to distinguish an outdated proposal from a genuinely ambitious one. Without that experience, her board would have approved the original proposal without question.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"She could do that because she knew. She'd done the work herself."*

**Implication:** Personal AI experience is not just enriching — it is a prerequisite for leadership judgment. CEOs and board members who haven't done the work cannot evaluate proposals, timelines, or strategies with any real discernment.

**Utley argues that fiduciary duty has expanded — from financial oversight, to strategy and risk, and now to imagination.** A board member who cannot imagine what is possible with AI cannot recognize when leadership is thinking too small, approve appropriate timelines, or distinguish genuine AI strategy from AI theater. Governance without imagination is incomplete governance. ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Fiduciary duty now includes imagination... If not, you're not qualified to oversee those who can."*

**Implication:** Boards should add AI fluency and imaginative capacity to their governance competency frameworks. The question is no longer just whether the AI strategy is sound — it's whether the board has the direct experience to evaluate that soundness at all.

**Utley draws a sharp diagnostic distinction.** if the problem is genuine fatigue, rest is the cure; if the problem is fear-based insecurity, rest makes things worse. Misdiagnosing the emotion leads to the wrong prescription and deeper avoidance over time. ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Because if you're fatigued, the prescription is rest. But if you're scared, rest won't help. The only cure for AI insecurity is AI fluency."*

**Implication:** Before prescribing any solution to AI resistance — whether a break, a new framing, or a training program — leaders must first accurately diagnose whether they're dealing with overload or fear.

**Utley observes that nobody reports fatigue about things they're genuinely good at or winning at — fatigue as an emotion signals a gap between expectation and capability, not actual overexposure. This reframes fatigue as a useful diagnostic signal rather than a settled state.** ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Have you ever felt 'fatigued' by something you're actually good at? Something you're winning at?"*

**Implication:** When a team or individual expresses fatigue about a topic or tool, leaders should treat it as a capability gap indicator and ask what skill-building would close it, rather than reducing exposure.

**Brad Anderson, president of Qualtrics, argues that companies wanting to lead in the AI age need every employee using AI every single day — every employee, every function, every day. This reframes AI adoption as a universal organizational discipline, not a function of specific roles.** ([source](It's a Skill, Not a Pill — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"If a company wants to lead in the AI age, every employee has to use AI every single day. Every employee. Every function. Every day."*

**Implication:** AI strategy should not be confined to technical teams or innovation labs. Executives should set explicit universal daily-use expectations and build the cultural infrastructure to support them across all functions.

**Jeremy draws on Clay Christensen's HBR insight that high achievers systematically over-invest in careers and under-invest in families because of asymmetric feedback loops. Work provides fast, concrete feedback — shipped products, closed deals, promotions. Family and relationships have decades-long feedback loops, and the resilience of loved ones who give the benefit of the doubt actually enables neglect to go undetected longer.** ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"When people who have a high need for achievement have an extra half hour of time or an extra ounce of energy, they'll unconsciously allocate it to activities that yield the most tangible accomplishments. And our careers provide the most concrete evidence that we're moving forward."*

**Implication:** The absence of a dashboard for being a spouse or parent is not just inconvenient — it is structurally dangerous. Leaders must create artificial feedback mechanisms for the relationships and activities that matter most, because the natural feedback loop is too slow to correct behavior in real time.

**Jeremy received over twenty confessional responses from founders, executives, and AI-forward leaders after his previous essay — all describing the same pull away from family and presence. These were not undisciplined people; they were among the most intentional professionals he knows. This response led Jeremy to conclude that if willpower were the answer, these people would have already solved it — and therefore the diagnosis of a discipline problem was wrong.** ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The thing that struck me is, these aren't undisciplined people. These are some of the most intentional, self-aware professionals I know. If willpower were the answer, they'd have solved this already. Which means I missed something last week. I diagnosed the right problem — but I've come to realize that I offered a discipline solution to what is actually an economics problem."*

**Implication:** When high-performing, self-aware people share the same struggle at scale, it signals a systemic issue rather than individual failure. Leaders should resist the instinct to address collective behavioral patterns with individual character prescriptions — the pattern itself is evidence that the incentive structure needs redesign.

**Jeremy applies Stanford Business School's famous application question — 'What matters most to you, and why?' — as a personal accountability test in the AI era. The question is powerful not because there is a right answer, but because it forces alignment between values and behavior. He argues that AI productivity gains make this question more urgent, not less.** ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Stanford Business School has a famous application question: What matters most to you, and why? It's a brutal question (in the best way). Not because there's a right answer. Because it forces alignment. Now layer AI on top of that. If AI gives us massive productivity gains, then we have to ask: What are those gains for?"*

**Implication:** Leaders adopting AI should answer the Stanford question first — before optimizing workflows — so that productivity gains serve stated values rather than default to more output.

**Jeremy poses a single governing question for the AI era.** 'Whatever you value most... are you now more of it?' This question functions as a recurring integrity test that cuts through productivity metrics, tool adoption rates, and output volume to ask what actually changed in a person's life. ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Whatever you value most... are you now more of it?"*

**Implication:** This question is a simple but powerful filter for evaluating any technology adoption. Leaders can apply it at team and organizational levels: does AI make us more of what we say we are as a company, or just faster at what we were already doing?

**For senior professionals, staying indispensable in the AI era requires being three problems ahead with AI-powered proofs already in hand — not waiting for more autonomous agents. The strategy is to use AI to accelerate execution on self-identified problems before others have even named them.** ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"While others wait for 'more autonomous agents,' you're already three problems ahead, with AI-powered proofs in hand."*
