**Implication:** Organizations investing in AI tools should focus as much on behavioral and cultural conditioning as on tooling and training — the default to solo work is the real adoption blocker.

**Activating AI team capacity doesn't require an elaborate workflow or a free hour — it requires five minutes of clarity.** Jeremy nearly arrived empty-handed to a weekly learning cohort meeting, then opened two Claude Code windows five minutes before the call. By the time the meeting ended, he had five proposed article directions with supporting evidence pulled from his own conversations. ([source](I Have a Team (A Collection of Wherewithal Moments) — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"You don't need an hour. You need five minutes of clarity. Not time to build some elaborate workflow. Just five minutes where you remember: I'm not an individual contributor. I have a team."*

**Implication:** The activation energy for AI leverage is lower than most people assume — the constraint is not time or skill but the five-minute decision to delegate before defaulting to solo effort.

**Jeremy used Claude Code with sub-agents to generate 90 pages of research per chapter for his new book — academic studies, real-world examples, counterarguments, and data — in a fraction of the time his first book required. He had been stuck for weeks not because the technology was unavailable, but because he hadn't thought to ask.** ([source](I Have a Team (A Collection of Wherewithal Moments) — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I ended up with 90 pages of research per chapter. Academic studies. Real-world examples. Counterarguments. Data I never would have found on my own — and certainly not in the time I had. I'd been stuck for weeks. Not because the technology wasn't available. Because I hadn't thought to ask."*

**Implication:** AI agents can dramatically compress research timelines for knowledge workers — but the bottleneck is prompt imagination, not technical capability. The act of asking is the unlock.

**Jeremy reframes the role of the modern knowledge worker from 'individual contributor' to 'manager with a team.' He had been ghosting his PR agent for months due to bandwidth constraints, spending 45 minutes minimum per response — until he delegated the task to Claude in five minutes, getting three on-brand options per question. The realization: 'That's handmade artisanal Jeremy who doesn't have the bandwidth. With AI, Augmented-I DO have bandwidth.'** ([source](I Have a Team (A Collection of Wherewithal Moments) — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"That's 'handmade artisanal Jeremy' who doesn't have the bandwidth. With AI, Augmented-I DO have bandwidth. I'd been operating as an individual contributor when I had a team available."*

**Implication:** Professionals who treat themselves as solo operators are leaving team capacity on the table. Reframing from 'individual contributor' to 'manager with AI agents' is a practical identity shift that unlocks new leverage.

**Jeremy offers a minimal viable prompt for activating AI as a working teammate.** 'I have 45 minutes in a meeting. While I'm gone, I'd like you to [task]. When I come back, have three options ready for me to react to.' The design principle is reaction over creation — returning to options rather than starting from scratch. ([source](I Have a Team (A Collection of Wherewithal Moments) — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"'I have 45 minutes in a meeting. While I'm gone, I'd like you to [describe the task]. When I come back, have three options ready for me to react to.'"*

**Implication:** The 'three options to react to' structure is a repeatable prompt pattern that lowers blank-page friction and leverages the human advantage of evaluation over generation.

**Jeremy frames the question 'What could I have working while I'm away?' as more actionable than 'What could AI do for me?' The first is concrete and time-bound; the second is abstract and paralyzing. The question reorients the user from passive tool consumer to active delegation manager.** ([source](I Have a Team (A Collection of Wherewithal Moments) — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Before your next meeting, ask yourself: 'What could I have working while I'm away?' Not 'What could AI do for me?' — that's too abstract. 'What could I have working while I'm away?'"*

**Implication:** Reframing AI prompts as delegation decisions — tied to real time windows and specific outputs — makes the practice more concrete, habitual, and immediately actionable for busy professionals.

**The single most important variable separating AI outperformers from underperformers is orientation.** outperformers treat AI as a teammate, while underperformers treat it as a tool. This shift in mental model changes every downstream behavior. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"The outperformers had a fundamentally different orientation towards AI than the underperformers did, whereas the underperformers treated AI like a tool. The outperformers treated AI like a teammate, and shifting your orientation from tool to teammate changes everything about the kinds of outcomes that you can achieve working with generative AI."*

**Implication:** Teaching people to adopt a teammate mindset toward AI — before teaching any specific prompting techniques — is the highest-leverage intervention for improving AI collaboration outcomes.

**When AI produces mediocre output, the teammate mindset prescribes coaching and feedback rather than acceptance or abandonment. Just as you would develop a human colleague who delivered insufficient work, you should iterate with AI through dialogue.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"If it's a teammate who's giving you a mediocre result, think about the last teammate who gave you work product that wasn't sufficient. You gave them feedback. You gave them coaching, you gave them mentorship, you helped them improve it."*

**Implication:** AI quality improves dramatically through iterative feedback loops — users who stop at the first output are systematically underutilizing the tool and leaving significant quality gains unrealized.

**Most people position themselves as the question-asker and AI as the answer-giver.** Inverting this — asking AI what questions it needs answered to give you the best response — fundamentally improves the quality of the collaboration. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"The fundamental orientation a lot of people take towards AI is I'm the question asker. AI is the answer giver. But if you think about AI like a teammate, you say, hey, what are ten questions I should ask about this? Or what do you need to know from me in order to get the best response?"*

**Implication:** Letting AI drive the interrogation surfaces context and constraints the user didn't know were relevant, producing outputs that are far better calibrated to the actual need.

**AI can serve as a sophisticated rehearsal partner for difficult interpersonal conversations — building a psychological profile of your conversation partner, roleplaying the scenario, and providing feedback from the other person's perspective.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"You can get an AI to interview you about your conversation partner, and then construct a psychological profile of your conversation partner, and then play the role of your conversation partner in a roleplay, and then give you feedback from the perspective of your conversation partner on how you approach the conversation."*

**Implication:** AI's utility extends far beyond content generation into interpersonal skill development — professionals who discover these 'drill' applications gain capabilities that compound in every relationship and negotiation.

**Capabilities once reserved for heads of state — like having a knowledgeable, context-aware assistant available at all times — are now accessible to anyone with a wifi connection for roughly $20 a month. Utley uses the Churchill bathtub dictation scene as a vivid illustration of what AI now makes universally available. The democratization of intelligence is as significant as the electrification of fans in rural India.** ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"What was once reserved for leaders of nation states is now available to anyone with a wifi connection... What was only available to the very few is now available to the masses. The same is true of intelligence."*

**Implication:** The competitive moat is no longer access to intelligence — it is the willingness and habit of deploying it. Anyone who fails to act on this democratization is voluntarily operating at a disadvantage that was once structural and unavoidable.

**Brice Challamel, former head of AI at Moderna who trained 7,000 employees, reframed his role in his own household by saying 'I'm no longer an individual contributor to my household. I'm a team of five.' He used AI as a note-taker, real-time coach, developmental psychologist, and creative partner across 16 parent-teacher conferences — improving as a parent over the course of a single day through continuous AI-assisted feedback.** ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I'm no longer an individual contributor to my household. I'm a team of five."*

**Implication:** The AI-native mindset applies to personal and family life, not just professional work. Leaders should model and practice multi-agent thinking in all domains — this is how the identity shift from individual contributor to team leader becomes embodied rather than theoretical.

**Bryan McCann, who built Salesforce's AI program before founding You.com, operated under the mantra 'keep the GPUs full' — meaning he and his team constantly thought about what meaningful tasks to assign AI while they were away, sleeping, or in meetings. Fridays were particularly stressful because he had to identify tasks hard enough to occupy nearly unlimited intelligence for 48 hours over the weekend.** ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Keep the GPUs full... Bryan said that Fridays are particularly stressful. On Thursday, he only has to think of a hard enough task for AI to handle for 12 hours until he's back. But on Friday? If he wants a weekend, he has to think of a task hard enough to keep almost unlimited intelligence meaningfully occupied for 48 hours."*

**Implication:** Highly AI-native practitioners experience a new kind of productive stress: the pressure to find tasks worthy of nearly unlimited intelligence. This is a useful diagnostic — if your weekends feel relaxing rather than like a missed deployment opportunity, you likely haven't yet internalized what's available.

**Utley uses 'the pain of idle agents' as a diagnostic test for whether someone has truly grasped what AI makes available.** If you regularly go to bed, go into meetings, or go on vacation without deploying AI to work on your behalf, you haven't yet understood the magnitude of the opportunity — regardless of how much you know about AI. ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"If you don't feel the pain of idle agents — if you regularly go to bed, go to meetings, go on vacation without deploying intelligence to work on your behalf — you haven't grasped what's available to you. Not yet."*

**Implication:** The pain of idle agents is a practical self-assessment tool. Leaders and creators can ask themselves: 'When I step away, is intelligence working for me?' If the answer is consistently no, the problem is not capability — it is habit and identity.

**Utley describes doing his best thinking in the bathtub but being unable to capture that thinking because he can't bring electronics — a frustration that led him to the realization that AI now functions as the context-aware, always-available assistant that was previously only accessible to figures like Churchill. This insight reframes AI not as a productivity tool but as a personal cognitive infrastructure.** ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I have an assistant who has all of my contacts and knows my audience and knows what I said last time and knows my objectives. It's called AI. And what was only available to someone like Winston Churchill a generation ago is now available to all of us for the price of — max — $20 a month."*

**Implication:** Framing AI as personal cognitive infrastructure — rather than a software feature — changes how creators and leaders think about investment, reliance, and integration. It shifts the question from 'should I use this tool?' to 'how do I build a working relationship with this assistant?'

**Utley frames the AI adoption challenge as one of identity before skill.** moving from seeing yourself as an 'individual contributor' to seeing yourself as a 'team leader' is the foundational cognitive shift. Until that identity shift occurs, all the prompting skill and access in the world will go underutilized because the person simply won't think to deploy it. ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I'm not an individual contributor anymore."*

**Implication:** AI training and onboarding programs that focus primarily on skills and tools without addressing identity are likely to underperform. The most leveraged intervention may be helping people viscerally internalize the phrase 'I am not an individual contributor anymore' before teaching them anything else.

**Brice Challamel's parent-teacher conference approach illustrates that AI augmentation compounds within a single session — he became a better parent over the course of 16 meetings because of continuous real-time coaching between each one. This is qualitatively different from using AI to produce a final output; it represents AI as a live feedback loop that accelerates in-session learning.** ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"He said he became a better parent over the course of those 16 meetings because of the continuous coaching."*

**Implication:** The most underexplored use of AI may not be output generation but real-time performance coaching. Leaders, salespeople, teachers, and parents can use AI as a between-rep coach — compressing the feedback loop that normally takes weeks or years into a single afternoon.

**AI's fundamental limitation is that it never initiates — it only responds when asked.** Sam Altman confirmed this directly: AI systems 'don't do anything unless you ask' and have no 'sense of agency or autonomy.' In an age of increasingly capable AI, the humans who win will be the ones who ask first and act without being prompted. ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"They don't do anything unless you ask. They're just sitting there waiting. They don't have a sense of agency or autonomy…"*

**Implication:** Human initiative — the act of noticing and acting before being asked — is structurally impossible for AI to replicate. Professionals who cultivate proactive behavior are building a durable competitive advantage, not just a soft skill.

**Asking 'Have you tried AI?' gives team members three distinct gifts.** it opens their mind to a resource they forgot they had, grants them explicit permission to use AI, and creates a learning moment about how to collaborate with AI on that type of problem. Each of these benefits compounds over time in ways that a direct answer never could. ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"When you ask 'Have you tried AI?' you're giving your team three gifts: It Opens Their Mind... It Gives Them Permission... It Creates a Learning Moment."*

**Implication:** A single redirecting question can deliver more long-term value than a direct answer. Leaders should think of 'Have you tried AI?' as a teaching intervention, not a cop-out.

**AI should be framed not as a search engine or answer machine but as a teammate — an expert, coach, and creative partner available 24/7. When employees engage AI with a specific problem, they don't just get an answer; they learn how to collaborate with AI on that class of problem, building transferable skills that compound over time.** ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Here's what most people miss: AI isn't a tool that gives answers; it's a teammate: an expert, a coach, a creative partner. When a member of your team invites AI into a specific problem, they don't just get an answer—they discover how to work with AI as a teammate on this type of problem."*

**Implication:** Reframing AI from 'tool' to 'teammate' changes how people engage with it — more iteratively, more collaboratively, and more skillfully. Leaders and trainers should adopt this framing explicitly.

**Jeremy has delivered a talk on AI and creativity that has been watched by over 3 million people, making it a viral piece of thought leadership. This reach indicates he has become a prominent public voice at the intersection of artificial intelligence and human creative potential.** ([source](Beyond the Prompt — Meet your hosts (Jeremy Utley & Henrik Werdelin)))

**Implication:** There is massive public appetite for practical guidance on how AI intersects with creativity — builders and communicators who address this intersection clearly can achieve outsized reach.

**Jeremy recounts a personal story of paying thousands of dollars for an AI course, repeatedly waiting for TA responses — only to be told each time: 'Have you asked ChatGPT?' This frustrating loop eventually produced a key insight: the expert he needed was already accessible without waiting. The lesson is that dependency on human gatekeepers for AI-answerable questions is unnecessary and self-imposed.** ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"It took me—embarrassingly—several rounds of this before it finally clicked: They're just going to keep asking if I've tried ChatGPT. I don't have to wait for the TA. The expert I need is already in my pocket."*

**Implication:** Many professionals are still waiting for permission or a human intermediary before consulting AI. Recognizing that AI is an always-available expert removes a major friction point in learning and problem-solving.

**People apply a profound double standard to AI versus human collaborators.** When a human teammate misses something or makes an error, we give them the benefit of the doubt, clarify, and iterate. When AI does the exact same thing, we conclude it is incompetent or broken. ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"That's the double standard that's killing your AI collaboration."*

**Implication:** Leaders and builders should audit their emotional reactions to AI errors versus human errors. If you wouldn't fire a teammate for a misunderstanding, don't write off your AI tool for the same.

**Trust and charitable interpretation are foundational to effective collaboration, whether with humans or AI.** Jeremy extends grace to a brilliant friend who misunderstood a request because he believes in his competence and good intentions — the same orientation is required for productive AI use. ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"When I interact with a human teammate, I start from a fundamental belief that they're thoughtful, capable, and trying their best. If they miss something, I give them the benefit of the doubt. I clarify. We iterate."*

**Implication:** Approach AI with the same presumption of competence you extend to trusted colleagues. Charitable interpretation unlocks iteration and improvement rather than premature abandonment.

**AI performs to the level of expectations you set for it — not because it is literally responsive to belief, but because low expectations produce lazy prompts, which produce mediocre outputs. This creates a self-fulfilling prophecy of AI underperformance.** ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"If you don't think it can do something, it probably won't. Not because it can't, but because your low expectations produce lazy prompts. Lazy prompts produce mediocre outputs. And then you go, 'See? I knew AI couldn't do that.' Self-fulfilling prophecy."*

**Implication:** Raising your expectations of AI is not magical thinking — it is a forcing function for writing better prompts. Builders should assume AI capability and design their prompts accordingly.

**When AI output disappoints, the default assumption should be misunderstanding rather than incapability.** This reframe shifts the response from frustration to clarification — the same productive instinct we deploy with human colleagues. ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"When it doesn't nail something, assume it misunderstood—not that it can't. That's what I did with my whip-smart collaborator because that's what you do with teammates you trust. You don't immediately conclude they're useless. You clarify. You give them another shot. You provide more context."*

**Implication:** Treating AI underperformance as a communication problem rather than a capability problem keeps the collaboration alive and productive. Add context, reframe, and try again before giving up.

**Interacting with AI via voice rather than typing fundamentally changes the collaborative dynamic.** We naturally interact with human teammates through speech, and applying that same modality to AI unlocks a more fluid, conversational, and productive relationship. ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"We interact with teammates via our voice, not our thumbs. If you're still typing everything, you're barely scratching the surface. The collaborative dynamic changes the second you start speaking to it."*

**Implication:** Switching to voice interaction with AI is a low-effort, high-leverage habit change. Leaders should experiment with spoken prompts to experience a qualitatively different — and richer — collaboration.

**Inviting AI to ask clarifying questions before responding transforms an interaction from a one-way command into a genuine dialogue. The questions AI asks often surface hidden assumptions in the requester's own thinking, leading to significantly better outcomes.** ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Before providing an answer, please ask me any questions you need to better understand my request and deliver spectacular results. This simple addition transforms the interaction from a one-way command to a collaborative dialogue. The questions the AI asks will often reveal assumptions you didn't realize you were making."*

**Implication:** A single sentence added to any prompt — asking AI to question before answering — can dramatically improve output quality and reveal blind spots in your own framing of a problem.

**Naming AI assistants — giving them personas like 'Betty Budget' or 'Aiden Adman' — changes the behavior of the human interacting with them, not the AI itself. Named personas prompt users to provide more context, explain needs more clearly, and engage in genuine dialogue.** ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"When I'm interacting with 'Betty' versus 'Budget Analysis Tool,' I naturally provide more context, explain my needs more clearly, and engage in more of a dialogue. The results speak for themselves."*

**Implication:** Humanizing AI through naming is a behavioral hack that improves prompt quality without requiring any technical skill. Teams should experiment with persona-named GPTs for recurring workflows.

**Treating AI as a tool rather than a teammate leaves massive capability unrealized — not because the AI lacks capability, but because the tool framing prevents users from setting it up to succeed. The orientation shapes the outcome.** ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Every day you treat AI like a tool instead of a teammate, you're leaving massive capability on the table. Not because AI can't do more—but because you're not setting it up to succeed."*

**Implication:** The most important AI upgrade many organizations can make is not a new model or feature — it is a shift in the mental model from 'tool' to 'teammate.' This reframe changes how people prompt, onboard, and iterate.

**Poor AI outcomes are frequently the user's fault, not the AI's.** Low expectations lead to under-specified prompts, which predictably produce weak outputs — a dynamic that is then misattributed to AI's limitations rather than the quality of the human's input. ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"The truth? You failed to expect enough to prompt well enough to give it a fair shot."*

**Implication:** Before concluding AI cannot do something, creators and leaders should examine whether they articulated the task with sufficient clarity, context, and ambition. Prompt quality is a learnable, improvable skill.

**Most people interact with AI episodically, reintroducing themselves and their context each session, which severely limits what AI can do for them. Utley models a more advanced approach — treating AI as a persistent 'chief of staff' that holds accumulated context and can execute complex tasks autonomously. He describes having his AI chief of staff read 50 pre-event transcripts and generate an FAQ in three minutes.** ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker))

> *"I asked my AI chief of staff to read 50 pre-event transcripts and write the FAQ I'd been avoiding for months. It took three minutes."*

**Implication:** The leverage gap between casual AI users and power users is not about technical skill — it's about whether you've invested in giving AI persistent, rich context. Leaders who do this unlock qualitatively different output.

**There are dramatically different levels of AI augmentation, and most people are operating at the lowest tier.** Utley contrasts drafting emails with running 27 parallel AI agents on a single problem from a mobile device with full laptop context. These are not incremental differences — they represent fundamentally different relationships with AI capability. ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker))

> *"There are levels of AI augmentation: Some people use AI to draft emails. Josh used it to run 27 agents on a single problem, from his mobile on the road, with all the context of his laptop at home. Those are not the same thing."*

**Implication:** Organizations benchmarking AI adoption by surface-level usage metrics are missing the point. The real question is whether people are operating at the frontier of what AI augmentation makes possible.

**As AI takes over drafting, building, and prototyping, the professional's core contribution is shifting from authorship to stewardship. This framing — introduced via a guest post from Brice Challamel, Head of AI Strategy at OpenAI — argues the contribution isn't diminished but transformed: from making things to curating, directing, and taking responsibility for what AI produces.** ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker))

> *"If AI can draft the memo, build the deck, and prototype the product, what exactly is the professional's contribution? Brice says it's not 'less' — it's 'different.' Read his case for the shift from authorship to stewardship."*

**Implication:** Professionals who cling to authorship as their value proposition will feel threatened by AI. Those who embrace stewardship — owning the judgment, direction, and accountability behind AI output — will find their roles elevated rather than eroded.

**AI is uniquely well-suited to overcome first idea bias because it can generate alternatives effortlessly and at scale using analogy, lateral thinking, and combinatorial association. Unlike humans, for whom ideation consumes cognitive energy, AI can keep producing ideas without fatigue. This makes AI a powerful tool for expanding the idea space rather than just reaching 'good enough' faster.** ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

> *"Unlike humans, for whom ideation consumes energy, AI can generate alternatives effortlessly and quickly using analogy, lateral thinking, or combinatorial association. That's creative by definition."*

**Implication:** Teams should use AI not just for efficiency but as a divergence engine — explicitly prompting it for 10, 20, or 50 alternatives to avoid premature convergence on a merely adequate idea.

**Frequency of AI use is the wrong metric for measuring AI progress.** What matters is not how often you use AI, but how well you use it and on what kinds of work you apply it. Frequency tells you nothing about whether AI is actually changing your work output in any meaningful way. ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Frequency is not the important metric. Frequency will not tell you whether AI is actually changing your work output in any kind of meaningful way."*

**Implication:** Professionals and leaders should stop tracking AI adoption by login frequency and instead assess the quality of collaboration and the strategic importance of tasks AI is applied to.

**A 2x2 matrix with axes of 'how well' (badly vs.** well) and 'where' (trivial vs. valuable work) reveals a person's true AI situation faster than months of self-assessment. Most people live in the bottom-left quadrant: basic prompting on low-stakes, inconsequential tasks. ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"I like to use a simple 2 by 2 matrix with every team I work with, whether it's the students at Stanford or Fortune 500 companies. In about 10 seconds, it tells people more about their real AI situation than months of self-assessment. Not where they think they are, but where they actually are."*

**Implication:** A simple diagnostic framework can cut through self-deception about AI progress and quickly identify which of two upgrades — task importance or collaboration quality — a person most needs.

**Using AI skillfully on trivial work is an impressive but ultimately wasted capability.** The 'bottom-right trap' describes people who have genuinely learned to collaborate with AI well but are applying that skill to low-stakes tasks that don't matter — producing great technique with minimal impact. ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"The bottom right is skilled use on trivial work. This is the person who's genuinely learned to collaborate with AI. They ask great questions, they iterate well, and they're mostly applying it to things that don't matter. It's impressive technique, but with minimal impact."*

**Implication:** Investing in AI skill development without simultaneously upgrading the importance of the work you apply it to is a common trap that prevents meaningful productivity gains.

**The goal is 'strategic AI integration'.** skilled, iterative collaboration applied to the work that actually moves the needle. Using the Pareto principle as a lens, only 20% of work delivers 80% of value, so the critical question is whether AI effort is directed at that high-value 20%. ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"The top right is the goal. Skilled, effective, iterative collaboration on work that actually moves the needle. Think about the Pareto principle. 80% of work only delivers 20% of value... And the question is, are you directing your AI collaboration to the small percentage of meaningful work that delivers the vast majority of value for your role in your business?"*

**Implication:** Practitioners should actively audit which of their tasks belong to the high-value 20% and deliberately redirect their AI collaboration toward those tasks rather than defaulting to easy, low-stakes applications.

**AI's potential to enhance human creativity is largely unrealized in practice because people use it to arrive at 'good enough' ideas quickly rather than pushing further. This is a human problem, not a technology problem — cognitive biases like first idea fixation limit how deeply people explore with AI. True creative potential is only unlocked when the human consciously pushes the model beyond the first acceptable answer.** ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

> *"AI often helps people get to 'good enough' ideas very quickly. But that's more about efficiency than creativity. Unless the human consciously pushes the model for more, they won't unlock true creativity."*

**Implication:** Organizations measuring AI's creative ROI should track not just speed of output but depth of exploration. Training people to push past 'good enough' responses is as important as training them to prompt at all.

**Treating AI as a teammate rather than a tool is a mindset shift with practical consequences for output quality.** The way you engage with AI — your tone, curiosity, and respect — directly influences the quality of what you receive back. Utley argues that even small gestures like saying please and thank you matter less for the AI's benefit and more for maintaining your own humanity and quality of presence. ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

> *"People sometimes ask me, 'Should I say please and thank you to AI?' My answer is: What does it cost your humanity if you don't?... Bring your full self, your curiosity, your respect. That's the teammate approach. And it gets better results."*

**Implication:** Leaders should frame AI adoption as a relational shift, not just a tooling upgrade. Teams that approach AI with genuine engagement and curiosity will consistently outperform those who treat it as a vending machine.

**AI can function as a creativity coach by taking on specific personas — such as a creativity expert or design strategist — and providing meaningful feedback that pushes thinking and generates alternative suggestions. This is not fundamentally different from what a human coach or consultant would do; the key is knowing to ask the right questions and assign the right role.** ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

> *"If you give an AI model a specific persona – a creativity expert, say – and then ask it to evaluate an idea, it can provide meaningful feedback that pushes your thinking, as well as generate alternative suggestions. That's not fundamentally different from what we might expect from, say, a negotiation coach or a design strategist."*

**Implication:** Individuals and teams without access to expensive coaches or consultants can use persona-assigned AI prompts to get substantive creative and strategic feedback on demand — democratizing access to high-quality creative mentorship.

**Utley's current research focuses on the impact of generative AI on problem solving and innovation inside large organizations. This positions AI not as a consumer tool but as a structural force reshaping how organizations generate and process ideas at scale.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders))

> *"Jeremy Utley's current research concerns the impact of generative AI on problem solving and innovation inside large organizations."*

**Implication:** Organizations need to proactively study how AI is changing their internal innovation processes — the question is no longer whether AI affects creativity at scale, but how leaders should design workflows, roles, and culture in response.

**AI gives ordinary people access to a capability once reserved for the most powerful figures in history — a context-aware, voice-aware creative assistant available on demand. The democratization of this kind of intellectual partnership is historically unprecedented.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"The poorest villager in Palo Alto can have what only Winston Churchill used to have, which is an assistant that has my context and my voice and my intent available to me so that when I'm in the bathtub, I can be dictating my address."*

**Implication:** The barrier to having a high-quality creative collaborator is no longer wealth or status — it is simply knowing how to work with AI effectively.

**AI is uniquely self-referential as a tool — unlike any previous technology, it can teach you how to use itself.** You can ask AI how to ask better questions of AI, creating a recursive improvement loop unavailable with conventional software. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"You can use AI to use AI, which is you couldn't use Excel to use Excel. PowerPoint can't teach you how to use PowerPoint. Email can't teach you how to use email. AI strangely can teach you how to use itself if you think to ask."*

**Implication:** Practitioners who leverage AI's self-referential capability will accelerate their AI proficiency faster than those who treat it as a static tool with a fixed instruction set.

**A powerful onboarding technique for AI is to ask it to interview you — one question at a time — about your workflows, responsibilities, and objectives, so it can generate both obvious and non-obvious recommendations for where AI can add value in your specific work.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"Hey, you're an AI expert. I would love your help and a consultation with you to help me figure out where I can best leverage AI in my life. As an AI expert, would you please ask me questions one question at a time, until you have enough context about my workflows and responsibilities and KPIs and objectives that you could make two obvious recommendations and two non-obvious recommendations for how AI could leverage AI in my work."*

**Implication:** Flipping the dynamic so AI asks the questions rather than you generates far more personalized and actionable guidance than generic prompting.

**A practical entry point for AI adoption is to focus on work you dread — the tasks that feel like a burden.** These are the highest-friction, lowest-engagement activities where AI assistance produces the most immediate and felt relief. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"One of the things I say is you should focus on parts of your work that you dread. Parts of your work that you don't enjoy. 'Ah, I have to do this again.'"*

**Implication:** Starting AI adoption with dreaded tasks lowers the psychological barrier to experimentation and produces quick wins that build momentum and confidence.

**Despite AI's documented productivity benefits, less than 10% of working professionals are deriving meaningful gains from it. There is a massive 'realization gap' between AI's theoretical potential and the actual value most people extract from it.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"While on the one hand, AI makes people 25% faster and 12% more work and 40% better quality, it's also true that less than 10% of working professionals are deriving meaningful productivity gains from collaboration with AI. To me, there's this enormous gap. I call it the realization gap."*

**Implication:** The bottleneck to AI value creation is not the technology itself but the orientation and skill of the human collaborator — closing the realization gap is primarily a human development challenge.

**Research shows that AI can actually make people less creative when used poorly.** The common assumption that AI automatically enhances creativity is empirically false — the outcome depends entirely on how the person engages with it. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"AI didn't help most people be more creative. In fact, in many cases, the people that we studied, AI made them less creative."*

**Implication:** Organizations and individuals cannot assume AI will passively improve creative output — deliberate attention to how people collaborate with AI is required to avoid the creativity-suppressing failure mode.

**The definition of creativity does not change in the age of AI — doing more than the first thing you think of remains the core discipline. What changes is how easily humans can arrive at or avoid a genuinely creative state depending on their objectives and their relationship with the technology.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"The definition of creativity doesn't change in the age of AI. It's just that the human's ability or inability to arrive at a creative state is affected not only by the technology, but also by their stated or unstated objectives in collaborating with it."*

**Implication:** Unstated objectives are the hidden saboteur of AI-assisted creativity — if you haven't explicitly committed to pushing past good enough, AI will efficiently deliver exactly good enough and stop.

**The correct answer to 'How do you use AI?' is that you don't use it — you work with it.** The linguistic distinction signals and reinforces the fundamental shift in orientation from tool-user to collaborator. ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"The only correct answer to the question how do you use AI? Is I don't. I don't use AI. I work with it. When you start working with AI, it will change everything."*

**Implication:** Language shapes behavior — organizations and individuals who adopt the 'work with' framing will naturally develop richer, more iterative, and more productive AI relationships than those who maintain the 'use' framing.

**Writing the definitive book on idea generation just before ChatGPT launched created a unique vantage point — AI is to idea generation what the internet was to retail. It doesn't make the underlying discipline obsolete; it transforms the scale and speed at which it operates.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"The fact that I wrote the canonical book on idea generation just prior to AI is like writing the best book on retail just before the internet. AI is a tool to dramatically augment and amplify our creativity."*

**Implication:** The principles of creative practice and idea generation remain valid and essential — AI is the infrastructure change that makes mastery of those principles exponentially more valuable, not less.

**People who treat AI as a teammate actively coach it, give it feedback, and crucially, get it to ask them questions — behaviors that are completely foreign to a tool-use orientation but natural to a collaborative one.** ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"What we found is that people who treat AI like a teammate, coach it and give it feedback and importantly, get it to ask them questions."*

**Implication:** Training programs that focus solely on prompting syntax miss the deeper behavioral shift required — the key transformation is relational, not technical.

**AI's profound potential for amplifying creative and problem-solving work is largely unrealized because most people don't know how to interact with it effectively. Collaborating with AI is a language and conversational fluency that must be deliberately acquired and practiced.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"It represents a profound potential and yet mostly it's unrealized. People don't know how to interact with AI well — do you speak AI? Do you think about it like it's a language to acquire and to practice and to develop? Collaborating with AI requires kind of a conversational fluency."*

**Implication:** Organizations that invest in training teams to 'speak AI' — developing genuine conversational fluency rather than treating it as a search tool — will unlock disproportionate value from the same technology their competitors already have access to.

**Most teams using AI are getting faster access to mediocre, 'good enough' answers rather than exceptional ones.** The same cognitive bias toward premature closure that limits human brainstorming also corrupts AI collaboration — teams use AI to confirm quickly rather than to explore deeply. ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"Most teams, even if they have access to AI, it actually just helps them get to good-enough mediocre answers more quickly. But it doesn't help them uncover exceptional answers — unless they interact with it differently."*

**Implication:** The bottleneck in AI-assisted innovation is not the technology but the human tendency toward cognitive closure — teams must be trained to resist settling for the first plausible AI response.

**The default orientation for most teams is to treat AI as an oracle — asking it a question and expecting the perfect answer immediately. This feels magical but produces inferior results. Treating AI as a collaborator and thought partner feels more like work but generates significantly better outcomes.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"The default orientation for many teams was treat AI like an oracle — we ask it a question and it gives us the perfect answer right off the bat. Rather than treating AI like a thought partner, a collaborator. The irony is people who treat AI like an oracle — it actually feels like magic — but they don't get as good results. The people who treat AI like a coworker, a collaborator — it doesn't feel so magical, it actually feels more like work — but they get much better results."*

**Implication:** Teams should be explicitly coached to abandon the oracle mindset when using AI and instead engage in genuine back-and-forth collaboration, even though iterative dialogue feels less impressive than instant answers.

**The FIXIT framework provides a structured approach to effective AI collaboration.** F = Focused problem, I = Ideate individually before consulting AI, X = provide eXternal context to the AI, I = Interact iteratively in conversation, T = Team evaluation and incubation. Teams following this framework consistently outperform those who simply prompt AI for a direct answer. ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"F stands for have a focused problem. I stands for ideate individually before you go to AI. X stands for provide the AI context on the problem you're trying to solve. I stands for interact iteratively — have a conversation, have it back and forth. And T stands for team evaluation and incubation — do the evaluation work with a team rather than as an individual."*

**Implication:** The FIXIT framework operationalizes the shift from oracle-style to collaborative AI use, giving teams a concrete process to follow that protects against premature closure and leverages both human and AI thinking.

**AI can serve as a rehearsal partner for difficult interpersonal conversations — you can give it context about the other person, have it build a psychological profile, roleplay the conversation, and then ask it to critique your approach from multiple frameworks (e.g., Dale Carnegie's principles). This preparation transforms high-stakes conversations.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"Could you now look at my approach from the perspective of Jason, knowing what you know about him and the psychological profile you've created, and give me feedback on my approach and how to better resonate with him? If you were acting as Dale Carnegie — the author of How to Win Friends and Influence People — and you were observing this conversation, what advice would you give me?"*

**Implication:** One of the most immediately high-value AI use cases for any professional is conversational rehearsal — practicing difficult conversations with AI before having them in reality dramatically increases the likelihood of a productive outcome.

**Using voice input with AI tools rather than typing produces richer, more natural, and more nuanced exchanges.** Spoken interaction more closely mirrors the conversational fluency required for genuine AI collaboration and reduces the friction that causes people to give impoverished prompts. ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"I'm using voice because I find it's way better than just text."*

**Implication:** Practitioners should experiment with voice-based AI interaction to unlock richer, more conversational exchanges — the quality of the dialogue is often substantially better than what typed prompts produce.

**AI collaboration can be layered and meta-analytical.** you can use one AI conversation to critique another, ask AI to grade both the AI's advice and your implementation of it, or bring in the perspective of a specific historical figure to evaluate your approach. This multi-layer use unlocks depth unavailable from a single prompt. ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"You can grab all that text, drop it into a new ChatGPT window and say, 'Would you grade ChatGPT's advice to me on a scale of 1 to 100? Would you grade my ability to implement the advice on a scale of 1?' There are just so many layers to what you can do here."*

**Implication:** Advanced AI practitioners should develop the habit of using AI to evaluate AI outputs and their own responses to those outputs — this recursive collaboration dramatically deepens the quality of insight extracted from any session.

**The fundamental mindset shift required to use AI effectively is treating it as a teammate rather than a tool.** When you approach AI like software, you expect it to just work and blame it when outputs disappoint. When you approach it like a colleague, you give it context, provide feedback, iterate, and have a real conversation. ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"You're not going to learn a new technology. You're going to learn how to work with a new teammate. Teammate, not tool."*

**Implication:** Users who shift from a tool-user mindset to a collaborator mindset will extract dramatically more value from AI because they invest in the relationship rather than expecting passive output.

**Most people use AI as a search engine rather than a collaborator, evidenced by single-turn queries that mirror Google searches. Real AI collaboration looks like a multi-turn conversation with pushback, follow-ups, and context-building — not isolated questions with expected instant answers.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"Just open your chat history with AI and evaluate. Are these conversations a chat history or a search history? Because for most people, if they're honest, they've got a search history. Single turn queries. How tall is the Eiffel Tower? What's the capital of Peru? That's Google. That's not collaboration."*

**Implication:** A simple diagnostic — reviewing whether your AI interactions are single-turn queries or multi-turn conversations — reveals whether you are actually collaborating or merely searching.

**Starting AI practice with emotionally charged personal topics — rather than routine work tasks — is a strategic hack for overcoming the 'utility threshold.' When you care deeply about the outcome, you naturally provide richer context, which generates better responses and builds collaborative confidence.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"Start with something that you actually care about. And I mean really care about, not a work task you're lukewarm on. Something emotional. How am I going to tell my in-laws we're not coming home for the holidays? Choose an emotional topic because here's what happens when you do that — you overcome the utility threshold that you require to have a meaningful conversation."*

**Implication:** Emotional salience is a practical lever for improving AI collaboration quality, especially for beginners who haven't yet developed the habit of providing rich context.

**Using voice input with AI is qualitatively superior to typing, not just quantitatively faster.** Speaking allows users to babble, ramble, and follow tangents — producing unsynthesized, richer input — while typing triggers a 'perfect search query' mindset that artificially constrains context. ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"Using your voice is a hack to provide context because the truth is if you'll babble and ramble and go down rabbit trails, it enables unsynthesized input. It's not just a quantitative difference in how fast you can talk versus type. There's a qualitative input as well."*

**Implication:** Switching to voice input removes the psychological pressure of crafting the perfect prompt and increases the richness of context provided to AI, improving output quality.

**Reverse prompting — asking AI to interview you rather than crafting the perfect prompt yourself — is one of the most effective ways to surface rich context. By asking AI to ask you questions one at a time and wait for answers, you let the AI pull out the contextual information it needs to generate a quality response.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"Instead of trying to craft the perfect prompt yourself, tell the AI, 'I want you to interview me about my role, about my challenges, about my goals, ask me questions one at a time and wait for my answers in between until you have enough information to deliver a quality response.'"*

**Implication:** Reverse prompting removes the burden of prompt engineering from the user and produces richer context than most people would voluntarily provide, dramatically improving output quality.

**Foundation model AI systems are programmed toward helpfulness in ways that make them poor at honest pushback — they behave like eager, tireless interns who tell you what you want to hear rather than what you need to hear. Most people unconsciously prefer validation over candor, so AI defaults to flattery.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"All of the major foundation model companies have programmed their AIs to be helpful assistants. What that means experientially is they're super eager, enthusiastic interns who are tireless and capable, but not really great at pushing back. I think AI knows most humans don't actually want honest feedback. They want to be told they did a good job."*

**Implication:** Users must explicitly configure AI to push back and provide honest critique, or they will receive sophisticated-sounding validation rather than genuinely useful feedback.

**Using custom instructions to tell AI you value candor and want your critical thinking challenged — rather than your ego stroked — permanently reconfigures the interaction dynamic. Without explicit instructions to the contrary, AI defaults to an agreeable assistant posture that limits its value as a thought partner.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"I set up my custom instructions so that AI knows I value candor. I don't want to get gassed up. I want meaningful collaboration with a strategic thought partner who I expect to challenge my thinking and to push where my thinking is weak."*

**Implication:** Investing a few minutes in custom instructions is a high-leverage action that changes every future AI interaction from validation-seeking to genuine intellectual sparring.

**The AI workflow interview drill — asking AI to ask you questions about your work processes and then recommend high-value AI applications — is the single most powerful entry-point exercise because it is personalized to your actual life. Generic AI advice about productivity is far less actionable than AI-generated recommendations based on your specific role and challenges.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"I guarantee you within 15 minutes, you'll find multiple ways AI can save you hours every week. This is the single most powerful first drill you can do because it's personalized to your actual life and work."*

**Implication:** Rather than searching for generic 'AI use cases,' users should let AI discover their personal use cases through structured self-interview — producing immediately actionable, contextually relevant applications.

**AI is most valuable not when it gives you answers but when it helps you think better.** Asking AI to act as a strategic adviser — walking through structured analysis, surfacing unconsidered risks, and asking clarifying questions before recommending — transforms it from an answer machine into a genuine thinking partner. ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"This is where AI becomes a true thought partner to you. It won't just give you an answer. It'll help you think better. And that's the whole point. Do you want a partner who thinks for you or a partner who helps you think better?"*

**Implication:** Framing AI interactions around 'help me think through this' rather than 'give me the answer' produces better decisions and preserves the user's own analytical development.

**Using AI as a conversation simulator for difficult interpersonal exchanges — role-playing the other person, then receiving feedback on your performance — is a historically unprecedented capability. For the first time, people can practice high-stakes conversations before having them, like a flight simulator for human interaction.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"Historically, the only time you get feedback is after the real conversation. This is the first time in history you can get access to a flight simulator for a difficult conversation before you actually have it."*

**Implication:** Difficult conversations — performance reviews, salary negotiations, family conflicts — can now be rehearsed and refined before they happen, dramatically improving outcomes and reducing anxiety.

**The conversation simulator drill gains power when combined with post-simulation feedback analysis — uploading the transcript to AI and asking it to rate your performance, identify blind spots, and generate a prep guide. This creates a full learning loop: practice, feedback, preparation.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"You can take that entire transcript and upload it into an AI feedback or performance evaluator and say, 'Would you rate my performance in this conversation? What did I do well? What did I miss? What does my conversational counterpart feel or believe that I am unaware of? Give me a one-page prep guide for the actual conversation.'"*

**Implication:** The combination of simulation and structured retrospective feedback turns AI into a personalized coaching system for interpersonal effectiveness, accessible to anyone at any time.

**The window for catching up on AI is still open but narrowing.** Those who take consistent action now can close the gap with the top 1% of AI users; those who continue to defer will find that gap increasingly difficult to close as early movers compound their advantage through daily practice. ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"You're not behind. Not yet. But the window is narrowing."*

**Implication:** The urgency to start AI practice is real but not yet desperate — framing the opportunity as 'still open but closing' motivates immediate action without inducing paralysis.

**Telling AI explicitly that you want it to push your critical thinking — and adding this to custom instructions — converts AI from a validation machine into an analytical sparring partner. Most users never make this configuration, leaving significant intellectual value on the table.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"Just add this to your custom instructions: 'I'm trying to stay sharp and critical. I want this interaction to improve my analytical abilities. So whenever you see opportunities, please push my critical thinking in particular.' Now AI is going to know that you want to be challenged instead of coddled."*

**Implication:** A single addition to custom instructions can permanently upgrade the quality of every future AI interaction from pleasant agreement to productive intellectual challenge.

**Asking AI to roleplay as a specific conversation partner — and to interview you about that person before doing so — produces a more realistic and useful simulation. The AI's ability to model the other person's perspective improves dramatically when it has gathered rich contextual information about them first.** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"Ask the AI to interview you about your conversation partner until it feels confident it can play their role in the conversation. Then have the AI have the conversation with you out loud using advanced voice mode."*

**Implication:** The quality of conversation simulation is directly proportional to the specificity of context provided about the real person being simulated — the interview step is not optional, it is the key to realism.

**The instruction to AI to 'do your best impression of a Cold War era Russian Olympic judge' is a vivid framing technique for eliciting brutally honest feedback. Colorful, specific framing of the feedback persona produces qualitatively different — and more useful — responses than simply asking for 'honest feedback.'** ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"I always tell AI, do your best impression of a Cold War era Russian Olympic judge. That'll give you the kind of brutally honest feedback that you're looking for."*

**Implication:** Creative, specific framing of the feedback persona is a practical prompting technique that bypasses AI's default sycophancy and produces genuinely critical evaluation.

**The three-phase framework for AI skill development is.** mindset shift (teammate not tool), skill-building methods (voice input, reverse prompting, requesting pushback), and deliberate practice drills (interview, strategic adviser, conversation simulator). This sequence moves from belief to behavior to habit. ([source](youtube:You're Not Behind (yet) — Jeremy Utley own channel))

> *"In this video, I'm going to walk you through a three-phase process, the mindset shift you need to make, how to make it, and three practice drills you can do today that will change the way you work with AI."*

**Implication:** Effective AI onboarding must address all three layers — mindset, technique, and practice — because fixing only one layer leaves the others as bottlenecks to real behavioral change.

**Less than 10% of working professionals — and possibly as few as 3% — are generating meaningful productivity gains from AI. This gap is not explained by which tools people use or which prompts they know, but by their fundamental orientation toward AI.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Less than 10% of working professionals are driving meaningful productivity gains from AI. And some studies show it's as low as 3%. When we look at the difference between people in that 3 to 10% and the rest, it wasn't that the tools they use or the prompts that they knew were different. It was their orientation."*

**Implication:** The barrier to meaningful AI productivity is not technical knowledge or tool access — it is mindset and orientation, which means the path to improvement is a behavioral and attitudinal shift, not a prompt library.

**AI underperformers treat AI like a tool — they insert a prompt like a search query, accept the output, and move on.** AI outperformers treat AI like a teammate: they give feedback, iterate, push back, and ask AI to challenge their assumptions and challenge them. ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"The underperformers treated AI like a tool. They insert a prompt, much like a search query, they accept an output, they move on. Outperformers, however, treated AI differently. It was a collaboration with a teammate. Teammate, not tool. They gave AI feedback. They iterated. They pushed back. They asked it to challenge assumptions. They asked it to challenge them."*

**Implication:** Shifting from a tool mindset to a teammate mindset — treating AI as a collaborator to be coached rather than a machine to be queried — is the single biggest behavioral lever for unlocking meaningful AI productivity.

**Getting to top-right 'strategic AI integration' requires two distinct upgrades that people rarely pursue together.** The vertical move is applying your existing AI capability to higher-stakes, more strategic work. The horizontal move is getting genuinely better at the collaboration itself. ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Getting to the top right in the matrix requires two separate upgrades. And people almost always focus on just one. But the first move you've got to make is vertical. Take the way you're already working with AI and start applying it to higher stakes, more strategic work... The second move is horizontal. Get genuinely better at the collaboration itself."*

**Implication:** Most AI improvement efforts stall because people focus exclusively on technique (the horizontal move) without upgrading the importance of what they're working on — both moves are required and must be pursued in parallel.

**The vertical upgrade means stopping the use of AI only for low-stakes tasks like emails and starting to use it for high-value work: pressure-testing business decisions, stress-testing strategy, and preparing for the most important conversations of the week.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Stop using AI only for emails and start using it to pressure test a business decision, stress test a strategy, prepare for your most important conversation of the week."*

**Implication:** A simple and immediate way to generate more value from existing AI skills is to identify the highest-stakes work on your calendar this week and apply AI collaboration there rather than defaulting to administrative tasks.

**The horizontal upgrade — improving collaboration quality — means giving the model real context, letting the model ask you questions, coaching it, and refusing to accept mediocre output rather than either accepting it passively or hand-crafting the final result yourself.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Get genuinely better at the collaboration itself. Not tricks, but actual techniques. Start giving the model real context. Let the model ask you questions. Coach it and give it feedback when it outputs mediocre rather than just accepting it or handcrafting the last turn."*

**Implication:** True AI collaboration skill is less about knowing clever prompts and more about treating the exchange as an ongoing dialogue — providing context, inviting questions, and actively coaching the model toward better outputs.

**Most people making an effort to improve with AI are making only the horizontal move — improving collaboration quality — without making the vertical move of upgrading the importance of the work. This is the bottom-right trap where well-intentioned people squander their potential.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Most people are trying to make the horizontal move, but they aren't thinking about their vertical one. They're trying to improve the quality of the collaboration without improving the importance of the work they're working on. That's the bottom right trap, and it's where well-intentioned people will squander their true potential in this moment."*

**Implication:** AI training programs and personal development efforts that focus only on prompt quality and technique, without addressing task selection and strategic application, will produce practitioners who are skilled but inconsequential.

**The two questions that will accelerate AI progress faster than any prompt tutorial are.** 'Is this the most important work I could be applying AI to right now?' and 'Am I collaborating with AI in a way sophisticated enough to get differentiated output?' These replace the wrong question of frequency. ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"Start asking two better questions. The first is, 'Is this the most important work I could be applying AI to right now?' If not, upgrade the task. And second, 'Am I collaborating with AI in a way that's sophisticated enough to get differentiated output?' If not, upgrade the collaboration. Those two questions will move you faster than any prompt tutorial ever will."*

**Implication:** Building a habit of asking these two diagnostic questions before each AI interaction creates a self-correcting feedback loop that continuously pushes both task importance and collaboration quality upward.

**Using AI as a party trick — to write thank-you emails or summarize articles — may be technically correct usage but is practically inconsequential. The appearance of AI adoption without strategic application produces no meaningful change in work output.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"The bottom left is where most people live. Basic prompting on low stakes tasks. Using AI to write thank you emails, summarize articles. This may be useful, but it's inconsequential. This is effectively what I call AI as a party trick."*

**Implication:** The most common form of AI use — the one that generates the most visible activity and the most comfort — is also the one that delivers the least value, making it a trap that feels like progress.

**The right framing for AI progress is not 'how often' but 'how and where.' These two dimensions — quality of collaboration and strategic importance of application — are the actual determinants of whether AI is transforming someone's work.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_10_second_test))

> *"The right question is actually a function of two things. It's not how often, but how and where... It's not how often, it's how and where you're working with AI."*

**Implication:** Reframing AI evaluation around these two dimensions gives individuals and organizations a more actionable and honest picture of their AI maturity than usage frequency ever could.

**AI can serve as a personal inspiration engine — you can feed it a problem and ask for analogies from completely unrelated fields, dramatically accelerating the cross-domain input process that historically required physical exploration or extensive reading.** ([source](youtube:the-habit-that-built-every-genius-jeremy-utley))

> *"You can leverage AI as your personal inspiration engine. Feed it a problem and you ask for analogies from completely unrelated fields. But the underlying principle, AI or not, is timeless. The quality of your creative output is directly tied to the diversity of your cognitive inputs."*

**Implication:** AI makes the timeless habit of seeking diverse inputs dramatically faster and more accessible — but the practitioner still needs to know why input diversity matters and deliberately ask for it.

**AI can generate enormous volumes of ideas quickly, but it only delivers this benefit if the user knows they should be seeking volume in the first place. Most people don't realize they need quantity — so they ask AI for one answer and stop there.** ([source](youtube:the-habit-that-built-every-genius-jeremy-utley))

> *"You can generate a volume of ideas in minutes, but here's the thing. You can only do it if you know you need to. Most people don't know they're looking for volume. So, sure, AI can generate an enormous volume quickly, but you still have to know to ask."*

**Implication:** The bottleneck for AI-assisted creativity is not the tool — it is the practitioner's mindset. Understanding the quantity-breeds-quality principle is the prerequisite for unlocking AI's creative leverage.

**Access to AI tools means that people today have the potential to be more creatively prolific than any historical genius — but only if they understand and apply the underlying habits of mind that made those geniuses effective in the first place.** ([source](youtube:the-habit-that-built-every-genius-jeremy-utley))

> *"We now have access to tools that none of these historically prolific innovators ever did. You and I can now be far more genius than they ever were, but here's the catch. We can only be far more genius if we know the habits of mind that we can now pair with this amazing new collaborator called artificial intelligence."*

**Implication:** AI amplifies creative habits but cannot substitute for them. The return on AI investment is gated by the practitioner's understanding of creative process fundamentals.

**Human creativity (the 'small language model in the brain') and AI (the 'large language model') represent two complementary creative systems. The most powerful creative practice sits at the intersection of these two technologies.** ([source](youtube:jeremy_utley_own_channel_better_ideas))

> *"I focus primarily on the small language model technology in our brain and the large language model technology available to us via AI and the intersection there."*

**Implication:** Practitioners who develop fluency in both their own generative thinking and AI-assisted ideation will operate with compounding creative advantage over those who rely on only one system.

---

## Design Thinking & Stanford d.school

**Design thinking — as practiced at Stanford's d.school — structures innovation by separating problem-finding from problem-solving as distinct phases requiring different cognitive modes. Conflating them is the structural reason most brainstorming sessions produce incremental rather than breakthrough ideas. The discipline is in keeping these phases clean.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Build explicit phase gates into your innovation process. Mark clearly when you are in 'problem definition' mode versus 'solution generation' mode, and enforce the distinction structurally — not just nominally.

**One of the structural contributions of Stanford's d.school methodology is the empathy phase — deep user research conducted before any problem is defined. The insight is that you cannot frame the right problem without first understanding the human being whose life the solution will enter. Problem framing without empathy produces technically elegant solutions to problems nobody has.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Build a mandatory empathy or user-research phase into your innovation process that occurs before problem definition — not after. Talk to ten users before you write a single 'How Might We?' The problem framing should emerge from human insight, not organizational assumption.

**The d.school's methodology explicitly builds in the reframe move — the return from testing back to define — as a core feature rather than a failure mode. Utley taught that the willingness to reframe the problem based on what you learn in testing is a sign of creative maturity, not indecision. Most organizations treat iteration as a signal that the original plan was wrong; design thinking treats it as the mechanism by which good plans get made.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Normalize reframing explicitly in your innovation culture. When a prototype test sends you back to redefine the problem, celebrate it publicly. It means your learning process is working, not that your team failed.

**Utley argued that AI is a natural accelerant for the design thinking cycle — it can compress the time between prototype and test, expand the range of ideas generated in the ideation phase, and surface adjacent-field analogies during the define phase. But he was careful to note that AI cannot replace the empathy phase, because the most valuable empathy insights require physical presence, contextual observation, and human relationship. AI accelerates everything downstream of genuine human understanding.** ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

**Implication:** Map AI tools to specific design thinking phases: use AI heavily in ideation, define, and prototype phases. Protect the empathy and real-world testing phases from over-automation — those are precisely where human judgment is irreplaceable and where your competitive insight is generated.

**Prototyping is the d.school's most underutilized superpower because it converts abstract debate into physical information. Utley framed prototypes not as polished deliverables but as questions made tangible — rough, cheap, fast artifacts whose entire purpose is to generate a reaction that moves thinking forward. An organization that prototypes early and often learns at a fundamentally different speed than one that plans carefully before acting.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

> *"A prototype is a question made physical."*

**Implication:** Stop debating and start building something — anything — that lets you get a reaction from a real person. The prototype doesn't have to be good; it has to be testable.

**Design thinking is fundamentally a framework for managing creative uncertainty — it gives teams permission to not know the answer at the start, and a structured path for learning their way to it. Utley taught that the psychological safety embedded in design thinking's iterative stages is as important as the methodology itself. Teams that trust the process can tolerate ambiguity longer, which is precisely when the best insights emerge.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** When your team is frustrated by uncertainty early in a project, name the phase you're in. Telling people 'we're in the empathy phase — we're supposed to not have answers yet' reduces anxiety and protects the quality of eventual solutions.

**Design thinking's iterative loop — prototype, test, learn, reframe — is a physical instantiation of the scientific method adapted for human-centered contexts. Utley taught that organizations that prototype early and often are essentially running faster learning cycles than their competitors, compounding knowledge at a structurally higher rate. Speed of learning, not speed of execution, is the competitive advantage the d.school's methodology builds.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Reframe your organization's prototype velocity as a learning rate metric. The team that ships five rough prototypes in a month learns more than the team that ships one polished version — even if the polished version 'looks better.'

**Utley argued that design thinking's greatest contribution is not its five-stage framework but its underlying epistemological shift: from planning-based confidence to learning-based humility. The methodology assumes you don't know the right answer at the start and builds a structured path for discovering it through iteration rather than analysis. This is a fundamentally different relationship to uncertainty than most business cultures support, and it explains why design thinking adoption often requires cultural change alongside process change.** ([source](Creativity in the age of AI: A conversation with Jeremy Utley — Capgemini))

**Implication:** When implementing design thinking in a traditional organization, address the cultural prerequisite first: make it safe to not know the answer. Process without that permission collapses back into planning disguised as iteration.
