**Utley's role as Director of Executive Education at Stanford's d.school positioned him at the intersection of rigorous academic methodology and real-world business application — and his core translation work was making the d.school's student-facing methodology legible and actionable for senior leaders who had never encountered it. His insight was that executives don't need a simplified version of design thinking; they need to understand why their existing problem-solving instincts systematically fail, and then design thinking becomes self-evidently necessary.** ([source](JeremyUtley.com — Best AI Keynote Speaker — profile))

**Implication:** When introducing design thinking to senior leaders, don't lead with the framework — lead with a diagnosis of where their current approach breaks down. The methodology only becomes compelling once the failure mode it addresses is visible.

**The physical and temporal structure of d.school workshops — tight time constraints, physical materials, standing work, and rapid team switches — is not accidental but is itself a design intervention. Utley understood that these constraints force participants out of their default cognitive modes and into the generative, associative thinking that produces novel ideas. Creativity thrives under carefully designed constraints; it does not require unlimited time and resources.** ([source](Brainstorming Alone? Here's the Best Method — Jeremy Utley (own channel)))

**Implication:** Use time pressure and physical constraints deliberately in creative sessions. A 10-minute sketch exercise often produces more usable ideas than an hour of open discussion. Constraint is a creative tool, not an obstacle to be overcome.

**Utley and co-author Perry Klebahn drew directly on their d.school teaching to construct the ideaflow framework — the argument that idea volume is the leading metric of innovation health maps directly onto design thinking's quantity-first ideation phase. The d.school's empirical observation was that teams generating more ideas produced better final solutions, and this became the empirical foundation for the book's central thesis. Design thinking gave Utley both the methodology and the evidence base for his creative productivity framework.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Jeremy Utley & Perry Klebahn, 2022))

**Implication:** The ideaflow metric is not an abstract concept — it has a decade of classroom evidence from the d.school behind it. Treat idea volume tracking as a validated pedagogical tool, not an experimental one.

**One of the d.school's core pedagogical moves is making divergent and convergent thinking explicit, named, and temporally separated in creative sessions. Utley applied this directly to his work on ideaflow, arguing that the volume-first principle — generating many ideas before evaluating any — is not just a creativity hack but a structural requirement of effective innovation process. The failure to honor divergence before convergence is the most common and most costly mistake in organizational creativity.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Jeremy Utley & Perry Klebahn, 2022))

**Implication:** Label every creative meeting as either 'divergent' or 'convergent' and enforce the distinction. Mixing them is the default that produces mediocre output — the separation is the discipline that produces breakthroughs.

**The 'How might we?' prompt is design thinking's most portable and underused tool.** Utley taught that the phrasing is precisely engineered: 'How' assumes a solution exists; 'might' signals that multiple solutions are possible; 'we' assigns collaborative ownership. This three-word construction encodes the entire creative orientation of the d.school — optimistic, plural, and collective. Replacing 'we need to fix X' with 'How might we X?' reliably shifts a team's cognitive posture from defensive to generative. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Jeremy Utley & Perry Klebahn, 2022))

**Implication:** Adopt 'How might we?' as the standard framing for any problem your team brings to a creative session. It is a simple linguistic intervention that has a measurable effect on the quality and quantity of ideas generated.

**The d.school's studio model — working in teams, in physical space, with visible artifacts — is itself a pedagogical argument about how creativity works. Utley taught within an environment designed to externalize thinking: sticky notes, whiteboards, physical prototypes, and visible work-in-progress are not decorative but functional. Making thinking visible allows teams to build on each other's ideas, spot patterns, and iterate collectively in ways that are impossible when ideas stay inside individual heads.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show podcast))

**Implication:** Make your team's ideation work visible. Even a simple whiteboard covered in sticky notes changes the dynamics of creative collaboration — it turns private cognition into collective material that the group can manipulate, combine, and improve.

**Utley consistently linked design thinking's empathy-first orientation to his broader argument that most innovation failures are framing failures. If you don't understand the human experience of the problem deeply enough, any framing you produce will be contaminated by your own assumptions rather than grounded in actual user reality. Empathy is not a warm-up exercise — it is the error-correction mechanism that prevents teams from building brilliant solutions to the wrong problems.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer podcast))

**Implication:** When a project is failing in the market, the first diagnostic question should be: 'Did we do sufficient empathy work before we defined the problem?' Most failed innovations can be traced to skipped or superficial empathy phases.

**Design thinking, as taught at the d.school under Utley's direction, is less a methodology and more a set of permissions — permission to not know, permission to make something ugly, permission to change your mind, and permission to prioritize human experience over technical elegance. Most organizations don't lack the tools to innovate; they lack the cultural permissions that make using those tools safe. The d.school's pedagogy is fundamentally about granting those permissions in a structured context.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders podcast))

**Implication:** Before rolling out any innovation methodology, audit what your culture currently punishes. If it punishes not knowing, rough work, or changing direction, the methodology will be adopted in name only. Fix the permission structure first.

**The 'define' phase — crafting a precise 'How might we?' question — is where most innovation value is either created or destroyed. Utley taught at the d.school that a well-formed problem statement is narrow enough to guide action and wide enough to invite creative solutions. Most organizations skip straight from observations to solutions, bypassing the single step most responsible for generating novel answers.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck podcast))

**Implication:** Invest serious time before any ideation session crafting the question. A better-framed 'How might we?' will generate better ideas than any facilitation technique applied to a poorly framed one.

**Design thinking's five-stage process — empathy, define, ideate, prototype, test — is not a linear checklist but a repeatable framework for externalizing the creative cycle. Jeremy Utley taught this methodology at Stanford's d.school and consistently emphasized that its power lies in making implicit cognitive moves explicit and therefore teachable. The process works across industries because it addresses the universal structure of human-centered problem solving, not any domain-specific content.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show podcast))

**Implication:** Any team in any industry can adopt a structured creative process. You don't need to be in a design firm to use design thinking — you need to be solving problems for humans, which is universal.

**The empathy phase of design thinking is not a feel-good formality — it is the information-gathering stage that determines whether everything downstream is solving the right problem. Utley consistently argued that teams skip or abbreviate empathy because they are eager to solution, but this impatience is precisely what produces solutions no one wants. Deep user observation before problem definition is the highest-leverage investment a team can make.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders podcast))

**Implication:** Before your next brainstorm, double the time you spend talking to the people who have the problem. The quality of your empathy work sets the ceiling on the quality of every solution you generate.

**The ideation phase of design thinking requires the deliberate separation of generation from evaluation — a discipline the d.school encodes structurally in its workshops. Utley repeatedly emphasized that when teams allow evaluation to contaminate generation, evaluation always wins, and the result is a short list of safe, predictable ideas. The mechanical separation of 'yes, and' divergence from critical convergence is what makes design thinking's ideation phase produce genuinely novel output.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer podcast))

**Implication:** In your next ideation session, create a hard rule: no evaluation during generation. Use a timer if necessary. The cognitive separation of these two modes is not optional — it is the mechanism that produces novel ideas.

**Stanford's d.school was built on the conviction that creativity is a learnable skill, not a fixed trait — and every element of its pedagogy is designed to prove that point through experience rather than argument. Utley's work as Director of Executive Education at the d.school was explicitly oriented toward helping professionals unlearn the belief that they are not creative by giving them small, accumulating wins. The methodology's deepest function is identity change, not just technique transfer.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show podcast))

**Implication:** Don't introduce design thinking to your team as a tool — introduce it as a proof of concept that they can be creative. The first workshop's most important output is not the prototype; it is the shift in self-perception.

**The d.school's insight that most innovation failures are framing failures — not solution failures — reorients where organizations should invest creative energy. Utley consistently argued that the discipline of problem framing, including sitting with a question long enough to restate it multiple ways before generating solutions, is the highest-leverage skill in the innovation toolkit. Teams that rush to ideation before nailing the problem definition are solving with great energy in the wrong direction.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck podcast))

**Implication:** Before your team generates a single solution, run a problem-reframing exercise: write the problem five different ways and ask which version, if solved, would create the most value. This alone can redirect months of wasted effort.

**The d.school teaches empathy as an active, observational discipline — not simply asking people what they want, but watching what they do, noticing the gaps between stated preferences and actual behavior. Utley emphasized that the most valuable insights from the empathy phase are rarely what users say explicitly; they live in the workarounds, the frustrations, and the unarticulated needs that surface only through close observation. This is why user surveys alone are insufficient inputs for genuine innovation.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders podcast))

**Implication:** Replace or supplement your next user survey with direct observation sessions. Watch people attempt to solve the problem your product addresses, without explaining your product. The gap between what they say and what they do is where your best design brief lives.

**Utley taught that the test phase in design thinking is not about proving that your solution works — it is about learning as fast as possible what is wrong with it. This reframe from validation to learning fundamentally changes what a 'successful' test looks like: a test that surfaces a critical flaw early is more valuable than one that confirms an existing hypothesis. Organizations that treat testing as proof-gathering miss the entire learning function of the phase.** ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer podcast))

**Implication:** Go into your next user test explicitly looking for what's broken, not what's working. Brief your team: 'A test that finds three fatal flaws is a great test. A test that confirms our assumptions is a test we should be suspicious of.'

**The d.school's methodology democratizes innovation by removing the genius requirement — it replaces inspiration with process. Utley's consistent argument is that design thinking gives ordinary teams a repeatable path to extraordinary output, not by lowering standards but by structuring the conditions under which great ideas reliably emerge. This is the methodology's most subversive claim: that innovation is not a talent problem, it is a process problem.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck podcast))

**Implication:** If your organization believes it doesn't have enough 'creative people,' the real problem is almost certainly that you don't have a reliable creative process. Installing one — even imperfectly — will outperform searching for more talented individuals.

**The Stanford d.school's cross-disciplinary model — deliberately mixing engineers, business students, designers, social scientists, and humanists in the same creative space — is a structural argument about where innovation comes from. Utley taught that the collision of different mental models is not just enriching but generative: novel combinations arise precisely because participants don't share the same vocabulary and assumptions. Homogeneous teams optimize within existing paradigms; diverse teams recombine across them.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show podcast))

**Implication:** The next time you staff an innovation team, deliberately include someone who has never worked in your industry. Their naive questions and cross-domain analogies will consistently outperform insider expertise in the early divergent phases of the process.

**Jeremy Utley holds a lecturer position at Stanford, situating him within one of the world's leading institutions for design thinking and innovation education. This academic grounding gives his work on creativity and AI a rigorous, research-informed foundation.** ([source](Beyond the Prompt — Meet your hosts (Jeremy Utley & Henrik Werdelin)))

**Implication:** Practitioners engaging with Jeremy's frameworks can expect them to be rooted in Stanford's design thinking tradition, making them more transferable to organizational and educational contexts.

**Utley serves as Director of Executive Education at Stanford's d.school and co-hosts 'Stanford's Masters of Creativity,' positioning the d.school as a place where creative problem-solving is taught as a rigorous, learnable discipline. This institutional context grounds his frameworks in both academic research and executive practice.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck))

**Implication:** Leaders seeking to build creative cultures can draw on validated, research-backed frameworks from design thinking traditions rather than treating creativity as purely innate. Creativity can be systematically taught and operationalized.

**Design thinking is fundamentally a language for interdisciplinary collaboration that puts the human being at the center of creativity and innovation efforts. It aligns teams not just on solutions but — more importantly — on the human problem to be solved. All expertise is then placed in service of that human being.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders))

> *"Design thinking, very simply, it's a language for interdisciplinary collaboration. So you've got folks coming from different backgrounds. How do you make sure to maximize the respective perspectives and expertise of a diverse group? Design thinking offers one such way. It really puts the human being whose life you're trying to improve at the center of your creativity and innovation efforts."*

**Implication:** Leaders building cross-functional teams should invest in shared problem-framing language before jumping to solutions — alignment on the problem is the prerequisite for genuine innovation.

**Jeremy discovered the d.school not through formal research but by noticing interesting energy down the hall at a startup in India. Despite being enrolled at Stanford — where the d.school was founded — he had no awareness of it until outsiders pointed him toward it. The best opportunities are often invisible until someone names them for you.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

> *"I keep hearing these interesting rumblings down the hall from the design department... And I said, What's that? They said, We're at Stanford, what do you mean? What's that? I said, I don't know."*

**Implication:** Proximity to great resources doesn't guarantee awareness of them. Leaders and educators should actively surface opportunities for people — especially the ones hiding in plain sight.

**The Stanford d.school was built on the insight that society's hardest problems don't fit neatly into a single discipline. David Kelley and co-founders created a space for cross-departmental collaboration — bringing MBAs, lawyers, doctors, engineers, and journalists together — because real-world challenges require teams with interdisciplinary fluency, not just deep domain expertise.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

> *"a lot of the problems facing society don't fit neatly. They're just MBA problems, or just law problems there. They have lots of different facets to them. And we need to be equipping teams that are going to be interdisciplinary in nature, with language to bring their respective disciplinary expertise to bear."*

**Implication:** Organizations tackling complex problems should deliberately build interdisciplinary teams and invest in shared language for collaboration. Domain depth is necessary but not sufficient — you need the connective tissue of shared creative process.

**Jeremy's path to Stanford's d.school ran through direct exposure to poverty alleviation work — a venture accelerator in Bolivia, an AIDS orphanage in Zambia, and a solar startup in India. His deep curiosity about economic development, not a design background, was what made him 'd-schooley.' Passion for real human problems is a stronger predictor of design thinking aptitude than formal creative training.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

> *"I got roped into a venture accelerator program in Bolivia. And I saw firsthand in the developing world, the power of entrepreneurship and small business to drive dignity and poverty alleviation."*

**Implication:** Design thinking and innovation education are most powerful when anchored to genuine human stakes. Recruiting for curiosity and empathy — not just creative credentials — builds stronger innovation teams.

**The Wonder Wander technique is taught at Stanford's d.school and referenced in Jeremy's book 'Ideaflow,' suggesting it is not an informal tip but a validated, teachable practice embedded in a serious creative methodology. Its inclusion in both academic and published contexts gives it credibility as a repeatable tool.** ([source](youtube:brainstorming-alone-jeremy-utley))

> *"We tell folks at Stanford this all the time, we write about it in Ideaflow."*

**Implication:** Practitioners can adopt the Wonder Wander with confidence that it is grounded in both academic pedagogy and practical creative frameworks, not merely personal anecdote.

---

## Personal Creativity & Self-Development

**The habit that built every genius looks like doing nothing — it is the practice of unhurried, undirected thinking time.** Utley points to the incubation phase of creativity: the period when the conscious mind releases a problem and the subconscious continues processing it. Protecting this idle time from the relentless filling-in of schedules is one of the highest-leverage creative investments an individual can make. ([source](The Habit That Built Every Genius (It Looks Like Doing Nothing) — Jeremy Utley YouTube channel))

> *"The most productive thing you can do is sometimes nothing. The incubation phase is real, and we've scheduled it out of existence."*

**Implication:** Deliberately protect unscheduled, screen-free thinking time in your week. Walks, showers, and long drives are not wasted — they are the substrate in which novel connections form. Treat this time as a non-negotiable creative appointment, not a luxury.

**Waiting for inspiration is a strategy for not creating.** Professionals don't wait to feel ready — they show up and generate, and inspiration emerges as a byproduct of that generative activity rather than a prerequisite for it. The discipline is treating idea generation as a non-negotiable daily practice, like exercise, regardless of how motivated you feel in the moment. ([source](It's a Skill, Not a Pill — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Inspiration is a byproduct of doing the work, not a prerequisite for it."*

**Implication:** Build a generation ritual that does not depend on mood. Schedule idea time. Set a quota. The routine creates the conditions for inspiration to show up — you cannot wait for it and also produce consistently.

**The expert's curse in creativity is that deep domain knowledge narrows the idea space.** The more fluent you become in a field's existing vocabulary, the more your new ideas are recombinations within that vocabulary. Cross-disciplinary exposure is not intellectual enrichment — it is corrective medicine for the narrowing effect that expertise inevitably produces. ([source](The Reason You Don't Have Better Ideas — Jeremy Utley YouTube channel))

> *"Your expertise is narrowing your creative range. The more expert you become, the more your thinking is constrained by the vocabulary of your domain."*

**Implication:** Deliberately consume outside your domain of expertise. Set a rule that a meaningful fraction of your reading, listening, and learning comes from fields adjacent or entirely unrelated to your core work. Treat this as professional necessity, not indulgence.

**Are you more of what you value? This question — which Utley poses as a personal accountability tool — surfaces the gap between stated creative values and actual creative behavior. Most people say they value curiosity, exploration, and idea generation, but their daily behavior reflects judgment, execution, and consumption. The question demands behavioral evidence, not aspiration.** ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Are you more of what you value? Not do you believe in it — are you actually more of it than you were?"*

**Implication:** Use this question as a weekly personal audit. List your stated creative values — curiosity, generativity, exploration — and then list your actual behaviors this week. Where the two diverge is your real development agenda.

**Stopping trying to be original is one of Utley's most counterintuitive prescriptions for personal creative development.** Originality anxiety — the paralysis produced by trying to generate ideas that have never existed — is itself the block. All creative work is recombination; the pursuit of radical novelty is both historically inaccurate and practically counterproductive. Freeing yourself from the originality imperative is what allows genuine creative flow. ([source](Stop Trying to Be Original — Jeremy Utley YouTube channel))

> *"Stop trying to be original. Originality is a byproduct of deep engagement, not a starting point."*

**Implication:** Release the pressure to generate unprecedented ideas. Instead, ask: what connections have I not made yet? What exists in one domain that could serve a purpose in another? Recombinatorial thinking is not a lesser form of creativity — it is how creativity actually works.

**Fear, not fatigue, is what causes most people to disengage from AI and new creative tools.** Utley argues that when people say they are 'tired of AI' or find it frustrating, the underlying emotion is more accurately identified as anxiety — the discomfort of having one's self-concept as a capable professional threatened by a new competency requirement. Naming the emotion correctly changes the developmental response. ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"You're not tired of AI. You're scared of it. And that's okay — but let's call it what it is."*

**Implication:** When you notice resistance to a new creative tool or practice, ask whether the resistance is genuine exhaustion or disguised fear of incompetence. Fear responds to different interventions than fatigue — it needs small, repeated exposures and quick wins, not rest.

**The 'doing gap' — the space between knowing what to do and actually doing it — is the decisive personal development challenge of the current moment. Most people who engage with creative and AI capability content are consuming rather than applying. Accumulating frameworks without behavioral change is a form of sophisticated avoidance. The gap that will determine personal creative development is not knowledge, it is execution.** ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The doing gap will kill more careers than AI will. Not because AI takes your job — but because you never actually changed your behavior."*

**Implication:** After every piece of creative development content you consume, commit to one behavioral change before the next consumption. Measure your practice-to-consumption ratio. If it is heavily weighted toward consumption, you are developing a knowledge stockpile that produces no creative output.

**The imagination ceiling is a personal creative limit set not by innate ability but by the quality and diversity of inputs a person has accumulated. When 70% of people get stuck on AI-assisted creativity tasks, the failure point is almost never the tool — it is the narrowness of the input diet that constrains what the person can imagine asking or directing the tool to do.** ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The bottleneck isn't the AI. The bottleneck is your imagination — and your imagination is only as rich as what you've put into it."*

**Implication:** Systematically audit and expand your input diet. The ceiling on what you can create — with or without AI assistance — is set by the breadth and depth of what you've consumed, experienced, and cross-referenced. Invest in the inputs before you expect better outputs.

**Being the prompt — not just writing prompts — is Utley's prescription for the most effective personal relationship with AI as a creative tool. The person who uses AI well is one who brings their full self, their context, their half-formed intuitions, and their genuine questions to the interaction. AI amplifies what you bring; the quality of your self-expression in the interaction determines the ceiling on what you get back.** ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Be the prompt. The best prompt isn't a sentence — it's a person who shows up with something real."*

**Implication:** Invest in articulating your own thinking more precisely — your beliefs, your questions, your half-formed hypotheses. The better you are at externalizing your internal world, the more useful any AI interaction becomes. Self-expression is the leverage point.

**The rising opportunity cost of being human means that as AI handles more execution and production tasks, the premium on distinctly human creative capabilities — taste, framing, intuition, the ability to ask generative questions — increases dramatically. Personal creative development is now not just a quality-of-life investment; it is a professional survival strategy with accelerating economic urgency.** ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The opportunity cost of not developing your human creative capacity has never been higher — because the machines are handling everything else."*

**Implication:** Prioritize the development of capabilities that AI cannot replicate: taste, aesthetic judgment, cross-domain analogical thinking, deep listening, and the ability to ask questions that reframe a problem. These are not soft skills — they are the premium human value layer in an AI economy.

**Building a personal creative team — even a virtual or AI-assisted one — is a strategic response to the limits of solo creative output. Utley describes 'wherewithal moments' as instances when access to collaborators, advisors, or tools fundamentally changes what a person can accomplish. Recognizing that personal creativity has structural limits and deliberately building support infrastructure around it is itself a creative act.** ([source](I Have a Team (A Collection of Wherewithal Moments) — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I have a team now. And the moment I had a team, I realized how much I'd been leaving on the table by trying to do it alone."*

**Implication:** Map your creative support infrastructure. Who are your thinking partners, your input sources, your challenge-givers? Where are the gaps? Building this structure deliberately is one of the highest-leverage personal creative investments you can make.

**The nine habits productivity gurus get wrong are, for Utley, largely habits that optimize execution at the expense of generation. Productivity culture — inbox zero, time blocking for tasks, deep work on defined deliverables — is structurally biased toward output of known work rather than creation of new possibilities. Personal creative development requires resisting the productivity optimization that crowds out generative time.** ([source](9 Habits Productivity Gurus Get Wrong — Jeremy Utley YouTube channel))

**Implication:** Audit your productivity system for generation-hostile structures. If your calendar is fully optimized for task completion and contains no protected time for exploration, question-asking, and idea generation, you are running a system that will produce excellent execution and creative atrophy simultaneously.

**Most people operating with AI are still in a 'have you tried it?' phase — occasional, superficial use that produces no fundamental change in creative output or capacity. Moving from experimentation to genuine creative augmentation requires consistent daily practice, a willingness to use AI on real work, and the patience to build a working relationship with the tool over time rather than auditing it in isolated tests.** ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Most people haven't really tried AI. They've sampled it. Trying it means making it part of your actual work for long enough that it changes how you think."*

**Implication:** Commit to a genuine thirty-day immersion in AI-assisted creative work on your actual highest-priority project — not a sandbox experiment. The behavioral and cognitive shifts that unlock real creative augmentation require sustained engagement, not intermittent testing.

**Utley frames the question 'If you were an AI, I'd think you were terrible' as a provocation about the quality and specificity of human thinking. If a person's ideas, outputs, and responses would be indistinguishable from a low-effort AI generation — generic, undifferentiated, predictable — then they have not yet developed the distinctly human creative voice that makes their contribution irreplaceable. The standard is specificity, perspective, and earned insight.** ([source](If You Were an AI, I'd Think You Were Terrible — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"If you were an AI, I'd think you were terrible. Generic. Predictable. No point of view. The question is: are you bringing something a machine couldn't?"*

**Implication:** Use this as a personal quality standard: review your recent written outputs, presentations, and ideas and ask whether they reflect a genuinely specific human perspective or generic competence. The goal is not to beat AI at volume — it is to produce what AI cannot: earned, situated, perspective-saturated insight.

**The move from solo ideation to collaborative creative practice — what Utley calls working with AI as a creative partner rather than using it as a tool — represents a fundamental shift in creative self-concept. The person who works with AI maintains authorial identity and direction while expanding creative capacity; the person who merely uses AI outsources agency and becomes dependent on prompts that produce generic output.** ([source](Don't Use AI. Work With It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Don't use AI. Work with it. There's a difference — and the difference is whether you're the author or the audience."*

**Implication:** Define your authorial position in every AI interaction. What is your perspective, your taste, your judgment in this session? AI amplifies the author who shows up fully; it replaces the person who abdicates creative responsibility. Your job is to remain the author.

**Real augmentation — the kind that genuinely expands what a person can create — looks different from the productivity gains most people expect from AI. It is not faster completion of the same tasks; it is the ability to operate in domains, at scales, and with a diversity of inputs that were previously inaccessible. Utley challenges individuals to ask not 'is AI saving me time?' but 'is AI enabling me to do things I could not have done before?'** ([source](This Is What Real Augmentation Looks Like — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Real augmentation isn't doing the same things faster. It's doing things that weren't possible before."*

**Implication:** Raise your evaluation standard for AI's role in your creative work. If every use case is efficiency gain on existing tasks, you are under-utilizing the tool. Identify one creative project this month that would have been impossible without AI assistance and pursue it.

**Not being creative is a learned behavior, not a natural state.** Children are uniformly creative; what happens in schools and corporate environments is systematic suppression of generative instinct through the rewarding of judgment over production. The uncreative adult was trained into that condition — which means the condition can be untrained. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck podcast))

> *"Creativity isn't something you have or don't have. It's something that gets educated out of you."*

**Implication:** If creative suppression is learned, it can be unlearned. Adults who believe they are 'not creative' should treat that belief as the first thing to dismantle — not as a fixed fact about themselves but as the residue of an environment that punished generation.

**The single biggest barrier to creative contribution is the belief that you are either creative or not.** This fixed mindset about creativity functions as a self-fulfilling prophecy: people who believe they lack creativity stop practicing generative behavior, and the absence of practice confirms the belief. Dismantling the belief must come before any technique will take hold. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders podcast))

**Implication:** Before teaching any ideation technique, diagnose and address the limiting belief. Organizations that train brainstorming methods without first addressing the 'I'm not creative' belief are building on sand. The mindset intervention is the prerequisite.

**Premature self-judgment is the primary mechanism by which creative confidence is destroyed in individuals.** When a person evaluates their own ideas the moment they generate them, evaluation wins every time — the internal critic is faster and louder than the generative voice. Separating generation from evaluation in personal creative practice is just as essential as separating them in team brainstorming. ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer podcast))

**Implication:** Build a personal practice where the rule is 'generate first, evaluate never in the same session.' Keep a dedicated idea notebook where criticism is prohibited. Return to yesterday's ideas with a fresh evaluative eye rather than collapsing both modes into a single moment.

**Ideaflow — the volume and velocity of ideas you generate per unit of time — is the leading personal creativity metric.** It predicts future creative output the way fitness predicts athletic performance. Most people have no idea what their personal ideaflow rate is, which means they have no baseline, no improvement trajectory, and no signal about whether their creative capacity is growing or atrophying. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Measure your personal ideaflow. For one week, write down every idea you generate — product ideas, process ideas, questions, observations. Count them. That number is your baseline. Now try to increase it. What you measure, you improve.

**Creativity is a skill, not a talent — and that distinction changes everything about how you develop it.** Skills are built through deliberate practice; talents are either present or absent. The moment you accept creativity as a skill, you stop waiting to be chosen by inspiration and start treating idea generation as a trainable discipline with a measurable improvement curve. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

> *"Creativity is a skill, not a talent. And that's actually good news, because skills can be developed."*

**Implication:** Stop auditing yourself for creative 'gifts' and start designing a practice. Set daily or weekly idea quotas. Track your ideaflow over time. The metric that rises is the proof that creativity is being built, not discovered.

**Creative confidence compounds.** Small creative wins rewire the self-concept from 'I am not a creative person' to 'I generate ideas.' The goal in early creative practice is not great ideas — it is the accumulation of attempts that gradually shifts identity. Identity change, not output quality, is the real measure of early creative development. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

> *"The goal isn't to have a great idea today. The goal is to become someone who generates ideas — and that identity shift is what makes the great ideas possible eventually."*

**Implication:** Judge your creative practice by number of attempts, not quality of output. The early-stage win is showing up and generating, not producing a breakthrough. Trust that the identity shift precedes and enables the quality leap.

**Reclaiming personal creativity requires building tolerance for ambiguity — the ability to sit with open questions without prematurely closing them into answers. Most people have been trained to resolve uncertainty as quickly as possible, but generative thinking lives in the space before resolution. The creative person's core capacity is the willingness to stay in that uncomfortable uncertainty long enough for novel connections to form.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Practice holding questions open. When you feel the urge to resolve a problem quickly, treat that urge as a signal to slow down and explore. The discomfort of ambiguity is the exact environment in which original ideas are most likely to emerge.

**Practicing generativity as a daily discipline means setting a personal ideaflow quota — a fixed number of ideas generated per day or week regardless of quality. The quota does two things simultaneously: it removes the pressure of quality from the generative act, and it accumulates volume that statistically contains better ideas than a smaller pool ever could. The quota is both a practice and a mindset intervention.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

> *"Set a quota. Not a goal — a quota. A goal is something you hope to achieve. A quota is something you commit to regardless."*

**Implication:** Establish a daily or weekly personal idea quota and track it. Ten ideas per day is a common starting prescription. Don't filter — just generate. The discipline of meeting the quota, not the quality of any single idea, is the point of the practice.

**Jeremy operates across multiple roles simultaneously — Stanford lecturer, author, and AI keynote speaker — demonstrating a portfolio approach to intellectual work. This multi-role identity allows him to translate academic insight into practitioner-facing content at scale.** ([source](Beyond the Prompt — Meet your hosts (Jeremy Utley & Henrik Werdelin)))

**Implication:** Thought leaders who bridge academia, publishing, and public speaking can amplify their ideas far beyond what any single channel allows — a model worth considering for anyone building influence around a core idea.

**When productivity is amplified 100x by AI, the opportunity cost of human moments inflates proportionally.** Choosing dinner with your wife no longer costs 1 unit of output — it costs 100. This creates a psychological and ethical pressure at the nervous system level that pure productivity arguments ignore entirely. ([source](Don't Use AI. Work With It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human was about how when your output is 100x, choosing dinner with your wife costs 100 units instead of 1 — and what that does to your nervous system at 8pm."*

**Implication:** Leaders adopting AI at scale need to deliberately protect human moments and relationships, because the math of AI leverage will unconsciously make those moments feel more expensive. The default will be to work, not to live.

**Jeremy nearly cancelled a long-planned family trip to his kids' great-grandparents' house in favor of two business opportunities. The moment of negotiation with himself — rehearsing lines like 'this is a special opportunity' and 'you can't miss this one' — disturbed him more than the opportunities themselves. It revealed a gap between stated values and actual behavior.** ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I almost exchanged a family trip for two business opportunities... And I caught myself negotiating. This is a special opportunity… You can't miss this one… What if you say no?… And for a moment, I almost moved the trip. That moment bothered me more than I expected."*

**Implication:** Builders and leaders should audit not just their outputs but their instincts — the moment you start negotiating against your own stated priorities is a signal worth paying attention to.

**Utley identifies a common psychological trap.** tasks that stay on the to-do list are often not hard but 'should-have-already-done' tasks. Their difficulty is social, not technical. AI surfaces this pattern by making execution trivially easy — which exposes that the real barrier was avoidance, not effort. ([source](Stop Reintroducing Yourself to AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The reason that FAQ sat on my list was not that it was hard. It was that it was the kind of task I should have already done. You know the feeling. There's a version of it on your list too."*

**Implication:** AI makes it harder to hide behind 'I don't have time' as an excuse for avoided tasks. When AI can execute a task in minutes, the remaining bottleneck is clearly psychological — which is useful self-knowledge, even if uncomfortable.

**Adding a P.S.** to a follow-up message — signaling the next problem you're already tracking — trains colleagues and managers to expect continuous initiative rather than one-off contributions. This small communication habit shapes others' perception of you as someone with sustained momentum. ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"P.S. Also keeping an eye on [next bug from your list]. That P.S.? It signals momentum. You're training them to expect initiative."*

**Implication:** Professional reputation is built incrementally through small signals as much as large deliverables. Deliberately communicating what you're watching next turns a single act of initiative into a pattern others come to rely on.

**Jeremy argues individuals should commission themselves to transform their own roles before someone else does — drawing on Jeff Bezos's example of telling Steve Kessel to 'kill your own business,' which led to the invention of the Kindle.** ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Don't wait for your organization to figure this out. Jeff Bezos saw Napster and told Steve Kessel: 'Your job is to kill your own business.' Kessel invented the Kindle. What would it look like to commission yourself to transform your own role before someone else does?"*

**Implication:** Waiting for organizational permission to transform is a losing strategy. Individuals who proactively redesign their own roles using AI will create the future they want; those who wait will have it created for them.

**Utley frames the AI learning journey as a four-stage progression.** Fear → Familiar → Fluent → Fun. Everyone starts at Fear — including those who now appear expert — and the goal is not to skip fear but to move through it. This normalization of the fear stage removes the shame that keeps people stuck. ([source](You're Not Tired of AI. You're Scared of It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I think about it as a progression: Fear → Familiar → Fluent → Fun. Everyone starts at Fear. (Even the people who seem like experts—trust me, we all started terrified.) The goal isn't to skip Fear. It's to move through it."*

**Implication:** Leaders introducing AI to teams should explicitly name this progression and validate that Fear is the normal starting point — doing so lowers the activation energy needed to begin.

**The best insights and phrases often emerge unplanned in the act of doing.** Utley's most memorable line from his keynote — 'it's not a pill, it's a skill' — came out spontaneously mid-delivery and surprised even him. He observes this is usually how the best things happen. ([source](It's a Skill, Not a Pill — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I didn't plan it. It just came out. Which is usually how the best things happen."*

**Implication:** Creative output — including insight and communication — is often a byproduct of showing up and doing the work rather than deliberate pre-planning. Building in space for unscripted iteration in presentations and conversations can surface better ideas than scripted preparation alone.

**Jeremy reframes what looks like a discipline problem as an economics problem.** High achievers feeling pulled away from family and presence aren't failing at willpower — they are rationally responding to asymmetric incentives created by AI amplification. Diagnosing it as a character flaw leads to the wrong prescription: more guilt and harder willpower, both of which will fail because the incentive structure is relentless. ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"When you think 'I lack discipline,' the solution is willpower: try harder, calendar-block more aggressively, feel guilty when you fail. (And you will fail, because the incentives are relentless.) That's not strategy. That's drift. When you recognize 'the opportunity cost of being human has fundamentally changed,' the solution is structural. You don't fight the current. You build a dam."*

**Implication:** When you or your team struggle to disconnect from work or AI tools, don't default to moralizing about discipline. Redesign the environment and incentive structure instead — because willpower alone cannot sustainably compete with compounding economic returns.

**Jeremy opens with a personal scene of sitting with his wife and experiencing a metacognitive loop — knowing he should be present, thinking about whether he's present, not actually being present because he's thinking about presence. He uses this moment of honest self-observation to establish that the problem is not hypothetical or abstract — it is already operating in the minds of people who care about being human.** ([source](The Rising Opportunity Cost of Being Human — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I was sitting on the couch with my wife the other night. No crisis. No conflict. No drama. Just talking about the day. And in the back of my mind, I could feel it. The temptation to think, This is not productive."*

**Implication:** The pull away from presence is not dramatic or obvious — it operates as a subtle background process even in moments of apparent calm. Leaders should not wait for a crisis to notice this dynamic; the metacognitive loop Jeremy describes is a diagnostic signal worth monitoring in everyday life.

**Jeremy draws a sharp distinction between what AI can do 'for' him versus what it can do 'as' him.** Being a father, husband, best friend, and follower of Jesus Christ are roles that are irreducibly his — no agent or automation can perform them in his place. This framing gives concrete shape to the human-AI boundary. ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"No one else can be a father to my kids. No one else can be a husband to my wife. No one else can be a best friend to my best friends. No one else can follow Jesus Christ on my behalf. AI can do a shocking number of things for me. It cannot do those things as me. Those are irreducibly mine."*

**Implication:** Identifying your 'irreducibles' — roles no one can perform for you — is a practical first step to protecting what matters most in an AI-augmented life. These roles deserve calendar protection, not leftover time.

**Jeremy reflects on a recurring pattern across his life stages — school, early career, kids, advancement, and now AI — where there has always been a 'perfectly reasonable excuse' for not yet becoming the person he hopes to be. He is determined not to let AI become the latest version of that excuse.** ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"It used to be school. Then early career. Then kids. Then advancement. Now AI. At every stage, there's been a perfectly reasonable excuse for why I'm not yet the person I hope to be. I don't want AI to become the next excuse."*

**Implication:** AI is neither a villain nor a savior in the story of who you become — it is the latest context in which you choose alignment or drift. Naming this pattern prevents the rationalization before it takes hold.

**Jeremy is explicit about his faith as a non-negotiable personal value and uses it as a test case for AI alignment.** He asks not whether AI is making him a more efficient worker, but whether it is helping him become a more wholehearted disciple — real, daily, and relational rather than performative or theoretical. ([source](Are You More of What You Value? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"I'm a Christian. I want to be a wholehearted disciple of Jesus Christ. Not performative. Not theoretical. Real. In daily life. In relationships. In how I spend my attention. And I have to ask myself: is AI helping me become more of that man, or just a more efficient worker?"*

**Implication:** The test of AI's value is not productivity — it is formation. Whatever your deepest commitments are, the right question is whether AI is helping you grow into them or simply accelerating a version of yourself that is already misaligned.

**Jeremy's turnaround at BCG came not from increased intelligence or longer hours, but from adopting a single behavioral rule: always volunteer for unclear follow-ups. His father's advice — 'always leave with the monkey on your back' — reframed initiative as a systematic practice rather than a personality trait. Within three months, he moved from worst performer to top quartile.** ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Every meeting ends with tasks, next steps, follow-ups. When it's unclear who's doing what, you volunteer. Always. Never let anybody else leave with the monkey on their back."*

**Implication:** Career acceleration is often less about raw talent and more about consistent, visible initiative. Deliberately volunteering for unglamorous work builds trust and reputation faster than performing well on assigned tasks alone.

**The most powerful prompt is not the one typed into an AI tool — it is the internal prompt a person gives themselves: 'What needs doing that nobody's asking for?' This reframes the concept of prompting from a technical AI skill to a fundamental posture of proactive leadership.** ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"The most powerful prompt isn't the one you type into ChatGPT. It's the one you give yourself: What needs doing that nobody's asking for?"*

**Implication:** Developing the habit of self-prompting — regularly asking what unseen problems deserve attention — is a metacognitive practice that compounds over a career and cannot be outsourced to any tool.

**One of the most significant inhibitors to creative efficacy is simply the lack of time — people feel they are 'victims of the calendar.' Utley argues this is a choice, not a circumstance. High-output creative individuals actively reclaim time by designing their schedules around thinking, not just doing.** ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck))

> *"We're victims of the calendar. But what creative people do is wield their calendar as a weapon."*

**Implication:** The calendar is the most underrated creative tool available to any knowledge worker. Reclaiming even one recurring block per week for unstructured thinking can meaningfully shift creative output over time.

**The most creative people are far more comfortable leaving decisions unmade and sitting with ambiguity far longer than their less creative peers. This 'delay of closure' is a deliberate creative strategy, not indecision.** ([source](youtube:the-habit-that-built-every-genius-jeremy-utley))

> *"The most creative architects weren't any smarter than their peers, but there was something else that distinguished them. They were far more comfortable leaving decisions unmade. They delayed closure. They sat with ambiguity far longer than their less creative colleagues did."*

**Implication:** Tolerance for ambiguity is a trainable creative asset. Rushing to resolution forecloses the subconscious processing that produces breakthrough insights.

**Utley frames his role not as an expert dispensing answers but as 'a front row student in the world's coolest classroom.' This learning posture — even after 14+ years teaching at Stanford — reflects a belief that creative mastery is a continuous becoming, not a fixed state to be achieved.** ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders))

> *"He considers himself to be a 'front row student in the world's coolest classroom,' and shines a spotlight on the ways every single one of us can emulate creative masters and grow in our own journey of becoming."*

**Implication:** Leaders who model intellectual humility and genuine curiosity create the psychological safety that teams need to take creative risks — positioning yourself as a perpetual learner is itself a leadership practice.

**Jeremy's career path was not planned — it emerged through exposure to unexpected environments.** A chance comment from a colleague in India ('you're kind of d-schooley') redirected him from management consulting toward design thinking and teaching. His entire career pivot happened because he kept poking his head into rooms that weren't his job. ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

> *"at some point, one of them said to me, you know, you're kind of de Schooley. And I took that as an insult... he goes, No, no, no, that's a that's a good thing."*

**Implication:** Career breakthroughs often come from following curiosity into adjacent spaces rather than executing a pre-set plan. Being open to informal signals and unexpected communities can be more valuable than rigid career roadmaps.

**Jeremy spent 13+ years at the d.school and describes himself as 'just as invigorated' as when he started.** This sustained engagement suggests that working at the intersection of creativity, teaching, and real-world problem solving creates durable intrinsic motivation — the kind that outlasts novelty. ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

> *"13 years later, I'm just as invigorated by it, just as excited about it as I was then."*

**Implication:** When you find work that combines genuine curiosity, human impact, and continuous learning, engagement doesn't decay over time. For leaders building teams, creating those conditions matters more than extrinsic incentives.

**Creativity is best defined as doing more than the first thing you think of.** This framing captures the essential cognitive discipline of pushing past the satisficing instinct that causes most people to stop at 'good enough.' ([source](youtube:wv779vmyPVY))

> *"Creativity is doing more than the first thing you think of. It speaks to a profound cognitive bias that we hold. It's been called functional fixedness. It's been called the Einstellung effect. But the basic premise is humans tend to fixate on an early solution and be satisfied."*

**Implication:** This definition makes creativity actionable — it reframes the creative act not as inspiration but as the deliberate refusal to settle, which is a trainable behavior.

**Education and professional training heavily emphasize critical thinking but almost completely neglect non-critical thinking — the generative, associative mode of thought. This imbalance is a systemic gap that leaves most people trained to evaluate ideas but not to generate them.** ([source](youtube:bloom-mood-podcast-jeremy-utley))

> *"When I was at Stanford I got an entire semester's worth of instruction on critical thinking. Did I get any instruction on non-critical thinking? Any? And that's something that we can cultivate."*

**Implication:** Individuals and organizations should deliberately invest in building generative thinking skills to compensate for the overwhelming institutional emphasis on critical evaluation.

---

## Experimentation & Prototyping

**Most organizations have a doing gap, not just a thinking gap.** The instinct to plan more, analyze more, and align more before acting is precisely the instinct that prevents learning. Utley argues that the companies being disrupted are not being out-thought — they are being out-experimented by competitors who are willing to act with incomplete information. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The doing gap will kill more companies than AI."*

**Implication:** The competitive advantage in most industries right now is not better analysis — it is faster iteration cycles. If your organization is famous for its planning process, ask yourself when it last ran a real experiment that could have failed. That answer reveals the actual innovation health of the enterprise.

**The doing gap is the distance between knowing what to do and actually doing it.** Utley observes that most teams are not suffering from an information deficit — they have read the articles, attended the workshops, and understand the frameworks. The bottleneck is translation into action. This gap grows wider and more dangerous the faster the external environment moves. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Leaders should diagnose whether their team has a knowing problem or a doing problem. If people can articulate the right approach but aren't executing it, no amount of additional learning or planning will fix the issue. The prescription is forced experimentation: assign an action with a deadline, not another discovery session.

**AI has dramatically lowered the cost of prototyping and therefore dramatically raised the cost of not prototyping.** When generating a first version of almost any artifact — a pitch, a product concept, a process design, a curriculum — now takes minutes rather than days, the excuse of 'we don't have the bandwidth to test this yet' has evaporated. The organizations not running continuous experiments with AI tools are leaving a structural learning advantage on the table. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Recalibrate your team's threshold for what constitutes a 'real' prototype. If you can generate a functional first version of something in 20 minutes with AI, then running five experiments instead of one is not a resource question — it is a decision question. Change the decision, not the budget.

**Utley frames the imagination ceiling as the primary constraint on AI-driven experimentation.** Most people can generate a prompt for a task they already know how to do — they struggle to imagine experiments that would genuinely challenge their assumptions or surface information they don't already have. The limit is not AI capability; it is the user's ability to conceive of what to test. ([source](The Imagination Ceiling: Why 70% of Your People Are Stuck on AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"The imagination ceiling: why 70% of your people are stuck on AI."*

**Implication:** Training teams on AI prompting is necessary but insufficient. The upstream skill is experimental imagination — the ability to ask 'What don't I know that I should know?' before reaching for the tool. Invest in question-design practice as the precursor to effective AI experimentation.

**Utley argues that boards and senior leadership teams are structurally ill-equipped to imagine the future because they have no experience building in it. They can evaluate existing businesses with rigor but cannot prototype new possibilities because prototyping requires tolerance for ambiguity and the willingness to be wrong in public — two behaviors that are systematically punished at the senior level.** ([source](Your Board Can't Imagine the Future — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Your board can't imagine the future."*

**Implication:** Organizations that want genuine strategic imagination at the leadership level need to create protected spaces for senior leaders to experiment without consequence — scenarios, simulations, working prototypes they can hold and interact with. Abstract strategic discussion cannot substitute for firsthand experience with new possibilities.

**Working with AI as a creative partner — rather than just using it as a production tool — requires a disposition toward experimentation. You have to be willing to run variations, follow unexpected tangents, and treat the outputs as prompts for your own thinking rather than final answers. This experimental stance is a learned habit, not a natural response to a new tool.** ([source](Don't Use AI. Work With It. — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Don't use AI. Work with it."*

**Implication:** Teams that approach AI with a 'give me the answer' posture will get less from it than teams that approach it with 'let's see what this surfaces.' Training people to treat AI interactions as experimental loops — hypothesis, response, reflection, next hypothesis — unlocks a class of insight that transactional use never reaches.

**Utley observes that real augmentation — the kind that produces new human capability rather than just faster output — always looks like a series of experiments. The people who have genuinely extended their cognitive range with AI did not start with a workflow; they started with curiosity and ran many small tests, most of which didn't pan out. The augmentation is a downstream product of sustained, low-stakes experimentation.** ([source](This Is What Real Augmentation Looks Like — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Stop trying to implement AI through top-down workflow redesign and instead create conditions for bottom-up experimentation. Give people protected time to try things without accountability for immediate ROI. The organizational experiments that produce no visible output often build the tacit knowledge base from which real capability eventually emerges.

**Utley identifies a specific cognitive trap.** people treat their first interaction with an AI tool as a real test of what the tool can do. One disappointing output and they conclude the tool is not useful for their work. This is the opposite of experimental thinking — it is a single-trial conclusion that forecloses learning. Good experimenters treat every output as a data point, not a verdict. ([source](Have You Tried AI? — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Have you tried AI?"*

**Implication:** When introducing teams to new AI tools, explicitly brief against the single-trial fallacy. Frame the first uses as calibration experiments, not performance evaluations. The expected outcome of first use is not 'this worked' but 'now I understand the starting point.' That reframe prevents premature dismissal of tools that require iteration to unlock.

**Utley's concept of 'wherewithal moments' — instances where someone finds a way to act with available resources rather than waiting for perfect conditions — is a form of experimental courage. The person who builds a rough version with what they have is running an experiment; the person waiting for the right budget, tools, or team is not. Wherewithal is the disposition that converts ideation into learning.** ([source](I Have a Team (A Collection of Wherewithal Moments) — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** Create organizational recognition for acts of wherewithal: the team member who built the rough prototype over a weekend, the manager who ran the pilot with three customers before requesting resources. Celebrating resourceful action more than careful preparation sends the signal that matters for an experimentation culture.

**Utley notes that idle AI agents represent a specific form of organizational waste that most leaders haven't learned to feel yet. When an AI system capable of running experiments sits unused because no one has designed tasks for it, the organization is forfeiting learning at near-zero marginal cost. The pain of idle agents — like the pain of idle factory capacity — should register as a strategic signal.** ([source](Do You Feel The Pain Of Idle Agents? — Methods of the Masters blog))

> *"Do you feel the pain of idle agents?"*

**Implication:** Build into your AI governance practice a regular question: 'What experiments could our AI systems be running right now that they aren't?' The ceiling on AI value creation is not capability — it is the human imagination applied to designing experiments for capable systems. Expand that imagination and the value follows.

**Being the prompt — rather than just writing prompts — means embodying an experimental posture toward your own work and thinking. Utley uses this phrase to capture the shift from using AI transactionally to living inside an iterative, generative cycle. The person who 'is the prompt' treats every output — from any source — as a starting point for another experiment rather than an endpoint.** ([source](Be the Prompt — Jeremy's Newsletter (Substack)))

> *"Be the prompt."*

**Implication:** The highest-leverage thing an individual contributor or leader can do with AI is to develop an experimental identity around it — the kind of person who never accepts a first draft as final, who always runs a variation, who treats every interesting output as a question rather than an answer. That identity, once formed, produces compounding creative returns.

**The doing gap — the distance between knowing what to do and actually doing it — is itself a framing problem.** Most people frame action as requiring certainty, which is why they never start. Reframing the first action as an experiment rather than a commitment collapses the doing gap by removing the psychological stakes attached to being wrong. ([source](The Doing Gap Will Kill More Companies Than AI — Methods of the Masters blog))

**Implication:** When a team is stuck in analysis paralysis, reframe the next action explicitly as a question rather than a commitment: 'Let's run a two-week experiment to test whether this framing is right.' The experiment frame makes starting safe.

**Prototypes are questions made physical.** The moment an idea becomes a prototype, it stops being a belief and starts generating information. Organizations that prototype early and often learn faster than organizations that think carefully and act slowly — a bad prototype beats a good debate every time. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders podcast))

**Implication:** Shorten the distance between question and prototype. The goal of a prototype is not to demonstrate an answer — it is to provoke a better question. Build to learn, not to prove.

**Design thinking's prototyping discipline is not primarily about testing products — it is about testing assumptions.** Every prototype encodes a set of beliefs about what users need, how they behave, and what they value. Running the prototype exposes those beliefs to reality. The discipline forces you to articulate what you believe before you test it, which itself is a clarifying act. ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** Before building any prototype, write down the two or three beliefs it is testing. This makes the experiment legible and makes its results actionable. Teams that skip this step build prototypes that generate ambiguous data — and then argue about what the results mean instead of learning from them.

**Speed of experimentation is itself a form of competitive advantage that compounds.** The team running ten experiments per quarter is not just ten times smarter than the team running one — it is developing an institutional learning muscle that gets faster and better with each cycle. Iteration velocity is a capability that, once built, widens rather than closes over time. ([source](Jeremy Utley on Why Mastering Ideaflow Elevates Everything Else — Passion Struck podcast))

**Implication:** Don't evaluate your experimentation rate only in terms of what each individual experiment returns. Ask what iteration velocity you are building as an organizational capability. The compounding returns of a fast-cycling team show up slowly and then suddenly — usually when it's too late for slower competitors to catch up.

**One of the core disciplines at the Stanford d.school is bias toward action — an institutionalized preference for doing something over discussing something. This is not anti-intellectual; it is a recognition that the information generated by action is categorically different from the information generated by analysis. The d.school treats making as a form of thinking, not a phase that follows thinking.** ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Build action checkpoints into every creative or strategic meeting. Before anyone leaves a room, someone should be able to answer: 'What will exist by the next meeting that didn't exist before today?' If the answer is only 'a better shared understanding,' the meeting was discussion, not innovation work.

**The concept of 'failing fast' is frequently misunderstood.** It is not a celebration of failure — it is a prescription for keeping the cost of being wrong as low as possible. The goal is to reach the point of falsification at minimum investment. Utley's framing is that you want to kill bad ideas cheaply and early, not that failure itself is valuable. ([source](From Bonehead to Brilliant: Innovation through Ideaflow — TD.org Accidental Trainer podcast))

**Implication:** Reframe 'fail fast' for your team as 'kill bad ideas cheaply.' This shifts the focus from a cultural posture about failure to a practical discipline about cost management. The question before every experiment becomes: 'What is the cheapest and fastest way to find out if this idea is wrong?' That question produces better experimental design.

**The habit of daily idea generation is itself a form of low-stakes experimentation.** Every idea quota — Utley's prescription of ten ideas per day on a chosen problem — produces mostly ideas that will never be used. But the practice builds the experimental habit: generate, evaluate, discard, repeat. The discipline of generating without judgment trains the same cognitive muscle that effective prototypers use. ([source](Ideaflow: Achieving Breakthrough Creativity and Ideas — We Are Human Leaders podcast))

**Implication:** Frame daily ideation practice to your team not as a creativity exercise but as an experimentation habit. The person who generates ten ideas a day and discards nine of them is practicing the same skill as the team that runs ten prototypes and kills nine. The muscle is the same — only the artifact changes.

**One of the structural lessons from the Stanford d.school's teaching methodology is that students learn design thinking by doing it, not by studying it. The curriculum is built around rapid project cycles — short-duration experiments that force students to prototype, test, get feedback, and iterate within hours or days, not weeks. This pedagogical architecture is itself a prototype of what organizational learning should look like.** ([source](AI and Design Thinking with Stanford's Jeremy Utley — Bloom Mood Podcast))

**Implication:** If your organization wants an innovation culture, model the learning architecture of the d.school: short cycles, real artifacts, real users, fast feedback. The lecture-then-do sequence that most training follows is the wrong order. The doing is the learning, and the reflection afterward is what converts experience into transferable insight.

**The d.school mantra 'show, don't tell' is an experimentation principle dressed as a communication principle.** When you show someone a prototype, you are running an experiment — you are watching what they notice, what confuses them, and what questions they ask. The information content of a three-minute prototype interaction exceeds the information content of a thirty-minute presentation about the same idea. ([source](Jeremy Utley: Co-Author of Ideaflow & Director of Stanford's d.school — Kara Goldin Show))

**Implication:** Replace pitch decks with prototypes wherever possible. Even a paper mock-up, a role-play scenario, or a rough simulation gives observers something to react to that generates richer feedback than slides. The goal is not to impress — it is to surface the reactions, questions, and objections that only emerge from interaction with a real artifact.

**A prototype is not a polished product — it is a question made physical.** The moment an idea leaves your head and takes any tangible form, it stops being a belief and starts generating real information. Organizations that prototype early and cheaply learn faster than organizations that think carefully and act slowly. A bad prototype beats a good debate every single time. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

> *"A bad prototype beats a good debate."*

**Implication:** Stop treating prototyping as a late-stage polish step. Make something — anything — as fast as possible, because the prototype will tell you things your best thinking never will. The cost of not prototyping is hidden in all the meetings you're having instead.

**Ideas that remain inside your head are not assets — they are opinions.** The act of externalizing an idea, even crudely, transforms it from a private assumption into a testable artifact. This is why prototyping is fundamentally an epistemological move: you are not building a product, you are building a mechanism to generate knowledge you could not otherwise access. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Treat every prototype as a learning instrument, not a commitment. The question to ask before building is not 'Is this good enough?' but 'What will this teach us?' That reframe makes cheap, fast prototyping feel rational rather than reckless.

**The real enemy of experimentation is not risk tolerance — it is the organizational instinct to make experiments look like commitments before they are. When teams feel that a prototype will be interpreted as a promise, they over-invest in polish and under-invest in learning. Utley's prescription is to deliberately design experiments to look and feel provisional, so that failure is legible as data rather than defeat.** ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Leaders must actively signal that early experiments are not commitments. The cultural message 'we are testing this' has to be explicit and protected, or the incentive system will force premature investment in ideas that should be cheap to kill. Name the experiment as an experiment out loud.

**Utley draws a sharp distinction between testing an idea and loving an idea.** The emotional investment people make in their ideas before those ideas are tested is the primary reason organizations prototype too late and too expensively. If you have already told three executives about your idea, you are no longer testing it — you are defending it. Prototyping must happen before the idea acquires social weight. ([source](Ideaflow: The Only Business Metric That Matters — Perry Klebahn & Jeremy Utley))

**Implication:** Create a norm of testing before socializing. If your first instinct after generating an idea is to share it with decision-makers for buy-in, flip that sequence.
